Chapter 1 - A Fishy Tale
      Detective Paige Burner sighed and pushed open the door that led into the Laguna Niguel Ritz-Carlton's suite seven. She was immediately struck by the pungent odor of decaying seafood.
      "Bleh," she said, grimacing in mock disgust. The truth was, Paige can and has eaten sandwiches next to mutilated bodies writhing with maggots, where the victim fully lost control of their bowels before or after death. Simpler smells, like pooled and drying blood, were almost comforting.
      The uniformed officer in charge of the crime scene, Clement Blankenship, greeted her at the door.
      Blankenship was one of those wannabes who would never in a million years graduate to detective status. She held a general disdain for long-term uniformed officers in the abstract, and for Blankenship in the specific.
      Paige felt that uniformed officers were fake cops. Street janitors. If you didn't aspire to make detective, in her mind, you were lazy, ignorant, or corrupt.
      Uniformed officers didn't solve crimes. And more often than not, they committed them. Low-paid, uneducated, if they didn't steal dope money, they were sadists that took joy in human misery and beating suspects.
      "Ah, Detective Burner! So good to see you," Blankenship lied. He was an ugly little man, all balding head and decaying buckteeth. "What we have here is..." His eyes began to wander toward her chest. In another moment, he'd begin stammering.
      Paige cut him off. It was best to not let him get worked up in the first place. "Has the scene been disturbed?" she inquired.
      "No one has been into the bathroom, the actual site of the altercation."
      She winced internally at his misuse of terminology. Buffoons like Blankenship tried to use stilted speech, and often failed spectacularly at it. The department was trying to breed it out of them, because it hurt them in the courtroom when they testified.
      "But," he said, "About ten P.C. have been in here, and about four of us uniformed officers. The victim is, uh, in the state of being nude. Disrobed."

      "Plainclothesmen?" she asked. She knew what he meant, but felt like subtly chiding him.
      "Affirmative. Detective Gautier, Detective Charles, Detect..." Paige cut him off again. She was in no mood for repetitive brayings from a jackass.
"Thanks, Clement. That'll be all. I'll take it from here." "Are you sure? I could..."
"Quite sure, Clem. Thanks."
      Duly chastised, Blankenship had already slunk away, leaving behind only the faint, forgettable aura of a second-rate officer.
      Paige was called in for the oddball murder scenes, the cases that had officers with twice her years on the force scratching their heads at the onset. This was undoubtedly going to be a doozy.
      Now she could relax and work in peace and silence. She set her Rubbermaid bag down and donned her forensics gear: surgical scrubs, hairnet, O.R. mask, and gloves. Few were as meticulous as she.
      Humming "Ode to Joy", she turned the corner that led to the over- sized bathroom. Such opulent surroundings were rarely the setting for murders. Splayed awkwardly across the cold tile floor, under bright lights, was the corpse of an attractive thirty-something year old woman. No wonder they took so long to call her, she thought. Her body was perfect.
      She raised her Nikon and began snapping photos before ever entering the bathroom. Considering it, she removed her shoes to enter in her stocking feet. This was one aspect of detective work she could never decide on. Outside shoes could bring in contaminants: dirt, dog shit, etcetera. But shoe covers could also remove evidence. When working alone, she usually worked barefooted or in pantyhose. She liked working alone.
      The victim, a Ms. Maureen Bedford, lay on her back with her head facing the Jacuzzi tub. Her blond ringlets were matted, soaking in a puddle of blood that had already congealed to the consistency of thick Karo syrup. No flies in the Ritz at least, Paige mused. They would come, she knew. They always did.
      Having read a scant dossier on Ms. Bedford, now Maureen to her, she knew she was unique, even for this upper-upper class coastal neighborhood. You don't remain an unmarried woman here often, except in a few cases: gay, painfully ugly, or extremely eccentric. And Maureen was quite a catch.

      Her file didn't contain info on her family, but Paige knew the name. Descendants of a real estate mogul, the Bedford children each had a considerable nest egg. The four were probably sitting on twenty million or so apiece.
      Jealousy, money and revenge being the primary motives for murder in Paige's mind, net wealth and the bottom line had to be considered. And money was hard to forget in Laguna Niguel, where this branch of the Ritz- Carlton made other Ritz-Carltons look like Holiday Inns.
      She loved the view from this hotel. Looking to the west, you saw the endless expanse of the Pacific, but also the cove that the Ritz inhabited. At nine a.m., it was already populated with nannies pushing strollers, gold- diggers in French-cut bikinis, and older men in Speedos and gold chains, trolling for either of the other two.
      Opposite the slight inlet the hotel occupied, there was a private residence nearly the size of the hotel, and twice as ostentatious, all shining glass and metal. It was probably a forty million dollar home, minimum.
      One of Paige's first assignments as a uniformed officer was to escort a bewildered pair of married servants from that property. The couple, good- hearted but naive domestics from the south, learned the hard way about what happens if you stand up for yourselves against the super-rich.
      They had apparently angered their boss. Their owner, really, Paige thought. And thus found themselves summarily evicted from the house on a Sunday morning, without warning.
      It was hard for her to do, back then, because she understood the situation. Still, legally, the property owners could throw anyone out, live-in servants or not. But she didn't enjoy it, and such interactions discolored her view of the elite.
      Returning from her brief daydream, she gingerly padded into the room to gather evidence. She often didn't like to receive the analysis of others beforehand, when working a scene. She didn't want their preconceived notions to shape her view of events.
      She snapped shots of the gorgeous-in-repose female body on the floor, including several close-ups. You never really knew what clues the photos might hold later, when the actual body had been vivisectioned, stuffed and mounted, or cremated. And she knew, as hot as this chick was, she'd probably be mounted and stuffed several times on the coroner's table.

Necrophilia was a privilege afforded that morbid profession. Everyone on the force knew it, but few discussed it.
      So, it was best to get all of your evidence on the first pass. Paige didn't usually leave a scene before she felt she'd solved the case, anyway.
      She had graduated top of her class from the academy. This was no small task. She had worked her fingers to the bone, mentally and physically. She had endured hardships and trauma, repeated hazings, and even out-right threats.
      In the end, she was their superior, and she never quite forgave or forgot the type of cretins who'd impeded her. She carried her dedication to excellence with her throughout her career.
      While taking artful photos of the reclining nude, she noticed what had sent the other detectives running, and was also the source of the dead fish smell. A large Maine lobster, blue-green, lay on its back between the toilet and wastebasket, its claws open, tail extended. It was a big one, and her mouth watered slightly.
      Rock lobster, B-52s aside, was for suckers. Maine lobster was the eatin' kind. Boil for nine to twelve minutes, serve with real butter and ground garlic, a hint of sea salt and a fine wine. Yum.
      Paige shuddered and reminded herself to let someone not entirely distasteful take her out to dinner soon. She wanted lobster and to get laid, in that order. Forced to choose, she'd settle for the lobster and an enjoyable conversation.
      The future cadaver/necrophilia-subject-to-be mostly attended to, she redirected her focus on the ill-fated decapod, which belonged either in the North Atlantic, or in someone's stomach. Paige had once wanted to become an oceanographer or marine biologist, until 'Jaws' came out when she was six. Thanks, Mr. Spielberg.
      It was too bad she never got to utilize her marine biology knowledge, she thought. After snapping an abundance of photos of this dinner that got away, she produced an evidence bag, labeled it, and placed the lobster inside. She put the bag on the countertop, with the lobster lying on its back. For fun, she examined it with a biologist's eye. It was an adult American lobster, about three pounds or so, female. Examining the swimmerets on the abdomen, she noted brine shrimp eggs and a few dead hatchlings.

      This was symbiosis in action. The brine shrimp, sea monkeys to laymen, often spawned there, giving their eggs a fighting chance at survival. Unless the lobster was harvested. And this one was fresh, no more than two days from the sea. Three, max. Otherwise the shrimp would have all hatched by now.
      Now donning her detective's hat, she performed forensics on the lobster. The cephalothorax, or main shell, wasn't cracked. It was too supple before boiling to crack, but it was bruised, indicating it may have been thrown or dropped. The facial region: eyes, brain, mouth and maxillipeds, was covered with ash. Interesting. Paige had no doubt that under the ash was a mass of burns.
      She turned her attention to the ashtray on the counter, ignored until now. She counted eight butts, two with lipstick. Using her tweezers, she examined each in turn, after taking a few photos of their initial arrangement. She bagged them together as she examined them.
      After she removed five of them, she found a roach buried in the ash. Unsurprising. This was California. Everyone, even a lot of cops, smoked pot. Paige certainly did, before work, after work. Occasionally at lunch. It made her a better detective, she felt, and relieved stress, helping her to sleep at night.
      She brought it to her nose and inhaled, trying to place the strain. It smelled like Purple Haze, a potent, smooth-smoking hybrid favored by smokers with money. The hard weed-heads smoked B.C. bud from Vancouver, or whites, rhino and widow. But there was a base note to the scent she didn't immediately recognize, a faint candy smell reminiscent of opium. On a whim, she left the bathroom and began to search the suite.
      Within a few minutes, she had her answer. She found most of an eighth of Haze. She knew her weed. In the baggie was a piece of foil. Inside of that were three cubes of rock cocaine. This was a package deal some sellers offered. For a hundred dollars, you got a sack of high-grade hydro and five stones.
      So, considering the lack of a pipe, the pack of Job Crystal papers, the roach, and the smell emanating from it, Paige concluded that the joint was a primo, marijuana laced with crack. And a good primo could burn for nearly ten minutes, she noted. Neither here nor there, but a good investigator considered everything, significant or not.

      She returned to the lavatory, pocketing the herb sack. No need to shame the victim after death. The coroner's toxicology report would show traces of THC and cocaine anyway, but that wouldn't be widely known or reported.
      Paige refocused on the wounds. Wound, in this case. Obviously, there was no gunshot involved, or stab wound. Blunt force trauma seemed most likely, but she knew poisoning or overdose could have had the same effect.
      The victim, she surmised, was most likely struck in the head, assuming murder was the case. Barring that, one possibility remained. But the other half of the murder dichotomy was often not considered by many murder investigators: It might not have been a murder at all.
      True, a long shot could mean that something akin to poisoning or overdose could have resulted in Lady Maureen falling over and striking her head on the floor. But what if that wasn't the cause? Yes, you could fall out from smoking crack, or even have a heart attack, but it wasn't very common in small amounts such as this. Maureen was a "chipper", an amateur. A light, weekend smoker.
      The autopsy would reveal more, but Paige wanted to know, now. She still had time to get herself a lobster for lunch, if she wrapped this up. She went back into the suite and called the front desk. "This is room seven. Do we have lobster on the menu?"
"Yes, ma'am. Shall I send up another one?"
      Paige paused. "No, thanks. I'll come down for lunch. Please have the chef prepare a two-pound lobster, with a Catalina chef's salad and a glass of Zinfandel."
"Certainly, madam."
The staff was polite to a fault.

      So the front desk clerks still thought the Bedford woman was alive. In California, catering to the wealthy depended heavily upon discretion. Apparently the maid who found her was not the gossipy type.
      Paige shed her forensics gear near the door to the hallway, so as to drop as few foreign hairs as possible, revealing a lithe body clad in tasteful business attire. In the hall, she shook her head, letting her own shockingly red tresses cascade down her neck and shoulders. Finally, she donned her bookish, vaguely horn-rimmed glasses and moved on.

      The glasses were just that: glass. She didn't need glasses to see, having twenty-thirty vision, but glasses were sexier. They implied intelligence, somehow, and she felt intelligence was sexy.
      Not that she had trouble attracting men. It was all about attracting the right sort of man, when you got down to it.
The ma�tre d' practically tripped over his Gucci loafers to seat her. "Hi," she said, "I phoned ahead for lunch a few minutes ago. Room
seven?"
      "Certainly, madam. Right this way. Your meal shall be out shortly." He pulled out her chair, seated her, and silently padded away.
      Thank god he didn't say something awkward about a beautiful lady such as herself eating alone, Paige thought. She sometimes considered wearing a fake wedding ring, but had always decided, why limit herself? It's not like she wouldn't marry, much less date, a ma�tre d', if he was "Mr. Right". Or even a Mr. "Right Now".
      In less than a minute, a small army of Chicanos and out-of-work actors set upon her table like reverse locusts. At the two-minute mark, she found herself alone again, with her salad, which was sumptuous, her wine, aromatic, and three steaming, fresh-from-the-oven croissants.
      The butter was of course, real butter and she had to have at least one croissant, so she dug in with ladylike grace combined with mannish hunger. Already, this was more food than she should eat in one sitting. Not one to fill up on bread, she had the salad and lobster to consider. She worked out too hard and often to let her stomach get distended. Better to eat smaller meals more often than to develop a pooch.
      She spiked a cherry tomato and savored it. Organic, she knew. Most high-end food in California was, which was one of the reasons she settled here. It was French dressing, she noted, not Catalina. Most Cali chefs considered Catalina dressing to be French dressing for white trash, suitable for picnics and patrons of dining establishments such as Ryan's, and therefore wouldn't allow it to be served with their food, period. Chefs were fussy that way.
      Oh well, she mused, I'm not paying for it. So she ate and drank, and thought deeply. Paige knew instinctively that she was missing something and needed to reconnoiter her thoughts.
      She idly fingered the sack of irie in her pocket, but decided against it. As a rule, that was her reward, not her crutch. It was her breakfast, along

with coffee, and her prize at the end of a hard day's work. But sometimes she slipped and took a few tokes at mid-day to clear her head and think about a case in a different way.
      Like clockwork, the well-oiled restaurant machinery arrived with a bright-orange steaming lobster as she reached the three-quarters point of her salad. Timing was everything.
      Now she was glad she had stopped with one roll, as delicious as it was. The lobster was big, probably a full pound bigger than she had specified. T.S Elliot, she thought, I'm eating it all.
      She began with the claws, cracking them with the nutcracker provided. That was her nickname at the police academy, of course. She was called nutcracker, for more than one reason. In hand-to-hand training, often against officers much bigger than herself, she wasn't averse to hitting below the belt. Or kicking. Or biting, for that matter.
      The garlic butter, combined with the freshest lobster you could get short of cooking it on a boat, actually made her a little wet. It was culinary ecstasy.
      She ate some of the top half of her baked potato, but had no intention of eating it all. She got her potassium from a chemical supply house in Pomona, of all places. She had slathered it in cheese, butter and bacon from the crystal dishes provided with more than a little guilt. I'll work out on Sunday too, she rationalized.
      The rest of her meal was a near feral experience. She all but grunted as she ate the second claw and started in on the tail. This, she decided, was better than sex. Slightly. Better than bad sex, anyway.
      For a moment, she thought lovingly of her Hello Kitty vibrator waiting patiently in her underwear drawer at home, and then resumed her feast. She tried to slow down, but she had a serious case of the munchies, and was starting to realize what she had overlooked.
      A few minutes later, having eaten all of the lobster, Paige stood, straightened her skirt and went back to the day's case, after a brief trip to the hotel restaurant's ladies room.
      In the restroom, she had a revelation. She could barely wait to finish peeing before rushing to her vehicle for more forensics gear. All she needed was her black light, but she also grabbed a few sterile airtight containers.
      Paige took the stairs to the first floor instead of waiting for the elevator, and had to discipline herself when she got there to wear her clean

room attire.
      Properly dressed and a little calmer, she reentered the bathroom and crouched by the toilet. It was difficult to discern, but she thought she had the missing piece of the puzzle.
      When she turned off the fluorescents and turned on the black light, she knew she had solved it. She dialed the office.
"Chief, you need to come down here. I figured it out."
      Paige had it knocked out, but the last step had to be done before the chief got to the hotel, for decorum's sake. She grabbed a spoon from the minibar. There was no real need to sterilize it, but she heated it under the tap for two minutes, and dried it on a clean hand towel. She had minimized the contaminants, at least.
      High profile case that it was, or would be, she knew he would be here soon, so she had to act fast. She returned to the bathroom and took a picture of the late Ms. Bedford so she could reposition her later.
      Carefully, she spread the dead girl's legs. She then lay down on the floor and put her face close to her crotch.
      Spreading the labia minora with her gloved left hand, Paige spied her prize, this time visible to the naked eye. She used the spoon deftly.
      "Just hold still, sweetheart. You won't feel a thing. I even warmed it up for you."
      She bagged and tagged her last piece of evidence, then smoothed the deceased's vaginal lips back into place, and reclosed her legs.
      Just to be sure, she checked the viewfinder of her camera, and made a few adjustments. Satisfied, she sat on the bed and waited.
      She could have left the body in its new position, but Paige felt the woman deserved a little decency in death. She thought about it some more, and then got up and covered her with a crisp linen sheet from the hall closet. Now she could relax.
      Ten minutes later, the chief was banging the door down. He was a bit high-strung.
"Who is it?" she teased.
       "Open the goddamn door, Burner. This is a bunch of goddamn bullshit!"
Paige smirked and then opened the door. "Calm down, boss. She's not going anywhere." The chief, Terry to her, exhaled sharply.

      "Her family is hot! This is a big deal with them, personally and financially. I don't know the specifics, but for someone found dead at 7:00 A.M., there's been a ton of pressure applied to the department. What have you come up with?"

      In the morning, she got up to use the bathroom once again. I think she saw something frightening, fainted, hit her head, and never woke up. She passed out from the concussion and bled to death."

Chapter 2 - Just a Good Old Boy
      Jean Simons was a good old boy. Insofar as it was possible to be a good old boy Yankee genetic researcher in Alabama.
      And it was mostly possible, except for the Yankee part. Alabama actually has a rich, tech-heavy tradition due to the presence of NASA. NASA led to the formation of companies like Intergraph, maker of high-end graphic workstations. Being an outsider, though, was always a sticking point.
      He was well regarded at the university, at least. Being a PhD candidate, he had a lot of pull. College tends to mostly dissolve regional differences after a while, anyway.
      Still, a certain amount of hazing and teasing was inevitable. Such was the fraternity of men, regardless of their origins.
      "Damn, Jean," his research partner and roommate James was fond of saying, "If you had another 'm' in your name, you'd be a rock star."
      This was James's standard Kiss joke, which he mainly pulled out to impress some hot, impressionable sophomore girl.
      "If you had a fucking dick, you'd be a man." Jean had once replied, which had pretty much shut that down. "Your name is fucking James Brown."
      It didn't hurt that this little exchange had occurred at the student union cafeteria, in front of a group of their peers. After that, the two were able to put qualms aside and focus on academics. And the name of the game was genetic research.
      It was an exciting field, pretty much wide-open for the motivated researcher out to make a name for himself. And Jean and James were both pretty driven, if by differing motivations.
      James, "Jimbrowski" to Jean, was determined to stamp out Multiple Sclerosis in his lifetime. His mild obsession began back in elementary school with the M.S. Read-A-Thons. He had read his young ass off, trying to save kids his own age from becoming wheelchair-bound or paralyzed.
Jean had no such altruistic streak.
      James never caught on that "Jimbrowski" meant "dick" in the northeast. Northerners, even the nerds, were generally crueler than all but the most psychotic southerners. It was simple genetics and environment. The scarcity and harsh environs of the northern climate produced more hardened individuals by default.

      On some level, academically, James had to know this, but never really applied the knowledge. Jean used it often. He was full of biological theories of his own, and he didn't hesitate in applying them to his advantage, particularly when it came to women.
      For instance, he ignored what he considered nature's false beauty cues. Blonde hair is highly favored in western society because it correlates to an ability to reproduce, on a subconscious level. The attributes men generally find desirable in women almost all relate to viability of reproduction. And Jean had no desire to reproduce.
      This afforded him a certain selectivity, and allowed him to alter the dating dynamic, to a degree. Jean called it broken wing syndrome. He had arranged a number of biological facts into a formula he applied with much success.
      Women, in part, are biologically driven by a need for attention. They can also be quite competitive among themselves.
      First of all, the more attractive you are, the harder he would ignore you. The really gorgeous women became practically invisible. If he did get forced to talk to a woman, he still wouldn't express the slightest bit of interest. Interested females would then escalate their own actions and attentions, making it clear to Jean that they were interested and receptive.
      He likened it to a bird pretending to have a broken wing. Women are also programmed to respond to authority. Even if you are the captain of the chess team, there are women who respond favorably to that simple position of perceived power. Jean made sure he was always prominent in various organizations and academically.
      Blondes got ignored the most. If he was given a choice, it was always the brunette or the redhead. Even race was a factor. Black girls over white girls, etcetera. The point was to trigger a naturally occurring desire to be noticed and win in competition.
      He would never approach or respond favorably to the prettiest girl in a group. Number two tried harder, and also raised the ire of numbers one and three. Likewise, the slightly older girl inflamed and drove the younger one to prove herself worthy of attention.
      Women were generally attracted to one of three things: intelligence, brutality or security.
      He naturally had the intelligence factor down. But the sad fact of life was that women were usually most attracted to brutal men. Because from a

biological standpoint, brutal men begat brutal children. And brutal children survived.
      The bad-boy, brutality angle had a number of aspects. He drove motorcycles and muscle cars. He favored perverse and somewhat violent t- shirts, where appropriate. He hunted. There was usually a hint of leather in his attire at all times.
      Together these factors kept him in constant demand. Which was good, as libido and intelligence are intrinsically linked. It didn't hurt that he put it on women hardcore, sexually. And those women talked to other women about it.
      Best of all, if he tired of one, or had designs on a new one, he simply became too nice. Fawning. Clingy. Inevitably, they would seek a new bad boy.
      It wasn't a foolproof system, but it had served him well so far, and got him through grad school.
      But that was mere sideline. Jean had an entirely different area of expertise, where concerned research and academic life.
      Like most men of greatness, he was obsessed with a subject, almost dangerously so. In Jean's case, it was the authoritarian impulse.
      A renaissance man, his knowledge of history, politics, psychology, and economics drew for him a disturbing picture.
      Ponerology, the study of the psychology of evil, taught him to fear and respect the psychopath. Psychopaths, contrary to popular opinion, were not knife-wielding maniacs. They were businessmen, politicians, priests, bosses, world leaders.
      Four to six percent of men, and one to two percent of women possessed no human emotion whatsoever. This lack of internal constraints had far-reaching implications. Psychopaths are smart. Much smarter than most. This volatile combination enables them to social climb and acquire wealth with relative impunity, unimpeded. So most positions of power were held by psychopaths, globally.
      This, Jean surmised, had led to the development of a psychopathic society, which forced normal people to adopt aspects of psychopathy as survival traits.
      And this concept became part of Jean's internal struggle: was he himself psychopathic? He didn't want to believe this was so. But he also

conceded that a true psychopath could even deceive himself. Perhaps, Jean thought, I pretend to have emotions to fool myself as well as others.
      On a biological level, Jean also knew emotions were, like everything else, survival traits. Howard Bloom had explained it all wonderfully in 'The Lucifer Principle'.
      In essence, Bloom posited, nature was a motherfucker. She would kill a million lemmings if that meant one survived and strengthened the breed as a whole. Or even all of them, if they were too weak to survive.
      Interestingly enough, lemmings (also his all-time favorite video game) were a bad example for Jean to use. They did not, in fact, throw themselves into the sea en masse, as was the popular belief. The famous film footage from the fifties that purported to show this actually depicted Disney filmmakers herding the animals over a cliff in order to make a nature documentary.
      That really underscored Jean's basic belief; man was the most dangerous animal of all. Woman equally so.
      Although depicted as the fairer sex, women could be at least as harsh as men, if not worse, under some circumstances. Women, in almost equal measures, could and would either sacrifice themselves to save their children, or wouldn't hesitate to kill others for the same reason.
      To counter that (nature was delightfully slippery and hard to nail down), women also left newborns in dumpsters, flushed them down toilets, or fed them to dingoes.
      A woman could also cut your cock off or set you on fire while you slept despite the fact that, no matter the back-story, they could simply abandon their abusive situation.
      This led Jean to develop some interesting theories of his own in regard to gender. What if, he thought, men were actually the more sensitive half of the gender duo?
      It certainly seemed that way to him, which is why he never hesitated in manipulating them for sex. But perhaps this was self-serving cynicism on his part.
      This constant onrush of complex thought kept him busy mentally, but it was exhausting. He sometimes wondered if schizophrenia wasn't a simpler answer to his own personal issues.
      But even that realization wasn't a resolution, just a signpost on the road of life. A flashing yellow caution light perhaps.

weight, longevity, etcetera. But then it got impossibly complex. Like susceptibility to diseases for instance.

Even her ten- gig cards filled up fast when you shot at half resolution. She didn't ever use the full six megapixel ability of the camera, but she might, taking panoramas or large outdoor shots.

Studies showed that they introduced so much noise that picture quality actually began to degrade beyond that point. Until the technology improved, she's stick to what she had.

      It was an effort to cover her trail. PC forensics would get the machine when the normal investigators took over.
      On a whim, she opened a command line and typed in a few program names from memory. When she typed 'starcmd', a familiar interface window popped up. Jackpot.
      It was a common key logger, an app that recorded everything typed on a PC: which apps were launched, what sites were visited, etcetera. She checked a few settings to make sure the log file wasn't encrypted and wouldn't be deleted on exit. No need to screw over her own team. Maybe they'd find something she missed.

in this particular den of inequity. And, just maybe, Angel was the shooter. It was not a discountable possibility.
      The keylogger would reveal all, she mused. There was a chance the PC was 'pwned', under the control of a hacker, but she suspected the PC's owners had installed it for his own reasons.
      Now she had to slow down the PC forensics guys, who weren't always that thorough anyway. They were too busy with child porn, which was a messy job. They fought it, and viewed it. She tried not to dwell on that.
      She changed the administrator password to 'flower' and exited the accounts application. This way, the guys couldn't just waltz in, shut the PC down and take it to the lab. She needed time to solve the case. Paige played to win.
      By using a common word, they could brute force crack it, but Paige knew there were much quicker and better methods they could and probably would use. Still she'd have a day or two's lead on them. Plenty of time to solve the case before they even got started on it.
      Finally, she uninstalled the apps, deleted her settings, and edited all trace of herself from the keylogger's log file. Not a perfect cover up, but enough to fool PC forensics, she felt.

"Hey!" He called from outside, "I've got some good stuff. Are you
done?"
"Yeah", she said, walking over to the back door. "But call your wife.
You're taking me to a late lunch."

Chapter 4 - Fascist Gene
      "Ah, Mr. Simons! Good to see you. What's on your mind?" He motioned for Jean to sit down.
      He debated the offer and then sat. A palaver with the prof could eat up an hour, easily. "Just passing through, and I thought I'd say hello."
      "How goes the research? At last glance..." He pulled up Jean's current semester transcripts.
"...you seem to be in good standing."
      The mid-term grades had been posted the day before. Professor Woodard, at nearly seventy years old, didn't miss a trick.
"Classes are great," Jean said. "It's the thesis I'm struggling with." "Morally, physically, or intellectually?" Dr. Woodard intoned that
perhaps it was the last one. And he was mostly correct. Truthfully, it was a little of each. But it was a daunting intellectual exercise, most of all. Then again, this was his doctorate degree they were discussing.
      "Mostly the latter, of course. But I'm also fairly frazzled and stressed. And, even worse, I'm starting to have questions about the morality of my research."
      Jeans operating theory was that personality defects could be isolated and eliminated, much like diseases. That included ailments such as schizophrenia, psychosis and phobias.
      More to the point, Jean sought to eradicate the authoritarian impulse, the belief that one individual should be able to control another.
      Jean believed that this trait lead to Hitlers, Stalins, Maos and the like. Billions of human lives had been wasted as a result of this personality trait, which he felt was at the root of psychopathy.
      He even had a catchy name for it: The Fascist Gene. He could imagine it being referred to as such in articles about himself in Time and Newsweek.
      Eliminate he fascist gene, he surmised, and you eliminate totalitarianism. His general research focused on detecting the gene sequence that determined susceptibility to mental illness. His own pet project was his anti-authoritarian premise.
      "It's good you have doubts. That means you're human. Most good researchers hit this stage, eventually. If they don't, they're either amoral, or their research isn't all that important. Einstein had doubts. So did Salk. We know this because they wrote about it."

"I tend to think Einstein's work led to some bad shit," Jean said. "Oh, it definitely did. So did Salk's for that matter. So," he said,
igniting his ivory pipe in defiance of the university tobacco policy, "will yours."
      He looked Jean squarely in the eyes and exhaled slowly for theatrical effect.
      "All significant research has negative implications. It's the nature of the beast, my boy. Science is a dual-edged sword. You know this."
Jean nodded.
      "Consider the Internet itself. A DARPA project, fully. It was entirely owned by the military, from start to finish. That means it was designed to be insecure, openly-readable and trackable, for the right people."
      "I never thought about it much," Jean said. "To me, the net started with Tim Berners-Lee."
      Dr. Woodard sighed and re-lit. "Some people's kids," he said, rolling his eyes.
      "When the Internet went commercial, things got much worse. AOL is a Company operation. Give people free or cheap access with the perception of being anonymous, and watch them dig their own graves. The other side of the sword?"
"Freedom of information," Jean answered.
      "Exactly. For all of the Machiavellian machinations, the internet has done more good than harm. The propaganda is still slanted in favor of freedom-loving people with brains and morals. Sure, it can be a cesspool, and it's the new cable TV and electronic babysitter. But the net effect, pun intended, is a positive one."
      "And just to drive my point home," the professor continued, "even the most seemingly benign research can have negative implications. AR researcher Jaron Lanier crippled internet two, once, from the sheer volume of bandwidth he required for a demo."
"Damn," Jean exclaimed. It was hard to imagine.
      "So, I'd say keep an eye on the impact your research may have, intended or otherwise. But don't let it hold you back. If Einstein hadn't done the work, maybe the Germans or Japanese would have done it first."
      He gazed thoughtfully at Jean for a moment, "What, specifically are you struggling with?"

      "Morally, we've covered that. My concern is the long-term impact my work could have. The Nazis, to go Godwin for a moment, worked hard to eliminate the handicapped, both mental and physical. Given time, I've no doubt they have tried to eliminate schizophrenics, the autistic, Asberger's sufferers, etcetera. People that we now know are capable of great contributions to society."
Dr. Woodard stopped him to interject. "There's a little Nazi in you, it seems."
Jean  looked  both  stunned  and  puzzled.  But  he  didn't  object.
Woodard always had a bigger point to make.
      "What you're suggesting is that non-contributors to society are less deserving of life than yourself. A Nazi concept, basically."
      "Noted. But what I meant and felt was in opposition to that." Jean replied.
      "Oh, I know that, fully, Mr. Simons. I was really pointing out that there's a little of that in all of us. No matter how we fight to deny or suppress it. Or intellectualize it away."
      The professor emptied his pipe. "Bottom line, others will use your research for good and evil. What matters is what you yourself do with it."
"What else?" Woodard asked.
      "Well, it now seems to pale in significance, but it doesn't seem like this software project will be done before my thesis is due."
"Jean, a good software project is never done."
Jean's cheeks flushed with colors. He was embarrassed, "Gotcha." "Such is the way of committee work, Jean. And since that project is
basically beyond the scope of a single person, you're stuck." "I know. Hence my difficulty."

"Either approach is valid. There may be other ways to handle it" Jean stood to go.

      "If you spent less time banging co-eds, you'd have more energy for school."

      On his fourth research database archive, he found something extremely interesting. It was a fairly significant work from a promising University of Alabama upstart by the name of James Brown.

      Many on the list were aghast. Jean considered them undedicated slackers. They probably hadn't written the first
line of code, yet. Jean had a solid, commented outline, and really felt he had about an eighth of his module written. The easy parts, to be sure, but he had gotten started.
More importantly to his ego and wounded sense of pride, not to mention his life's work, were numerous congratulatory messages to big Jim Brown regarding his first solo publication.

Prior to this, they'd released co-authored papers, and shared all secondary research and sources. This was a major turning point in their relationships both personal and professional. Quite possibly a dead end.
He fumed, but resisted the urge to reply, either positively or negatively. When dealing with communication and bad feelings, it was always best to wait a day or three before replying.
With electronic communication, that was much more important. On the phone, face to face, it was a two-way street. Via an electronic conduit, the nuances of emotive expression were lost, essentially, and wildly open to misinterpretation. This was true of both chat and email.
      Furthermore, people had a tendency to unload rants and opinions in email that they never would have permitted themselves to express in person. This, he knew from experience.

Chapter 5 - Square Peg, Part Two
      P.J. followed Paige up the road to a local, non-chain steakhouse. They both exited their vehicles with a handful of notebooks, paperwork, and writing implements. Paige, in addition to these analog writing tools, brought along her Alianware laptop, all green shiny metal and blinking lights. She had hated working narco, even in California, where she never once had to bust anyone for pot. Somehow, working homicide was actually less seedy than the drug underworld, which was actually rife with rape, torture and kidnapping, in addition to massive numbers of killings.
      The recipes were pretty complete, which was rare. Meth recipes were often treated as folklore, passed on by word of mouth, and rarely written down. She couldn't be sure without more work, but they seemed to have had more than one author.
      It was neatly edited, with all the obsessive precision meth use brings, early on, anyway. She used to wonder how people who were so careful and paranoid got busted, when she joined the force.
      Later on she realized that their paranoia actually worked against them in the latter stages of their addiction. What was caution on the upswing of their bell curve turned into noticeably bizarre behavior on their eventual slide back down. It also uncovered and amplified any underlying psychoses they had.

      Post-decline meth use pretty much mirrored schizophrenia. This all led to carelessness and lots of behaviors that eventually got them tagged as users. And poor users of meth eventually became sellers, cooks, or whores.
      There was another type of meth user, Paige knew. The construction worker, the writer, the artist, the musician. The doctor, lawyer, cop, judge. They used meth to be productive. Super-productive. People who could easily afford their little habits, and used in moderation.
      There was still a negative health impact, but most never progressed to full-blown addiction. Addiction led to binge usage, which was the most unhealthy aspect of most drugs, and also disrupted their previously well- ordered and scheduled lives.
      Paige often felt that pharmaceutical meth and cocaine, devoid of contaminants, could revolutionize the country, if people's addictions were controlled. It would be a long time before even California considered that sort of decriminalization, she knew. But she dutifully voted Libertarian at each election, all the same.
      She was also keenly aware that such drugs fueled the Nazi war machine in World War II, and that benzedrine and amphetamines even got our own pilots amped up for long-distance flying and battle.
      Paige never needed speed, anyway, as she was naturally hyper to begin with. Coffee and marijuana were plenty. As long as she had at least one of them at hand, she could work twenty hour days for months at a time, provided she had a project to motivate her.
      Coffee to wake her up and get zinging, pot to inspire and spur her creativity, and when the caffeine wore off, more herb to sedate her and let her mind and body rest and repair itself. Her system got results, too.
      More so that the recipes, which mostly supported her belief that this murder was essentially a slightly more sophisticated robbery, Paige found what appeared to be directions.
      She'd actually overlooked it, before. She made a note to check the recipient. It was cryptic shorthand, so she didn't think much of it, before. It also didn't make reference to specific landmarks or addresses, so it would be fairly impossible to locate.
      But who ever it was sent to knew where it was, and what was there. Paige knew it was a lab or a cache of cooked dope. Possibly, a big stash of pre-cursor chemicals.

      That was the problem with prohibition. It resulted in ludicrous new laws that made the purchase of over-the counter drugs like Sudafed both legal and illegal at the same time. Worse than '1984', it resulted in a Kafkaesque world more akin to Terry Gillian's film 'Brazil'. Almost anyone could e arrested at any time, according to laws that were nonsensical on their face.
      Meth precursor laws also resulted in a lot of innocent farmers being robbed and even killed for even a quart of anhydrous ammonia. Meth users considered anhydrous homebrew to be the best crystal short of the super-lab variety.
      Whatever was once located at the spot indicated by the vague directions was gone, now, and was also valuable enough to kill for. Convinced that this was the lead she was looking for, Paige rechecked the log file.
      When she did, she felt a tingle of electricity. It was sent to Angel1968@yahoo.com. The suspected girl friend? She hadn't considered that the killer was female, but she knew that all too well that it was entirely possible.
      In fact, few people knew that meth was partly behind the Manson killings. Tex Watson and Susan Atkins were secretly imbibing from a Gerber's baby food jar full of meth on both nights, before the LaBianca house and the Polanski residence.
      That was a case she would have loved to have investigated. Poor Sharon Tate, beautiful and eight months pregnant, begging for her unborn child to be cut of her so it could live, even as she died. The epitome of tragedy.
      Ed Saunders, in 'The Family', laid out the case for the Manson murders being contract killings, and it was compelling stuff. Few knew that Charles Manson had extensive underworld ties due to a near lifetime in prison.
      In fact, he did time with one of the last surviving gangsters from the Capone era. Manson definitely had mob ties. And Leno LaBianca, supermarket owner, did as well.
      As coincidences go, in a murder investigation, that was highly significant. Saunders put forth the additional theory that the Hollywood party crowd at the Polanski-Tate residence were killed because they owed a considerable amount to the mob for drugs.

      Paige felt that theory didn't hold much water. Any single resident that night could have probably paid that in cash, especially to save their lives. Perhaps not Jay Sebring, but Abigail Folger, heir to the Folger's coffee fortune?
      Sharon Tate, one of the brightest up-and-coming stars in Hollywood, at the time? Even director Roman Polanski, absent that night, could have easily afforded it. Furthermore, Paige knew Hollywood types didn't deal with the mob directly.
      They bought from middle-men, and such middle men would incur the wrath of that organization for such a hit. It could have potentially led to a bigger investigation, and so the intermediary dealer would probably have been killed as a result.

But she was pretty sure the chief wasn't going to let her reopen that particular case file. Hollywood and the music industry regularly got away with murder. Ask Jim, Jim, Janis, Bob or Kurt, for instance. Hell, ask Elvis. Not all of them were murdered but a few of them definitely indicated a pattern. That included John Lennon as well. There were even huge anomalies in the RFK case that Paige would have loved to have tackled.

Oh, well. She was probably just born too late. Maybe she'd get an important case down the road.

She was working her way through bullet points of an investigative outline she'd developed, but it didn't pay to adhere too rigidly to it, a foolish consistency being the hobgoblin of small minds and all that.

      Shit. There was no correlation at all. The damn doper's date and time settings on the PC were wildly off, by a matter of years. Possibly by design, but more than likely through carelessness, or because the battery on the motherboard of the creakily ancient Wal-Mart purchased E-machine had failed.
It would have been too easy to solve, otherwise. Shit.

Kinderwhore girls dressed like pre- school prostitutes, mixing porn star caliber clothing with childhood icons like backpacks, lunch boxes, ribbons and oversized lollipops worthy of Willie Wonka.

Steampunk was sort of reverse future primitive, imagining an eighteen-hundreds era world populated by wild, steam- powered vehicle, machines and even computers.

Considered racially insensitive here in the multi-cultural U.S., Paige knew this was more an extension of their fascination with American culture that overt racism. Two-thirds of the female Asians she knew who dated outside of their race dated black men, it seemed.

However, looking at the file paths, they were all located in Angel's account, not Benjamin Miller's, as she'd expected. An unexpected twist. Maybe Angel wasn't a female after all. Or she was a female with rather unusual tastes.

Her affection had to do more with who had purchased it for her, a French girl named Suzanne Flechette.

      Yowie zowie. She had the motherfucker, now. She dialed an officer whose job it was to run DMV information for the department.
      Three minutes later, she had the name, address and phone number of the killer. She took a breath, looked at her watch and realized it was eleven
p.m. It was a good thing they ran a twenty-four hour shop at work. The suspense would have killed her.

      "WTF? Cll me". Tt was Bryce. Sometimes IM shorthand was just silly. She dialed him up, also unwilling to spend time laboriously typing.

But from the sound of his voice, the price would be too dear, and at this point she had enough evidence to convict, she felt. Or at least to generate a stack of subpoenas.

Finally, her laughter caused him to crack a smile.
      "Ok, let's see your melon, Paige." She didn't bite on that one, but led him to Evidence, where a cooler sat on a table, wisps of steam emanating off it in a display of convection and brownian motion.

Chapter 8 - The Trip
      Jean and Maxine drove about twenty mile northeast to a nondescript state park that was unmanned, and appeared to be little more than a parking lot and rest area. It was quiet and out of the way, but there appeared to be little of interest.
      When they'd taken off their helmets, placing them on their respective seats, Maxine asked, "What's in the pick-a-nick basket, Boo?"
"You'll see. Give me a few minutes."
      Jean produced four Zig-zags, and proceeded to crumble most of a sack of hydro, genetic strain unknown, onto the wooden top of the basket. He was dimly aware that it had been in his pocket when he had been stopped by the traffic cop, along with something else.
      "What's with the papes?" she asked, full of questions. "I have my pipe, and some clear skins. Them Zig-zags are nasty to me, now."
"Ssshhh," was his reply.
      He stuck two papers together in a L-shaped configuration and twisted half of the cleaned herb into a cone worthy of 'Catch A Fire'.
"Are you crazy? I could smoke for a week on that much weed." "Light-weight."
"Ok, three days. But, damn."
"That one's yours," he said, handing her the first one.
      Jean proceeded to roll another cone via a more complicated method she could not discern. Finally, he produced a cellophane packet, and extracted two squares of paper, each less than an eighth of an inch in size.
"Here," he said, and put one under his tongue.
      "Uh-uh," was Maxxy's reply. "Black people are not about eating no acid. Only, like Jimi Hendrix, and he was from Venus. Allegedly."
"C'mon," he said. "It's a single dose. Bayer's Chewable Baby Acid.
Paper laughter."
      "Hey, I sell lots of shit I don't take. Like meth. Black people will snort powder, but we don't do meth. We'll smoke crack, but not ice. Secretly, I suspect we don't want to work that hard. Ya'll get on that shit and go work 18-hour days. Black people like to go up and down."
"Trust me..." For the second time that day, she did.
      Without further debate, she locked eyes with Jean, opened her mouth, and stuck out her tongue.

      As he was placing the tab on her tongue, in a move vaguely reminiscent of a Catholic mass, she closed her mouth, engulfing his finger, and gave his fingertip a little swirl with her tongue.
      When she finished, Maxine asked him, "So what's this stuff do anyway? I mean, I know, but I don't know. You know?"
"Think happy thoughts," he said.
      He placed the remaining cleaned weed back in the bag, pocketed it, and flipped the handles around, somehow turning the picnic basket into a backpack. Before he put it on, he took out a small flute-like instrument and a pointed hat with a feather on it.
Placing the hat on his head, he asked, "How do I look?" "Like Robin Hood," she said.
"Excellent."
      He stuck the flute, actually a recorder, in his back pocket, put the now backpack on, and led them to a path into the trees.
      When they'd gotten inside the treeline a bit, he lit his cone and handed her the lighter.
"Maxxy?"
"Jean?"
"I love you."
She smiled, but said nothing in reply and lit her own spliff.
      And so they both walked and smoked in silence, enjoying the time away from their hectic college lives. American culture provides few respites from the constant barrage of audio-visual noise which prevents people from getting in touch with their inner selves.
      Another fifteen minutes later, and they were both thoroughly baked, so Jean put the remainders away. As they stood in a clearing, Maxine moved in close, delivering a kiss that was equal parts emotive and passionate.
"Mmmm," was Jean's reply. "Where are we going?" she asked.
"Just a sort of secret place I know of."
      He began to play the recorder as they walked. Soon, the acid started to kick in.
      The ground gradually became more hilly, and their progress slowed slightly. The evening's sun light filtered through the trees, giving everything a nearly supernatural glow.

      Maxine began to absorb more detail as time went on. Jean's somewhat hypnotic tunes at some point ended, and were replaced with bird songs. Soon, she found herself admiring the rough exterior of the trees, the fractal perfection of the pine needles, the magical properties of sunlight.
"It's so pretty," she said, nearly under her breath.
      They crested a hill, and came to a large clearing at the top. From their vantage point, they could see the setting sun.
"Let's take a break," he said softly.
      He produced a thin blanket from the basket and spread it on the grass, motioning for her to sit.
      They sat, fingers intertwining, knees touching, until the sun dipped beneath the horizon. Time seemed to slow and stretch. The longer they were still, the more they saw.
      Squirrels. Rabbits. The birds serenaded them until night was fully upon them. They both watched reverently as a doe and her fawn crossed the path at the base of their hill.
      "Bambi," Maxxy whispered, and they both broke into a fit of giggling.
      When they finally again fell silent, Jean said, "Look," and pointed upward. The stars were out.
      He lay backward and stretched his arm out behind her, and she followed his lead, placing her head on his arm, and nestling close to his body.
      The stars pulsed and swirled above their heads. Maxine had never in her life viewed the night sky with such clarity of vision.
      At some point, in the peak of their shared experience, a shooting star fell left to right across their field of view.
"Ooohhh," was all she could manage to say.
      Jean produced a second blanket from the seemingly bottomless picnic basket, and they eventually slept, spoon fashion, until the dawn.
      Jean awoke first, out of habit, and raided the contents of the basket, emptying it. Maxine opened her eyes twenty minutes later to find an array of sliced fruits, meats, cheeses, crackers, Melba toast and bottled water.
      When he saw she was awake, he lit a candle, which sat in the center of the arrangement.
"Good morning, starshine," he said

      Maxine's brief moment of disorientation gave way to realization as she recalled the wondrous beauty she had experienced the night before.
      Hunger and thirst helped to ground her, and they ate breakfast gazing at each other with an implied hunger that went beyond simple nutrition.
      When most of the smorgasbord had been consumed, Jean asked, "How do you feel?"
      "It's funny. I feel like I was just born, today. Great. Sort of what I remember from being a kid, when a puddle of water in the park was like a pool. An ocean you could play in all day and never get bored."
      "That's as apt a description of the experience as I've ever heard," Jean said. He produced two champagne glasses, a miniature bottle of Crystal, and a single rolled joint.
      He filled their glasses and lit the remainder of their breakfast. Handing her a bubbling flute of the shimmering pink-yellow liquid, he raised his own and asked, "To us?"
      "To us," she agreed, and they clinked their glasses together in a toast that was symbolic of something much deeper.
      As they sipped their champagne and smoked, Jean said, "In ancient Japan, a couple who desired to be married met with the local Shinto priest. The two sat opposite him as he conducted the ceremony, which culminated in his igniting of a small pile of marijuana on the altar between them."
      "I never knew that," Maxine said. "And I have a minor in cultural anthropology."
      "The couple each inhaled a small amount, and with this, they were then two souls eternally connected. From there, they went to their honeymoon, and a lifetime of companionship."
      "So, what are you saying, Jean?" she asked, her eyes aglitter, teeth sparkling.
"I'm sayin," was his reply.
      They finished their own drinking and smoking ritual, and Jean began to repack the picnic basket.
      He threw out the few remaining morsels of food, and said, "For Bambi," and they both had to laugh.
      When he had the refuse packed away, the spot again pristine, he extended his hand and pulled Maxxy to her feet. The held each other in warm embrace for what felt like forever.

      Then Jean took her hand, and grabbed the blankets, leaving the basket on the ground.
"Come on," he said.
      Instead of heading back to the park, they continued the trek they had began the previous evening, passing the treeline at the foot of the hill where the deer had crossed.
      They continued for fifteen minutes or so, treading up and down the increasingly hilly terrain. An unfamiliar sound akin to white noise began to invade the ears of Maxine.
      They crested a final hill as the sunlight exploded with brilliance, to encounter a verdant prairie, pastoral to the last detail. To their far left was a small waterfall, and a blue ribbon of water several feet wide sauntered down the middle of the wide clearing.
      Maxxy was way ahead of him. She ran to the waterfall, giddy with excitement.
      "I was going to bring you here last night, but we got here a little late. It's prettier in the sunlight."
"I'm glad we waited," Maxine said solemnly.
      And with that she unabashedly began to remove every trace of her clothing.
      Jean spread out the blanket for her to pile her clothes onto, and then he did the same.
      For a long while they splashed and frolicked in the cool, refreshing waters, laughing and yelling like children.
      Eventually, they left the stream and dried each other with the other blanket, to make love for the first and last time.

Chapter 9 - R & R

In the online nerd rap world, it was 1986 all over again, it seemed. Each Nerdcore MC, as they called themselves, could be likened to a spiritual predecessor from rap's golden era.

      By Saturday, she was bored stiff, and half hoped the phone would ring with the chief calling her back in early.
      On Sunday morning she nearly hurt herself rushing to answer the phone, for that very call.
      "Are you done slackin yet?" the chief asked, not being one to dance around the issue.
      "I guess..." Paige replied, not wanting to seem too eager, but at the same time overjoyed to be returning to the work she loved. "I really could use a raise, though."
      "I'll buy you a ladder, goddammit. Do you know how to get to Lemon Heights?"
"I know how to read a map, chief. Got an address?"
      "Yeah, smartass, I do. I need you to get on down here before these morbid tomb raiders clean every last bit of evidence up. The stupid motherfuckers."
      He told her the street address, and Paige wrote it down on a Post-It note, grabbed her laptop, and hit the freeway.
      Sunday morning traffic in southern California was akin to a Friday afternoon is another city. Thirty miles turned into fifty minutes of travel time. Paige realized that in her haste, she'd forgotten to bring coffee, and hadn't yet smoked that morning.

      Lemon Heights was a two-lane road that seemed to meander endlessly over and around the only mountainsides in the area. Technically Orange, California, in part, Lemon Heights was considered a city unto itself.
      A lot of extremely wealthy but private families lived here, shoulder to shoulder with a smattering of celebrities who seemed to move in and out, buying and selling houses, but never settling in for more than a few years.
      Paige suspected it was too far outside of L.A. proper for your average egomaniacal party animal celebrity.
      She found the location using her nearly forgotten Thomas Guide under her seat. In years prior to the advent of Map Quest, Google Maps and the like, no one traveled SoCal without one, and Paige still considered it a required tool in her line of work.
      Particularly when she ran out of her apartment without printing up driving directions, as she had today.
      She drove down the driveway, which branched in two directions as it wound down the mountainside beneath the elevation of the road, and noted how secluded the ungated property was.
      She took the shorter right-hand branch when she saw the myriad of police cruisers and investigative team vans.
      The house was originally a split level ranch style home that had been added onto, with an incongruous second story tacked on, as if by afterthought, to the right-hand side. Rambling, and without much style, it did possess character.
      It made her wonder about the character of the owners. Overall, Paige thought, the property wasn't very impressive, at least at first glance.
      Although the entrance wasn't taped off, Paige dialed Terry's cell phone rather than risk disturbing a latent print on the door, or by touching the doorbell. She was also reluctant to track through the house without knowing more about the crime scene.
      "Get in here, goddammit," the chief said without so much as a hello. "What took you so goddamned long?"
      Paige interpreted that to mean, "Welcome back, Ms. Burner. It's great to have you back on the job," in Chiefspeak.
      She used a pen to depress the handle that opened the heavy oaken front door, which was framed on either side by two mammoth plates of

glass, each nearly fifteen feet wide, extending ceiling to floor. They were so immaculate, you could walk right into then if you weren't careful.
      She heard a cluster of voices far to her right, and followed them through the lush elegance. The interior had the same ramshackle lack of style as the exterior, with Japanese room dividers juxtaposed with a medieval suit of armor, for instance. The entire structure bespoke of the owner's eccentricities.
      At the far end of the house was a half hexagon sun room with a dark wooden floor. Several uniformed officers and plain clothes detectives stood around. As she approached, they ceased talking amongst themselves and parted like the Red Sea to reveal a door that opened upward from the floor.
      With a nod, one of the dicks indicated that she was expected down the stairs beneath the trap door. She made note of a rug off to one side of the room, which, she surmised, ordinarily covered the entrance to this secret sub-level.
      She descended to see the chief and a senior investigator standing over the sheet-covered body that was presumably that of the property owner. But Paige didn't assume anything.
      "How did you expect me to find you in this labyrinthine abode, chief?" she asked.
      "You're a goddammed detective, goddammit!" the chief fairly exploded.
      He did have a point, there. This did not dissuade Paige from asking her next dumb question, "What do we have here?"
      Paige knew Terry's exact words in advance, and they said them in unison: "A goddamned dead body, goddammit!" The investigator behind the chief turned bright red in an effort not to laugh.
      He knew this little performance art piece was for his amusement, but the chief continued, undaunted. He either didn't know or didn't care that Paige was lightly mocking his predictable nature.
      "This," the chief said, "was Larry Tate," and pulled back the forensics hospital sheet to reveal a distinguished looking gentleman in a tacky robe worthy of Hugh Hefner.
Paige had to laugh, further increasing the chief's ire.
      "What's so goddammed funny, Burner? Are you stoned out your goddammed mind?"
"Larry Tate, chief. Darren Steven's boss on Bewitched."

      "Yeah, that's a goddammed laugh riot, Burner. I guess I'll leave you, Mr. Tate and Dick Sergeant here to play the goddamn sitcom edition of Trivial Pursuit. My grandson has a Pop Warner game this afternoon."
      With that, Terry stormed up the stairs, sending the officers above scrambling to get out of his way, like Spaniards before a Pamplona bull.
      "You know," Brad the investigator said, "No one else would ever get away with fucking with him like that, in a million years."
"I know," Paige said, lightly smiling. "Dick Sergeant. Mheh."
      Brad shot her a look, but she knew him too well to be intimidated, and he folded, eventually matching and exceeding her growing grin.
      "Mr. Tate, here, was robbed this morning." He nodded to the open safe. "His wife, Sharon, is upstairs, equally dead."
      If Brad knew how unlikely the name of the wife was in conjunction with her husband's, he didn't let on, so Paige let it pass without comment. She did get a slight chill at the foreshadowing she had experienced earlier that week.
      Premonition? Jung called it 'synchronicity'. But in Paige's line of work, you had to be at least a little psychic to get the job done. At the end of the day, they were both labels describing the same phenomenon.
      Most scientists, almost all, when asked in public forums, said they didn't believe in extra sensory perception. At the same time, any scientist worthy of the title had at lest a passing familiarity with quantum communication.
      Quantum communication occurs at the subatomic level, and happens instantaneously, without regard to time or distance. Long since established as reality through procedures such as quantum linking, quantum communication is the exact same phenomenon as so-called psychic activity. It was a matter of terminology.
      In fact, ordinary people experienced it routinely, and weren't aware of it. Researchers established that dogs often wait for their owner's return several minutes prior to their arrival, no matter how randomized the patterns.
      It has also been established that average people can accurately predict who is calling or emailing them at a rate statistically more significant than random guesses.
      The subject interested Paige, but she didn't dwell on it. She just considered herself a bit more gifted than others, and paid close attention to

hunches, feelings and instinct, like any good detective does.
      She shook off this minor, insignificant coincidence and refocused on the job at hand.
      "Can I see the other one?" she asked. As they began to walk up the stairs, Paige nodded to the open safe. "Any idea of what was in there?"
      "No," Brad answered. "The usual things you'd kill for, I'd expect. Cash, jewelry, maybe stock and bond certificates. Maybe we'll catch them in a resale attempt on the back end. As it stands, we're stone cold. No leads at all."
      They emerged from the wine cellar and tasting room into the sunroom, blinking off the change in luminance.
      Paige followed Brad up a circular staircase, through the master bedroom, into a cedar closet bigger than her living room and kitchen combined. The smell of wood was nearly overpowering. She guessed it was a relatively new addition.
      It was the same scenario. Another body, another open safe. Viewing the corpse wouldn't tell her much that the blood splattered on the walls already hadn't.
"Who alerted the police?" Paige asked.
"The neighbors. Shots fired. We were here within ten minutes or so.
Erm, fifteen, tops."
"So they've been dead for two hours or so. Any servants here?"
      "No one. The back sliding door was open, front door locked. I guess the help has Sundays off."
      They walked down the staircase to the solarium again. "Can I get some coffee?" Paige asked. Wordlessly, the head of the Orange County investigative unit went off to fetch Paige a cup of joe.
      She opened the curtains of the sunroom to take in the view and think while she waited.
      Any doubts she had before about the property were instantly erased. There was a huge, intricately tiled pool immediately outside the windows. Bougainvillea bloomed, growing across a wooden bridge that spanned a section of it.
      That must be a bitch to clean, she thought idly. Bougainvillea, although beautiful in bloom or not, dropped flower petals like Dennis Miller dropped obscure references to eighteenth century poets and philosophers.

      Beyond the pool, an exquisitely landscaped mountainside extended down hundreds of yards toward a huge pond and waterfall at the bottom. Everywhere Paige looked exotic roses bloomed in colors exceeding those of the rainbow. Red brick paths extended in all directions, intersecting with brick platforms and staircases.

Chapter 10 - Departure
      When Jean and Maxxy finally resumed their academic life, after each had taken another day to recuperate, their heads were full of new approaches and ideas for their respective projects.
      Maxine had a more unified view of encapsulation, inheritance, and object-oriented programming in general. She had come to realize that polymorphism was conceptually related to pointers. With that realization, she broke through the remaining barriers that had kept her from completing her third-year coding assignments.
      Jean's new approach, in part, was based on the gram of superlab meth that came in the pharmacologically-cornucopic array he had gotten through Maxine.
      Psychedelics, LSD, anyway, didn't have the same mind-expanding effect on him as it did on Maxxy, as he'd done it plenty of times before. It was more of a refresher course, for Jean. He dropped once or twice yearly to clear the cruft from his head and refocus.
      But his routine for the next two weeks had expanded to include snorting a half-line of meth into each nostril at 5 a.m., before J.B. awoke. With that, Jean exhibited a razor-sharp focus on his writing and coding that had caused Beezy to casually remark that he thought Jean had contracted Asberger's Syndrome from a dirty keyboard.
      Jean failed to see the humor in this. This new batch of weed, as potent as it was, could barely cut through the intense focus and drive he was getting from the crystal methamphetamine. He still smoked it, but it wasn't until after 5 p.m. or so each day that it began to affect his mental state, making him more relaxed and creative.
      But the upshot was that he had the energy and direction he had previously lacked, coupled with new ideas and approaches to both his doctoral dissertation and his code module.
      Early into his second week of crystal-fueled coding and writing, Jean found his projects essentially complete, more than a week early in the case of the Douglas Project, and two weeks before his doctoral deadline.
      He's taken an approach to his paper that had been arrived at via discussions with both Jbeez and the Professor.
      Essentially, his thesis paper had two different conclusive endings. The current paper was self-contained, but lacked the hard-hitting, wave- making impact he desired.

      It was enough to get his degree, he knew, but nothing Earth shattering. That was reserved for his alternative conclusions, which depended on results from the Douglas Project.
      Jean could hardly believe he was essentially done with school. His coding module had been checked in, and was winning accolades on the discussion list. It really was an exercise in artful minimalism.
      While writing it, he had kept in mind what Knuth said about premature optimization being the root of all evil. So he had focused on working code first, and then rewrote it with a new attention to speed and efficiency.
      The end result was that this critical import module was blazingly fast and efficient. Multi-threaded, it iterated through the .adm file un multiple passes, much in the way they were iterating through the genome itself. It was a small masterpiece of coding.
      And the speed at which it operated was critical, as the import module would be the bottleneck that couldn't be spread over multiple computers. Inadvertently, Jean had made his mark as a programmer before gained recognition as a genetic researcher.
      He'd even uncovered a subtle problem in the .adm file format, which could have potentially derailed the project and blown the group's scheduling.
      Flushed with success, he dialed Maxine. They'd spoken a few times, and met casually on campus to talk about coding issues, mainly, but they had yet to have another date.
      "Hi, Jeaner," Maxxy said before he spoke. Caller ID removed the element of surprise he used to enjoy in the distant past.
"Maxine, how would you like to buy me a steak?" Jean said.
"What a thoroughly post-modern concept. You're done?" she asked. "I'm done. Little-d done, but done nevertheless."
"Jean, that's great! Now you can stop neglecting me."
      In reality, Maxine had needed time alone to finish her projects as well, but had still craved the closeness they had shared prior to his recent flurry of work.
"I've missed you, too," he said.
"Well, since I'm taking you out, be ready to roll out at seven sharp.
I'll pick you up," Maxxy said. "Wear something low-cut." Jean laughed at this. "Okay, Ma. I'll be ready."

Beaming, he took his final code dump and semi-final draft to Dr.
Woodard.
"Jean!" Woodard mocked. "I thought you'd dropped out." "Heh. Sorry, boss," jean said. "I've been busy."
      To illustrate and accentuate this statement, he dropped one hundred and sixty-eight pages on his mentor's desk.
      "Is that all?" Dr. Woodard teased. "In my day great conifer forests were felled in order that my ideas should be borne."
"Prehistory," Jean teased back.
      He then produced a CD, and related the tale of his optimizations, his bug squashing, and other adventures in coding.
      "Impressive," was Dr. Woodard's response upon viewing the code module's demonstration. "Just in time, too. You look like hell, son."
      Jean grinned bashfully. "Yeah, I know. But I'm in recovery. And it was worth it. Soon, you and I will be colleagues. Peers."
      The professor smiled. "My boy, I am peerless. In academia, I am eternally the server to your client. But it's good to aspire."
      They both laughed at this. The prof had often told Jean that he thought Jean's talents exceeded his own in many technical aspects. Jean, however, was aware of his own limitations, and of Woodard's caliber, with his Sisyphean patience and penetrating insights.
      "Doc," he said when the joking ended and the office air once again grew serious, "I'm selling out."
      Woodard knew this meant Jean was going on to work in the private sector, a position he respected, but didn't fully approve of.
"I understand, Jean. Really."
      Jean looked relieved to hear this. Dr. Woodard's disapproval would have made the decision much more difficult.
      "Universities rarely provide high-performance sports cars, for instance. They tend to lag behind the tech of the day, sometimes by five years or more, save for isolated pockets where they invent it."
Jean nodded at the essential correctness of what the professor was
saying.
"Had I to do all over again, were I a much younger man, I would be
out there in the trenches with you."
      This statement surprised Jean. He'd always imagined Woodard a strict idealist, bound to university life by principles alone.

"You forget," he continued, "I acquired my PhD n my late forties.
You're what, twenty-nine?"
"Thirty-three," Jean answered.
      "A lifetime of difference in those two ages, my boy. I had a wife, a son of college age. My cocky, risk-taking days were long over, at that point." A wistful look clouded his eyes for a moment.
      "Plus," Woodard continued, shaking off his memories, "hot cheerleaders are rarely found in the halls of biotech firms."
      Jean had to laugh. Many a coed had come to Woodard in low-cut blouses and skirts, seeking an easy way out, only to find themselves given hours of rigorous private tutoring. They either picked up the material, or they changed majors. Either way, Woodard was constantly treated to spectacular displays of feminine beauty.
"Do you have any prospects?" he asked.
      "Well, I need to work on my C.V. and resume, but I do have a few firms I'd like to work for. Mostly out west."
      "Am I right to assume a glowing letter of reference would aid you in your quest, good sir knight?"
      "I,	sir,	would	like	nothing	more,"	was	Jean's	reply.	He	left Woodard's office with a head brimming with possibilities and potentialities. He went back to his room intending to start work on his work-
related documents, but instead lay down to rest his eyes, and slept until five-thirty.
      Jean awoke with a start, disoriented. He never slept during the day. Idly, he remembered Dr. Woodard's talk about age, and academia versus private practice.
      Then he remembered he had asked Maxine to dinner to celebrate, and rushed to get ready.
      Seven p.m. and Maxine both found him downstairs in front of his building, dressed to the nines.
      Maxine pulled up to the curb in her Charger, Above The Law's 'Livin' Like Hustlers' on the booming system.
      They exchanged smiles as Jean got in. Before he'd gotten his seat belt on, she'd mashed the gas, slamming him back into his seat. He let the seat belt go, and it retracted back into its holder to his right.
      "I hope you like American food," she eventually shouted over the stereo. Jean noted with irony when they'd arrived that Outback Steakhouse

was ostensibly Australian, but he knew what she meant.
      When they walked in the door, Jean noted that they by-passed the patrons waiting for a table, and were immediately escorted to a back booth, already populated with drinks, salads and appetizers.
      "Two can play the game," she said simply at this. Tables were clearly turning.
      Jean ate and listened, occasionally prompting Maxine for more information regarding the end of her third year in school.
