      Eventually, she'd exhausted her store of new information as they'd simultaneously depleted the initial banquet of food. Jean looked around for a menu, only to see the wait staff approach in pairs. Two removed their empty plates and refuse, and two more replenished their drinks and brought entrees.
      When they'd left again, as quickly as they came, Jean found before him a sizzling New York strip, home fries, a baked potato, and twenty-four large deep-fried shrimp on an over-sized platter between them.
      Maxxy had a filet mignon wrapped in bacon, and steamed California vegetables.
      "Post-modern indeed," Jean remarked as they dug in. He was entirely unused to both the attention, and from a woman taking charge. But, coming from Maxxy, he rather enjoyed it.
"Is there a problem?" she asked grinning, as she bit a large shrimp in
half.
"Not at all, Maxine. But I find you're entirely unique in all the
world."
"I could have told you that," she said, "had you asked."
      "I pretty much already knew it," was his reply. "This dinner date just confirms it."
      When it came time for dessert, Jean knew they'd have to discuss their future, whether together or apart.

Chapter 11 - R & R Part Two
      Paige estimated that the property was some seven to ten acres in size. Even for the ultra-rich, that large of a property was extravagant in California, much less in Lemon Heights. She considered the upkeep requirements of such a property.
Brad returned with an oversized mug, steaming with some exotic

blend.


"I hope cream and sugar is alright," he said.
"As long as there's caffeine, my man." She took a sip, but it was a

bit too hot, yet. "So, what did a search of the property yield?"
      "Uh, nothing, yet." He paused and said, "We haven't looked, actually."
His tone suggested he'd never even considered it, until now.
      If Paige had had coffee in her mouth, she would have spewed it back out in comic fashion. But she resisted the urge to go ballistic.
      "You haven't searched the property yet?" she spat, instead. "The killer could be on the goddamned property right now, goddamnit."
Maybe the Chief had rubbed off on her a tad.
      Paige stormed out of the room, looking for the backdoor exit to the property, with Lt. Brad Pittman in tow.
      They stepped into the kitchen, and exited through the sliding glass door the killer assumedly used to enter.
      On the brick patio surrounding the pool, Paige scanned the property trying to decide which rambling path to follow first. On instinct, she headed for the garages, still patently ignoring Brad at her heels.
      Finding the first side entrance unlocked, they entered the four-car structure and turned on the overhead lights.
      What they found was an array of lawn care equipment, a battered El Camino, and three dead Mexicans.
      Paige produced a handful of evidence baggies and a pair of gloves, seemingly from nowhere. "Write this down," she snapped, and began to retrieve their wallets.
      Being illegals, there were no driver's licenses, but there were I.D. cards and various documents attesting to their respective identities.
      The biggest and oldest was named Jose Blanco, and the youngest and smallest of the bunch, apparently his younger brother, was Salvador.

Between them lay a Miquel Aguillera. All three had been shot, execution- style, in the head.
"Fuck," Brad said.
"Nice work, Lieutenant," Paige said.
      Within ten minutes, Brad had left Paige in charge and sped off to the nearby barrio where the murdered men resided to interview and inform their families.
      Obviously, Brad had botched things so far, and was trying to get a handle on the situation.
      Paige needed to think, having little confidence in the investigators she was supposed to be working with.
      She left the garage to Brad's team of forensics evidence people, knowing that she had the most important clues already. Details such as ballistics evidence generally only mattered in court.
      As it stood, there were still no suspects. Thanks to Brad, they had lost an hour's lead or more.
      An idea was forming in Paige's mind, but it wasn't there yet, and stubbornly wouldn't come. She decided to walk the property.
      The paths crisscrossed the hillside, following the contours of the land horizontally, and up and down the mountain, alternating between steps and landings.
      Paige absentmindedly admired the roses as she strolled about. She looked beyond the pond and waterfall and saw unmarked cars visiting the pop star next door, who had undoubtedly phoned in the initial report of gunfire.
      She briefly considered heading that way, but thought better of it. Her gut feeling was that the solution lay elsewhere.
      Paige eventually came upon a landing with a four-person swing, where two couples could sit facing each other in the cool of the evening and relax.
      Settling in to consider the situation for a while, Paige produced her sneak-a-toke and lighter from her lefthand pocket. A few hits and ten minutes later, she got back up, refreshed, and continued her survey of the property.
      At the upper left corner of the property she walked up a miniature reproduction  of  San  Francisco's  Lombard  Avenue,  interspersed  with

flowering clumps of hydrangea. From the new vantage point, she could now see the remaining areas to explore.
      The waterfall looked interesting, particularly seeing the large, colorful Koi fish that populated the huge manmade, split-level pond. But she wasn't here to sightsee, and it seemed to offer nothing beyond that.
      At the opposite end of the pond was a lakehouse-type building, complete with a dock and paddleboat. Such a languorous life of luxury the retired couple must have lived. Paige hoped they had enjoyed what they had when they were alive.
      Leading away from the pond and lakehouse was the gently curving driveway Paige had come in on. She headed in this direction, initially with the intention of following it back up the slope to the garage to check on the rest of the investigation. But something made her turn in the other direction, instead.
      It was a good five minute walk all the same. She passed under a grape arbor, with its yielding fruit framed by flowering Wisteria vines, past a minigrove of lemon trees, dodging the automatic sprinklers that chose to turn themselves on as she passed.
      When she had reached the bottom of the hill, Paige did allow herself to stroll along the massive Koi pond, with its pussy willow, pampas grass clumps, and spotted orange and white carp.
      After briefly inspecting the exterior of the locked lake house, Paige began her trek up the driveway, which seemed to be a quarter mile long or better, uphill all the way. With an estate like this, she thought, she'd have no need for a gym.
      But behind the lake house, Paige saw something that caused her synapses to twitch. She called Brad.
"Investigative Services, Lt. Brad Pittman speaking."
      "Brad! How's it going? What did you learn? Are you still with the families?"
      "Yeah, Paige. We didn't learn much. All three worked for the Tates, tending their property six and seven days a week. The families are distraught as well as scared, being illegals. We sort of wrecked their Sunday, in more ways than one."
"Give me you location, I'm coming over."
      Paige was familiar with the area. At the base of the small mountain that was Lemon Heights was a school reserved for the children of the elite

who lived there. A mile past that, to the right, was a street perpendicular to the one she was traveling on which demarked a different territory altogether.
      Children who lived beyond this cross-street attended a different school, mixed race and heavily Spanish speaking. Here were the cramped and squalid apartments that housed the servants of the wealthy.
      Paige pulled up to see that once again, the ostensible best her division had to offer had mishandled the situation.
      The parking lot of the ghetto apartments was full of unmarked cars and police cruisers, parked askance, this way and that, lights flashing, doors open in clear violation of basic procedures.
Brad was awaiting her arrival, no doubt hoping she'd save the day.
She brusquely told him to bring her to Jose's wife.
      The woman was a squat, sturdy and stout specimen with dark black hair, indicating a heavy mix of Aztec Indian blood.
Paige began, "Lo siento..."
      And with that, the woman burst into tears. Empathy for the distraught wife and mother found Paige comforting her as she sobbed and heaved a second time.
      Five minutes after the woman, Rosa, she learned, had regained her composure, Paige had the information she needed.
"There," Paige pointed, "Apartment eight."
      The gung ho uniformed officers surrounded the building, made the necessary announcements, and then kicked in the door.
      She knew no on was home, particularly because Rosa had described the car of the two men she sought, which was conspicuous in its absence.
      But after they cleared the premises by conducting a room-to-room sweep, Paige already had enough evidence to convict.
      The careless killers of the Tates, whether by accident, or because it had little value to them, had left behind an inventory sheet from Mrs. Tate's safe.
      On that single sheet alone, nearly two million dollars worth of jewelry was listed.
      When the initial excitement had died down Paige gave Brad the names of the suspected thief-killers, their vehicle make and model, and even a partial plate number extracted from Rosa's memory.

      He issued an APB, and at Paige's behest alerted San Diego authorities, and INS agents working the Mexico-California border station south of San Diego, the busiest in the country.
      She phoned the chief and asked him to meet her back at the Tate's property.
Paige had stopped her car in the driveway, engine idling, to await
arrival.
When he pulled up behind her and honked, she motioned for him to
follow her down the hillside. They drove past a guesthouse, with its adjacent vegetable garden, passing between the garage and a two-story pavilion, a building so large they'd all somehow failed to pay it any attention, whatsoever.
      The chief was lacking his usual sturm und drang, the blustering rage that was uniquely his own.
"Sorry to call you back over today, Chief," Paige began.
      "Not a problem, Burner. The little motherfuckers were losing, anyway."
      Paige had to grin at this. She could imagine the chief at the sidelines, watching ten year olds playing a child's version of football. "Run, goddamnit!" she heard him yell in her mind, "Throw the goddamned ball!"
      "I had other things we needed to discuss, or I wouldn't have bothered you."
"First, tell me how you figured this one out," the chief demanded.
      Paige nodded her head toward a pallet of new red bricks, a pile of sand, a concrete mixer, and another pallet of Easycrete mortar mix, covered with visquine.
      "Brick layers, boss," Paige said. She then turned and pointed up the hillside. "Look at the sheer volume of bricks up there."
      There were enough steps, paths, and landings made of red bricks to build five schoolhouses, it seemed.
      "These paths were an ongoing project, and the represent years of work. According to the wife of one of the victims, her husband's cousin and his helper laid most of these bricks over the past three years.
      The chief tried to imagine employing two fulltime bricklayers for years, and could not.
      "So, this guy killed some of his own family?" the chief asked rhetorically.

      "If they had killed Rosa, the wife, we'd have no leads to follow right now. And I suspect she's the one who told them all about the jewelry and valuables, innocently enough. She was the Tate's housekeeper."
"Well, I'll be goddamned," was all the chief could add to this.
      Paige then spent the next forty minutes recounting the day's events, her focus not on the case itself, but on the myriad of ways it was mishandled.
When she was more or less done, the chief's cell phone rang.
      "They got the sorry motherfuckers," he said, covering the mouthpieces with his hand for a moment. "Do your job, goddamnit!" he snapped into the phone, and disconnected the call.
      "They never made it to the border. They were already so fucked up on beer and cocaine they got pulled over north of San Diego."
      "I think that's sweet," Paige said mirthfully. "Anyway, I know you like to keep abreast of your operations, chief. I'll see you in the morning."
      As she turned her car around to leave, the chief considered how much easier his life would be if Paige headed up the Investigative Services Division.

Chapter 12 - Departure, Part Two
      Once again, Maxine appeared to read Jean's mind. "So, what are you doing after graduation?" she asked.
"Marrying you," Jean almost blurted out impulsively.
      "I'm not sure," he said noncommittally, instead. "I'm going to shop around my curriculum vitae."
      "In the wide world of Alabama genetic research?" she teased. She had him, there.
      "There are one or two firms here worth considering," he said, "but they're mostly in the northeast and out west really."
      "So when are you leaving?" there was a quality in her voice between acceptance and heartbreak.
"Maxxy..." he said.
      "Jean, I understand, really. I'm not staying in no damn Alabama neither. I just had to get out of L.A. for a few years. My fam is there, and L.A.'s in my blood."
      "But that could mean a year without you." He said this as if to himself.
"And a year without you," she reminded him. They both considered this.
"I could stay," he said, finally.
      "No, you can't," she replied. "You'll have a hard time starting a career with 'Jim Bob's 'Bama Hog Farm and Gene Splicing' as your first genetics job."
      "A career's not all that matters in life, Maxine. And for the first time, Jean realized and understood this. She was handling it better than he was.
      "Well, you've worked too damn hard for that," she said. "Besides, I expect more from a potential husband of mine."
"I was thinking the same thing," Jean said. "We'd make quite a

pair."


"Oh, Jean. We already do. The software we could write..." "...the babies we could make," he finished.
"It's funny you should say that. Do you know the prevalence of

unwed teen births in L.A. County?" "No," he had to admit.

      "Sixty-four percent. But in my neighborhood, it runs more toward ninety-eight percent. I dodged more than one kind of bullet when I left there. I would be dead, in Sybil Brand, or I'd have someone's bastard kids. Some things are worth waiting for, Jean. I do want kids, but not L.A. style."
      Jean considered this. "So where do we go from here?" he asked sincerely.
      "Well, I think you go west. I'd never cut it in the northeast. Too damn cold. Too damn east. That's why I'm matriculating here in the south. It's full of rednecks, but also a lot of good folks. And it's warm. We'll hook up again in a year."
"You'd wait that long for me?" "You'd better wait for me," she said.
"Of course," Jean said. "I love you, Maxine."
This time she returned his sentiments. "I love you, too, Jean."
      They both paused, as if absorbing the gravity of the matter. Eventually, it was Maxine who broke the silence, returning to business as usual.
      "This is my uncle, Pookie," she said, handing him a phone number. "Uncle Pook used to be a lieutenant in the Crips," she said, as casually as if she had said he was a plumber. "He can get you anything you want in the valley."
Jean accepted the gift without comment, and put it in his pocket. "Ever heard of a rap group back in the day, white dudes called Third

Bass?"


Jean had to admit he had not.
"Well, they talked a little shit about MC Hammer, of all people, on a

record. Hammer paid Mike Conception to put a hit on them boys."
      "But the kids, over-privileged New York Jew kids, really, didn't take it seriously. Their people paid off the hit, but they still almost got smoked in the Beverly Center Mall. My uncle Pook saved their asses. He was nineteen then, and already a ghetto vet."
      She took a sip of coffee. "He's retired, now. Still affiliated. But no longer in active duty."
"You make it sound like the military," Jean commented.
      "It is, really. There's a lot of street rabble and wannabes, but the core of the organization is tightly-knit and cunning."
"You're the realist thing in Alabama, Maxxy," he said.

"Maybe so," she replied.
"How are you going to be with senior year?" he asked.
      "I'll be ok. We'll still be in touch, right? If I get in a bind, don't think I won't ask for help."
      "You'd better," he said, echoing her admonition from earlier. "This feels like goodbye."
      "It's goodbye for now, Jean. Otherwise I'll try and make you stay, or I'll want to leave."
      She stood to go. "The meal's paid for. You just go out there and make me proud. Ok?"
She bent over and kissed him on the cheek, turned and walked away.
It was all he could do to not call out to her, to implore her to come back.
      He sat and brooded over events for a while, nursing his coffee and cheesecake. She had certainly commanded the situation. He considered how businesslike, how martial her manner of handling things was.
      Ultimately, he liked it. They both had a lot to look forward to, and their next few weeks were cleared for school, and in his case, a job search. She'd spared them both a lot of back-and-forth juggling of complex emotions and soul searching.
      Jean's mind wasn't on school when he got home. He went to bed and dreamed wildly of Maxxy until he fell asleep.
      The next morning, his head was clear. He proceeded to get fogged and caffeinated, his intention on finishing his paper so he could get on with relocating to California.
      Since he hadn't checked on the project the night before, he did this first. According to the announcement listserv, not only had Douglas compiled, but they had gone live.
      Already, hundreds of PCs around the world were iterating through DNA sequences, evaluating possible patterns, and tagging some for human evaluation.
      The project was a success. Even without the results they ultimately sought, they had created a DNA-sequencing supercomputer, a Beowulf cluster that would someday soon change the face of medicine and biology.
      And Jean was at the heart of it. He knew, as the number of participants grew, their chances of detecting second level encoding increased exponentially.

      Jean set about revising his paper. He had no compelling reason to leave his room anymore. He also had to finish off his stash, as he couldn't take it with him, and had to at least consider the possibility that he could be drug-tested as a condition of employment.
      The next two days were a flurry of smoking and writing. Just as he'd felt he'd completed his final revision, new results had been published to the Douglas Project mailing list.
      The first level two sequences were beginning to take shape. The project now had quantifiable results, aside from the successful implementation of the software model. This led to a massive revision of his paper. Now, Jean felt he had a body of work worthy of a PhD.
      Satisfied, he submitted his paper, along with the proper application and requisition fees, to the committee that would ultimately decide his fate.
      Dr. Woodard was a member of that committee. As badly as Jean wanted to see him, he felt it improper, this close to their decision, to meet with him.
      Jean still had a few other loose ends to tie up. He packed what few belongings he wanted to keep, and shipped them to a prepaid storage center in L.A.
      Biotech firms littered the west coast, but he was determined to use Los Angels as his base of operations, no matter where in California he eventually landed.
      He made a further revision to his C.V. and resume, creating several versions each uniquely tailored to attract the attention of specific types of companies.
      When he was sure Jimbrowski would be away for hours, Jean felt it was time to dispose of something else.
      He took a small foil packet from his dresser, a remnant of the now empty duffel bag. In it was a tiny clump the size of a pinhead, DMT. He'd never done it before, but knew what to expect, more or less.
      DMT came from two places that he knew of: the human brain, and the roots of the sensitive plant of Hawaii. It was a psychedelic that gave the experience of an eight-hour acid trip compressed into thirty minutes.
      Fifteen minutes of hardcore tripping followed by a fifteen-minute come down period. You could do it on your lunch hour, in theory.
      It was claimed to be an even more spiritual experience than LSD, which was difficult for Jean to imagine. One of his heroes, a researcher

named McKenna, claimed the experience was otherworldly.
      He put the chemical speck on the end of a needle, held it under a glass hanging over the edge of the table and lit it in the same manner as one would smoke hash.
      He watched the smoke rise, filling the tumbler, hesitated for a moment, and then inhaled.
      Jean sat back on the floor against his bed, and for the next twenty minutes, left his body.
      He felt himself rising, slowly at first. He looked down and saw himself sitting on the floor, cross-legged. He saw each detail of the room from an impossible perspective, in the manner of DXM and other disassociatives.
      Jean then became a creature of pure light. He began to accelerate, upward and outward, hurtling through the cosmos. He careened past solar systems, then galaxies. His perspective grew as the universe shrank.
      Soon, he was moving so fast, in a universe so vast, it was as though he was not moving at all, but floating. He observed nebulae. He could see the gravitational curves of space, the pull exerted by massive clusters of stars, acting in concert.
      Jean learned what some cosmologists had maintained was true. Black holes emitted light and energy. Wide bands of understanding filled his mind.
      Astrology, which he'd always dismissed, was essentially correct. The stars and planets exerted an influence on our development; their positions at the times of our conception and birth influenced our personality types and abilities.
      Suddenly, he was falling upward, and growing. He nearly panicked, not out of fear, but because he didn't want to return to Earth. But he thought of Maxine, and his work, and accepted what was happening with passive understanding.
      He continued to travel up, rotating. Soon, Jean was spiraling through a double helix, the proteins each color-coded for easy identification.
      He saw, with his own eyes, second and third layer encoding. He had a glimpse of a fourth layer, and had the impression that it was extraterrestrial in origin.
      Suddenly he was growing. He watched the DNA strand shrink, and moved past shrinking cells, arteries, veins.

      All at once, he had expanded to fill his own body. He had traveled the universe, and had never left his dorm. All of existence was within him.
      He sat for a long time, far longer that the prescribed come down period. He had a lot to think about.
      Finally, he got up and took a shower. He needed contact with Earthly things: water, fire, air, soil.
      The feeling of being from beyond was vaguely unsettling. He wondered if he were somehow alien, or if the experience had made him so.
      Jean grabbed his car keys, and went to the park for hot dogs. He had to feel the sun, the grass, the wind. He needed to feel mortal again.

Chapter 13 - Paige A Go-Go
  Paige's next few weeks were relatively unexciting at work. For whatever reason, strange murders occurred in clumps. She suspected the influence of the moon. She did a lot of support work on more standard cases, and found herself bored. Your average criminal, Paige decided, was decidedly uncreative when it came to homicide.
        She decided to cash in some of her vacation time. The chief was unusually supportive.
        "You can't take a goddamned vacation, goddamnit! If these inept assholes encounter a case that requires actual detective work, they freeze up like goddamned Eskimo pies!"
      "Terry," she began, "I can, and I am. We're talking two weeks max, out of an accrued nine weeks of vacation time I have. I'd like to cash out the remaining seven weeks."
      "No problem, goddamnit. Do you want that in small bills?" The chief was fuming.
      "Chief," she switched gears. "Things are slow right now. Statistically, this is our off-season. Which is why I'm so insufferably bored right now. I need to get out and recharge my batteries."
      "Burner," he huffed, "When you get back, we're going to have to seriously discuss your role in this organization."
      She walked out of Terry's office on tiny clouds. It was the first time Paige could recall such a feeling of freedom. She had always thought it was work that made her happy. She was beginning to realize it was instead adventure, and the unknown.
      She went home at the end of her shift and packed her bags. She left her smoking accessories, naturally, but did pack Ms. Kitty.
      Then she booked a one-way flight to New Orleans for the next morning. She spent the evening smoking and chatting with Bryce, and went to bed forgoing her usual evening session of cat-on-cat violence.
      Paige had always wanted to see the deep South. In her mind, it was the polar opposite of California, and that notion turned out to be one hundred percent correct. She had a loose itinerary in her head, but decided that she would basically play it by ear.
      New Orleans was filthy and beautiful. From the moment she entered the airport, she felt she was somewhere exotic like Istanbul. People moved with a languid slowness that made the entire city seem out of time.

      The organic feel of New Orleans, even at the airport, made L.A.'s filthiest streets seem positively sterile by comparison. It was unlike any city she had ever encountered.
      She rented a cherry-red Dodge Charger with unlimited mileage. The rental chain's agreement allowed her to return it at any outlet for an additional charge. Money was no object where her pleasure was concerned.
      She went to her room at the Radisson near the French Quarter, checked in, and got prepared for her first night out on the town in ages. But this was no ordinary town.
      The spirit of Mardi Gras was present year round. Unlike most cities, public drinking was tacitly permitted. Many bars had areas on the street side of the buildings where you could purchase massive go-cups filled with hand grenades, hurricanes, daiquiris, beer, or any other liquor you desired. As long as it wasn't in a glass container, you could drink freely on the streets of New Orleans.
      It was still daylight when Paige exited her hotel, looking rather sumptuous in attire that was decidedly more racy than her normal tastefully hot business wear she wore for work. She walked past Jax Brewery toward the Quarter, and turned right to take in the sights of the river.
      The Mississippi took her breath away in a manner that the Pacific Ocean had long since lost the ability to do.
      A bridge, far smaller and less impressive than the suspension bridges of San Francisco, nevertheless held a captivating charm of its own. It was nearly anachronistic, in her mind, as it spanned the rushing waters.
      The river seemed a mile wide, and was a model of maritime activity. Great hulking barges traveled up and down the waterway in a ballet the belied their massive weight, pushed along by tugboats that brought to mind a Little Golden Book from her early childhood.
      The city lights were already beginning to be reflected by the water, and Paige made a promise to herself to view it at night before she continued on her journey.
      Where the river walk ended, she took some stairs down to her left, crossed a parking lot, and reentered the outskirts of the French Quarter. She was assaulted by the smell of fried dough, powdered sugar, and a most enticing coffee aroma.
      This was the world famous Caf� Du Monde, a landmark that no one failed to mention to her when extolling the virtues of New Orleans. It

looked unassuming, save for the fact that she'd never seen a simple caf� filled with two or three hundred people.
      Paige found the walkup window used for ordering and bought a cup of coffee and two beignets. It was pretty much mandatory when visiting New Orleans. She had to wait for a young married couple to leave with their strollered baby in order to sit and enjoy the snack. The coffee was stronger and more flavorful than any she'd had before, and the hot beignets were the perfect compliment. Paige was in heaven.
      As she sat and watched the way even the obvious tourists adapted to the easygoing stroll that seemed unique to the region, she admired Jackson Square Park, the centerpiece of the Quarter, and the great Catholic church that loomed behind it.
      When she finished, she took a stroll around the pedestrian-only streets that surrounded the park. New Orleans still had horse-drawn carriages for hire, and, Paige knew, a streetcar that still ran to the Desire housing projects. For a time, she felt she could stay in New Orleans forever.
      Along the fence outside of the park, artists plied their wares, doing sketches in charcoal, watercolors, and even oils. Many were quite talented.
      Opposite the artists were dozens of t-shirt and postcard shops, which provided a stark contrast, art and commerciality, standing in eternal opposition to one another, while at the same time in peaceful coexistence.
      As she passed a side street approaching the church, a tall, well- dressed, clean-cut black man with a newspaper called her aside trying to attract her attention.
      He unfolded the paper to reveal three flat purple buds. "Want to buy some Thai stick?" he asked.
      "Fuck off, cop," she said without pausing. Paige definitely knew better, and the undercover was ham-handedly obvious, besides. She would have enjoyed getting high, but it wasn't worth being arrested over.
      She admired the church, but didn't go inside. God was more apparent out on the streets than inside, she knew. Paige turned left again past the church, and took another, doubling back a half-block to walk through Jackson Park. A looming statue of Andrew Jackson on horseback dominated the landscape, which was ruled by pigeons.
      As she neared the exit to the street, a middle=aged Rasta, whom Paige imagined she had read about in a Tom Robbins novel, appeared next to her as if from nowhere.

"Welcome to New Ohlans, dere, sister," he said, shaking her hand.
      Before Paige could react, he was gone again. In her palm was a large two-gram block of hashish.
      Paige looked around casually, and put this item of interest in her purse. She left the park and was once again back on the street Caf� Du Monde was on and thought, Jah really does move in mysterious ways.
      She walked alongside the waiting teams of horses, where drivers in tophats solicited her for carriage rides. Maybe next trip, she thought. But not alone, for sure.
      At the end of a block, she heard music that fully got her attention. It was a bar called The Matador. On stage inside were some twelve black musicians, young UNO students, by their looks.
      Their extensive horn section dominated the music, in the grand New Orleans heritage. But at intervals, one or another of them would take the horn from their lips to release a funk-charged rap in the old school traditions.
      Paige ordered two Heineken Lights, and sat, enthralled, for over an hour. When they had finished their set, she noticed she had barely started her second beer, so she finished it, used the somewhat dingy restroom, and exited.
      She decided to follow her impulses, and wander off the beaten path a bit, buoyed by a bit of alcohol.
      For a few blocks, the streets grew darker, and were unpopulated. Eventually, she began to see people, and hear pulsating jungle, breakbeat and techno coming at her from various side streets.
      She followed her ears, but also her eyes, as she spotted women in twos and threes down one particular street, which, in New Orleans, were little more than claustrophobic alleyways at times.
      Paige knew the men in this particular area of the city would have zero interest in her.
      She entered a club named Ruby Fruit Jungle, and Paige realized what she was here for. The dance floor pulsated with sweating dykes, femmes, lipstick lesbians, and women who refused to fall into archetypical stereotypes. A few wouldn't be out of place driving a minivan of kids to soccer practice.
      She ordered a Coke and another Heineken Light. She then went into a bathroom stall, after dumping our the coke and washing the can out in the

sink.
Using her penknife, Paige constructed a crude pipe, complete with
carburetor. She broke off a quarter of the hash block, and proceeded to get very high. Ordinarily, she would never smoke out of aluminum, but this was an emergency situation. And she was on vacation, after all.
      The same was true of smoking in public. But a gay club in any city was pretty safe, and in New Orleans, doubly so. She downed her beer, stashed the hash, and ditched the pipe.
Paige felt like dancing.
      Gay clubs weren't only the most permissive, least judgmental places to party, they also had the best music.
      She got down to Lords of Acid, Femme II Femme, Definition FX, Peaches, Junior/Senior, KMFDM, and a ton of other great acts she remembered from her checkered past. It was some sort of retro night, she ultimately decided.
      At some point, Paige found herself dancing with a blonde about her own height, who was forty pounds heavier, thirty of which seemed to be her tits. The encounter rapidly turned from a dance to something more.
      The girl motioned to someone behind her that Paige couldn't see, initially.
      She found herself bookended by two gorgeous women that night. The friend of the chesty blonde was tall and willowy, with an ethereal wispiness about her, and short red hair.
      Eventually, the three sat down for drinks in a back booth, where the music was a decibel lower, and was almost conducive to conversation.
      The short blonde, it turned out, was named Eve, and the tall, slinky redhead was Pam. They tried to talk as best they could, but after a round of drinks, decided to go outside.
      Pam and Eve held hands and kissed, taking pains to not make Paige to feel like a third wheel.
"Do y'all get high?" Paige asked.
      "Doesn't everyone?" Eve winked. Eve, Paige decided, was the decision-maker of the two.
"I have a little hash at my hotel," Paige said, making the next move. "We have some dank, too," Eve replied. All were fully cognizant of
the implications.

      They hailed a cab to the Radisson. A few bowls, joints and beers from the mini-bar later, and Paige was being treated to a stirring live sex act.
      Unnoticed for a time, Paige slipped out of her outfit, and into the king-sized bed. She focused her attention on Pam, who focused on Eve, who focused on Paige.
They went through endless variants and iterations that night. Ms.
Kitty, now forgotten, fumed with jealousy.
      When she awoke the next morning, the couple had absconded, leaving her a note with a phone number. "Call us next time you visit, Eve and Pam."

Chapter 14 - Road Scholar
      The following week found Jean in possession of a PhD in Biology, and several prospects for employment out west.
      The day before he was scheduled to leave, he met a final time with Dr. Woodard. His letter of recommendation was no small part of the reason he had received such lucrative offers.
      The fact that the Douglas Project was now a raging success certainly didn't hurt. New results were coming in daily as more varied DNA sequences were input, and the number of participants on the client side grew.
      Articles began to appear in science journals hailing the group's efforts. Jean was pleased to find himself singled out in IEEE Journal and Dr. Dobbs, due to high praise for his coding efforts from his colleagues.
      Dr. Woodard, much as Maxine had, spared him from unnecessary sentimentality. "Mr. Simmons, the University of Southern California is offering you a research fellowship, at my recommendation."
      Jean was shocked. He had no idea Woodard's influence was so far- reaching. The position was so prestigious as to alter his credentials once again, and give him new bargaining power with employers.
      "I'll be keeping an eye out for you in the trades," the professor intoned, expectation in his voice.
      After an hour or more of discussion, followed by farewells, Jean left the campus biology building, never to return. He was a free agent.
      It was time to hit the road. He drove his bike to a local jeweler, and spent considerable time searching for just the right engagement ring, on which he spent a considerable amount of money. He then waited an interminable amount of time at the DMV, waiting to perform a title transfer, only to find out he required the services of a notary.
      After another two hours of work, much of which time was spent standing in line, he had put his beloved Harley in the name of one Maxine Jackson.
      He drove to Maxine's building, making sure her car was nowhere in the lot. He locked the engagement ring in the rear compartment on the bike, and walked upstairs to apartment one thousand six.
      He placed the ignition and compartment keys in an envelope along with the title of what was now her motorcycle, and included a brief note.
"Have a great senior year, and I'll see you soon.

Love, Jean.
P.S. Check the trunk."
      It took some doing, but he managed to slide the envelope fully under her door. He lingered a few moments longer, and then walked back to his waiting vehicle, which was parked, fueled and ready to go.
      On a whim, he decided to track down Jimbrowski to say good-bye. He eventually found him in the union drinking lattes with other recent
M.A.s and Ph.D.s.
      J.B. saw him coming and leapt up to intercept him away from the table where his new, non-mutual friends sat.
"Beezer, I just wanted to say good-bye. I'm out of Alabama for

good."


"I can't say I'll miss you, Jeaner," he replied, half seriously.
J.B. stuck out his hand, and Jean accepted it, shaking it with firm

deliberateness.
"Well," Jean said, "look me up when you finally decide to come out
west."
"If I ever do, I will." James replied. "But Florida has a Disney
theme park, too. I'll probably retire here. Too much family for me to move."
"Fuck Florida," Jean said, grinning.
      "Yeah, pretty much," was Jim's final word on the matter. With that sentiment hanging in the air, the two former partners departed, ending a four-year working relationship that bore much fruit, little of it bitter.
      Jean began his trek down south, driving below or at the speed limit, in no hurry at all. He found he was becoming less impulsive now that he was no longer smoking pot, and the THC slowly left his blood stream.
      His coffee intake, however, was way up. He made a note to try and cut back on that, as well.
      He meandered down to the coast. I-10 was quicker and he'd have to take it eventually, but for now he wanted to drive the scenic route and enjoy his newly-won freedom.
      He reached the ocean as the sun was setting, and was treated to a spectacular view as the sun dipped below the horizon to his right.
      Briefly Jean wondered why he'd never taken time off for a trip to the coast before. He'd been so engrossed in his work, his head was up his

ass, he thought.
      Rather than recharge with coffee and press on, he opted to stop for the night. He checked into some Mom and Pop motel near the coast, and gave no wake up call.
      The next morning, Jean awoke at nearly ten thirty, checked out, and had a huge breakfast at the Huddle House restaurant nearby.
      It was such a nice day he spent an hour walking along the beach after he'd eaten, until the noonday sun began to get the best of him.
      The humidity and heat of the Deep South were as oppressive as the politics, and Jean was glad he had the sanctuary of the Charger windows and air conditioning. On the bike, he would have had to travel at night, he thought.
      Outside of Waveland, Mississippi, his attention was drawn to a motorcycle cop in the grassy median dividing the lanes of the coastal highway.
      As he turned to look, memories of his near arrest rekindled, he saw a Charger passing in the opposite direction. The body paint was the same color as his, driven by a woman whose hair matched it perfectly.
      Jean's emotions swirled with thoughts of Maxine, and his deep- seated loathing of police and their overlords. Cops weren't fully to blame, he knew. The real evils were committed in voting booths and legislative sessions nationwide.
      Sometimes Jean felt a dictatorship would be more honest and easier to deal with. Then, you'd more or less have a single person to blame, instead of a nebulous, many-headed hydra.
You'd also have a single enemy to kill.
      He turned north past Waveland and intersected with I-10, the artery that ran all the way to the West Coast.
      Louisiana roads were notoriously bad, and he wanted to minimize the distance traveled there. By sticking to I-10, he traveled in a straight line across the state, on the best roadway they had to offer.
      The interstate, particularly from Texas to Florida, was also a primary drug smuggling corridor, he knew, and was glad to not be holding for what felt like the first time since high school.
      Jean was tempted to stay the night in Lafayette and look up an FSU alumni he knew from the list who taught at UCC. Dixie Flatline was his

online 'nym. But instead, he pressed on all the way across the rest of the state, to Lake Charles, for the night.
      He knew if he and Dix got together, there'd be drinking and revelry, and Jean would probably get high, as well. The last thing Jean needed, at this point was to blow his detox efforts, or worse yet, get arrested in a foreign town for disorderly conduct.
      The next two days found Jean in Texas, where the heat and lack of variety in the scenery would have driven him mad, were it not for the audio books he'd downloaded and burned to CD.
      He was in awe at the prescience of Philip K. Dick, as he listened to 'A Scanner Darkly,' a dark, cautionary tale of drugs and police state machinations.
      Then he listened to Dick's 'Ubik,' which was about a man whose life seemed to be a dream, until he learned he was already dead, and was preserved on to the inside of a computer.
      Again, Jean was struck by PKD's foresight. The notion that we existed in a simulation was only recently explored academically, although it was a popular theme among some sci-fi authors. The writer of the paper he'd read placed the chances we were in a simulation at twenty percent.
      Past San Antonio, Jean listened to William Gibsons's 'Neuromancer' with guilt, having never read the classic, proto-cyberpunk novel in which Gibson coined the term 'cyberspace'.
      When he learned Dixie Flatline was a character in the book, a flatline construct, or a dead guy in a computer, he thought the chance of being in a simulation ranged from forty to eighty percent.

Chapter 15 - Arrested Development
      Having sated her need for carnality, Paige was free to explore the city without distraction. She began her day, once again, at the Caf�' DuMonde. New Orleans wasn't like Istanbul at all, she realized. It could only be France.
      The French, she knew, shared the same attitude toward work, and leisure, the same love of all things gastric and sensual. And they had left their indelible imprint on the city some three hundred years ago.
      Further up the street was a farmer's market. In New Orleans it was The Farmer's Market. It had the odd feel of a train station without trains. Virtually everything you can hope to find was on display for sale.
      There were small mountains of fresh fruit and vegetables, and Paige made note to stock up for her trip in the morning. Vendors had fresh shrimp, catfish and boiled crawfish, which Paige decided to try at lunch. They looked like tiny lobsters.
      There were Rastas with purple ribbons in their flowing dreadlocks, selling bolts of imported fabrics, silks, hemps, Kente' cloth. She approached a hippie chick in her late twenties selling beautiful pieces of handmade jewelry, and Paige knew she'd have to buy something.
      "Are you finding everything you need here in New Orleans?" the girl asked knowingly, glancing sideways at her apparent boyfriend, who sat disinterestedly reading an underground comic, looking like nothing so much as an updated Maynerd G. Krebbs.
      Paige knew instantly that the two did a little dealing on the side to supplement their income.
      "Yeah, I'm good, thanks," she said. "I'd like to hear about your jewelry, though."
      The girl's expression brightened, somewhat. The favorite subject of any artist was their art, an extension of themselves.
      "Well," she began, "These are all hand-made pieces, I do them all myself. The beads and whirls are made of copper from Argentina. I use hemp cords to string them so they're nearly impossible to break. This is amethyst, which is mined in North Carolina."
      Paige picked up and admired the light purple crystal as she continued. "These are jade, from the Orient, both China and Japan. The rough-hewn stones here are actually opal fragments, also from Japan."

      The jewelry was quite a change of pace from the gaudy diamond, gold and platinum trends that dominated the bigger world markets. She looked at the hand-labeled tags attached to the necklaces, and saw they were horrendously under-priced, with each selling for one quarter of what they would on the west coast, or less.
      "What about this section?" Paige asked indicating a few pieces off to the far left of the display table.
      "These," she said lifting one up to the light for Paige to admire, "are amber."
      The Louisiana morning sun was soft, and caused the bauble to glow a light yellow-brown.
"It's beautiful," Paige remarked.
      "Amber is crystallized prehistoric tree sap," the girl said. "They're a little more expensive because they have scientific value. Museums like to sell the ones with insects in them, if they don't put them on display."
      Paige remembered this from Jurassic Park, the Spielberg movie. She eyed the ones on the table, which seemed to be insect-free. "Do you have any like that?"
      "Well...actually," the girl said as she reached into a box beneath the fabric-covered table. "I do have one. I was thinking about keeping it. But you're pretty cool, and our rent is due."
      She handed Paige a necklace with a large, tear drop-shaped piece of amber hanging down at the bottom of the loop.
"I'm afraid it's forty dollars, though."
      Paige held it up to the light to reveal a tiny June bug encased in the dark, honey-colored stone. "Wow!" she said, duly impressed. "I'd love to have that. Are you sure it's for sale?"
      "Sure," the girl said. "I have a collection of them at home. Want me to put it on you?"
      Paige turned around and lifted her hair. The amber rode perfectly, right above her modest cleavage.
"Thanks a lot," Paige said sincerely when she turned back around.
She reached into her purse and produced a hundred dollar bill.
      "Oh, it's sort of early. I don't have much change, yet," the girl said upon seeing this.
"It's ok," was Paige's reply. "Keep it."

      The gratitude on the girl's face was heart-wrenching. "I can't do that," she said, "Let me go break it at the fruit stand."
"Keep it," Paige said more insistently.
      The girl said nothing, but looked at Paige with wonderment and gratefulness for a moment. "It looks great on you," she finally said.
      "I know," Paige said and smiled. "Thanks a lot. You two be careful." With that, she turned and continued her stroll through the market, happy to have fed a starving artist.
      Eventually, Paige's hunger got the best of her, and she headed across the street to Margaritaville, Jimmy Buffet's restaurant and bar right off of the quarter.
      Before she got to the bar, she knew she'd be having crawfish. "What's a good Louisiana drink?" she asked to bartender.
      "Hmmm," he said, apparently entirely used to questions posed out of the blue. "There's hurricanes and margaritas, of course. A bit fruity, as a lunchtime drink goes."
"Right," Paige said, "I'll have none of that," smiling. "There's the stereotypical mint julep, Scarlett," he said. "How clich�."
      "But, personally, I think the most authentic drink that summarizes the Louisiana experience, currently, would be the Abita Beers. Turbodog, maybe. There's a seasonal strawberry beer that I try to keep on hand for select clientele, there's Purple Haze..."
"That one!" Paige interjected. She handed the bartender a ten-dollar
bill.
"The lady knows what she likes," he said, returning a five across the
bar along with her opened bottle of beer.
      "Delicious!" she said after tasting it. "Can I get crawfish here?" Paige asked.
      "Can you? You can get five pounds of the biggest, tastiest crawfish in the state here. Spicy or very damn spicy?"
"Um, let's try spicy."
      "Let's get you a table, this could be messy," he said. Paige left the bill on the bar as the bartender led her to a prime seat where she could observe the activity on the street, and also people-watch the bar and restaurant patrons.
"I'll hook you up, my name's Chris."

      He was back in a flash, with another bottle of Abita. "This one's on me," he said demurely, and returned to his station.
      Within ten minutes or so, a waitress appeared with a mountainous platter of steaming crawfish, dotted with half ears of corn and boiled baby potatoes.
      "Y'all ever ate crawfish before?" the server asked. Paige looked around to make sure she was seated alone.
"No," she said, "but there's a first time for everything."
"Well, put yer napkin in yore lap. Break the tail off an peel it.
There's a little mud vein, don't eat that."
Paige realized she was referring to the creature's excretory system. "Then suck the juice out of the head. Put the shells on this other
platter. And enjoy."
      The harried waitress hurried off, as the lunch crowd was coming fast and furious.
      She tried to be neat about it at first, demurely breaking them open slowly, being careful not to make a mess. But soon enough, she had juice dripping from her hands, and it was all she could do to keep her chin clean, and prevent it from dropping into her lap.
      They did have the texture of lobster, and might even taste similar, except for the heavy taste of red pepper, bay leaves and onions, flavors that she wouldn't ordinarily consider applying to lobster. At any rate, they were delicious, and Paige ate almost all of them, and all of the corn and potatoes.
      Her favorite part was the fat surrounding the vein, and the savory mouthful of meat she got from the biggest ones. A girl could get used to this, she thought.
      She had to go to the restroom to clean up when she was finished. When she came back, her table was cleaned, and a hot cup of coffee, cream, and sugar awaited her. Louisiana folk sure were hospitable, it occurred to her.
      She sipped her coffee and considered the rest of her day. She decided to take a carriage ride, which was really a mini-tour, and get some ideas that way.
      She left a generous gratuity for the waitress, and after she'd paid her bill, went back to see Chris, the bartender.
"How was it?" he asked earnestly.
"Great, Chris, thanks so much for everything."

"Well, I'm glad you liked it," he said
      "And I wanted to thank you for the beer, too," she said, "That's some spicy stuff."
      "It was my pleasure, ma'am," he replied, and had to turn his back to attend to a new arrival at the bar.
In California, she might have considered "ma'am" to be insulting.
Here, she knew, it was a sign of respect having little to do with age.
      She stuck a ten in Chris's hurricane glass tip jar, and slipped away unnoticed.
      She approached the line of carriages from behind and assessed them as she walked slowly by, ultimately selecting a horse and driver wearing identical top hats festooned with a single white rose.
"Can I ride up front?" she asked.
      "Well," the carriage driver said, "I reckon I can suspend my prohibition for the interim you are in my presence, if you'll reveal to me the moniker by which you are known."
      "Paige," she said, "Paige Burner." And she knew she had found the driver for her. He reached his hand down and more or less pulled her up into the buggy's front seat.
"Where to?" he asked.
"You're supposed to tell me, I thought."
      "Perhaps, perhaps. But, multifaceted man that I am, I temper verbose dissertations on local history and geography with an expensive taxi service. That is to say, you can pay me extra to remain silent and ferry you to the destination of your choice. Within reason, of course. A sliding scale, both monetary and verbal, if you will."
      "I can't imagine anyone not wanting to hear you. You speak poetically," Paige said. "I just want an interesting, off-beat tour. But ending at the Radisson would be a bonus."
      "Smart lady," he said. Both inquisitive and pragmatic. And, fortunately for you, I do accept flattery as a gratuity."
      The driver, who she came to know as Dennis, brought her back and forth through various side streets surrounding the Quarter for over an hour. He kept up a running monologue that was part history lesson, part geography lesson and partly a philosophical treatise on existentialism.
      He seemed to know everything about everything, pointing out former and current brothels, notorious murder sites, both past and present.

Paige developed a sense of the politics of the area, from the 1800s onward. One fascinating aspect of his monologue was the dynamiting of the levee south of New Orleans in 1928, and the parallels between it and the Katrina disaster.
      When finally he arrived at her hotel, Paige felt she'd taken a college course on the city, conducted by an eclectic professor.
"What is there to do tonight that might interest me?" Paige asked. "Hmmm," he looked at Paige, as if assaying her. "I'd say the zoo
and aquarium, ordinarily, as you're obviously a woman of taste and culture, no doubt with a fond appreciation for animal life."
"I once wanted to be an oceanographer," she said.
      The driver ignored this, as if he needed no confirmation as to his accuracy.
      "However, you have a wild streak in you, a deep, yet hidden thrill- seeking nature. So, I could recommend the pedestrian House of Blues, or the historic Storyville Jazz Hall. In this particular instance, I invite you kill two birds with one stone and compensate me by way of accompanying me to a musical venue named Tipitina's tonight."
This request took Paige by surprise and she had to consider it.
Finally, she capitulated to his charm, wit and obvious intelligence.
"That sounds fun," she said. "Who's playing? No wait. Surprise
me."
"Oh, I expect you'll be surprised," he said. "I'll pick you up here,
out front, at eight sharp."
      He got down and helped Paige from the carriage, his large hand again engulfing her own.
      "Until then, madam," he said, and then signaled for his horse to begin moving again.
      Enchanted, she went up to her room, imbibed part of her dwindling stash of hashish, and pulled the neglected Ms. Kitty from her suitcase storage pocket.
      Otherwise, Paige knew, she's be going home with a strange man, breaking one of her cardinal rules.

Chapter 16 - California, Ho!
The drive was altogether more enjoyable once Jean got out of Texas.
The entire state bored him to tears, and seemed to have nothing to offer.
      New Mexico, with its many Indian reservations and famous military installations, held slightly more interest. He bought turquoise jewelry to send to Maxxy, and wished he could visit White Sands Proving Grounds and the base at Dulce.
      Dulce, New Mexico was long rumored to house a military installation that extended some seven stories or more underground, and was supposedly home to alien lifeforms.
      He did detour to Roswell, but was disappointed to find only cheesy gift shops and alien-themed diners, for the most part.
      There were multiple museums there, each purporting to tell the truth about the 1941 incident that occurred there.
      One was clearly an exercise in disinformation, but another held intriguing details Jean didn't previously know.
      He did find it odd that, Pre-Roswell, technology was mired at the vacuum tube level, and soon after, graduated to semiconductors and integrated circuits.
      Arizona was much more to his liking. He drove through the Joshua National Forest, which was entirely unlike any other forest in the world, filled with spiny Yucca plants instead of pine trees or redwoods.
      He visited the Great Meteor Crater, and was in awe of the mile-wide hole in the earth, and at the force and volume the space debris which created it must have held. Another meteor like that in modern times would spell disaster, he knew.
      He spent much of his time in Arizona at the Petrified Forest, and the Grand Canyon, which lay beyond it. He took a burro ride to the bottom one day, and a helicopter ride above it the next.
      When he felt he'd seen enough, he set out on his way again. He had also wanted to see Hoover Dam, but he knew he'd done enough sidetracking and sightseeing for this trip.
      California seemed stolid and pedestrian by comparison, with its identical towns and endless strip malls. What character it once possessed was plastered over with population growth and commerciality.
      Jean had job interviews up and down the coast, but he first made his way to the prestigious University of Southern California and accepted his

fellowship. It was a great honor, although there was no actual ceremony involved, as he had hoped.
      The details of being made a research fellow amounted to complete access to all of the universities facilities, and a monthly stipend of eight hundred dollars. In return, Jean's findings would be associated with the school.
      It wasn't quite as sweet of a deal as Benoit Mandelbrot once got from IBM, but a great honor, nevertheless. It would certainly help with living expenses until he took a position with one of the biotech firms currently courting him.
      He spent the day exploring the campus, and then decided to call Maxine.
"Jean! What took you so long to call?"
      "I'm sorry Maxxy. I did miss you. I was just on the road. How's the break going?"
"You've got a lot of nerve dropping that bike on me, you know." "Awww, the motorcycle was just a box for the engagement ring. A
delivery medium, to let you know I'm serious," he said.
      "Oh, you're serious, all right. Seriously crazy. I love it, though. It's a part of you here with me. And the ring, too."
"Does that mean you accept my proposal?"
      "Accept? Of course I do. I didn't think there was any question. I'm scared to ride the bike, but I'm not afraid of commitment."
      Jean's last traces of self-doubt concerning Maxine melted away as they spoke. He felt he'd gambled and won. They talked for nearly two hours, and finally agreed to call each other weekly for the duration of their separation, also making plans for Maxine to fly out next semester break.
      He traveled up the coast, starting in San Diego and venturing as far north as Bakersfield, which he felt was a desolate hellhole.
      Ultimately, he settled on D-Generation, a cutting edge outfit within an hour's drive of USC and L.A. They were hip, with a campus spirit in line with the best dotcoms in their heyday.
      They were interested in Jean's PhD research, and software development skills, in that order. He pretty much had free reign to pursue second and third level encoding, provided he kept the firm updated on his findings. His primary function, otherwise, was to oversee software development, with an emphasis on architecture concerns.

      Software engineering was an expensive process, and there was a steep curve associated with making changes or mistakes. The later changes were made or architecture mistakes were detected, the associated costs increased exponentially.
      So the company found Jean uniquely suited for the task, with his dual disciplines and track record of success in both fields, genetics and software development.
      Jean just liked the unrestricted lab access, and the relative freedom he had to conduct research. Between D-Generation and USC, he had everything a genome researcher could ask for. And he intended to use it.
      When Jean had settled in, both at work and at the university, which he visited nearly every weekend, he decided to visit Maxine's uncle. He eventually realized that drug tests in California were mainly for construction workers and people on probation.
      Half of his co-workers seemed to come to work lit, and took extended breaks. Being the boss to some of them, Jean didn't mingle or fraternize much, but was well-received, as many D-Gen employees were familiar with his work on Project Douglas.
      California's freeways were bad enough, but the inner city of L.A. was a harrowing experience. Parts of it were actually unmapped on his GPS unit, and he quickly realized why.
      Jean had always thought they were exaggerations, but L.A. ghettos really did look like a Snoop Dogg video. Sections fell to either blue or red plaid bandana-clad youth. Graffiti covered every available surface of houses, buildings, billboards and freeway walls.
      He was glad he came during the daylight hours, and even then, he drew stares and mean looks.
      Jean parked on the street in front of the house at the address given and knocked on the door. Maxine had a firm prohibition against talking on the phone where illegal business was concerned, and made it clear to Jean that this extended to her uncle, and to Jean himself, for that matter.
      A tall, gaunt black male of about thirty-five opened the door and ushered Jean inside.
"Mr. Pookie?" Jean said, feeling somewhat silly at the utterance,
"I'm-"
"Jean Simons. I know all about you. If you ever hurt my niece,
you'll get smoked, straight up." He looked Jean in the eyes, but Jean could

hear the seriousness in his voice.
      "Other than that, nice to meet you," he said, suddenly disarming and charming. "If Maxine loves you, you're fam. So what do you need?"
      "Well, I really need about a pound of hydro, so I don't have to bother you often." In reality, Jean was intimidated by both Pookie and South Central.
      "Indo, done deal. It'll be eight bills." This was four hundred less than Jean had ever paid in Alabama.
      Jean pulled out his wallet and put eight hundred dollar bills on Pook's coffee table, which Pookie ignored.
"Unlock your car," he said.
      Jean went to the window and chirped the alarm. Pookie dialed a number on his cell phone. "One pizza, extra crispy," was all he said into it, and hung up.
"Anything else?" Pookie asked.
"Well," Jean began, "There's this rainforest drug, it's an odd request.
But it's called ayahuasca, and it-"
      "It's pronounced aya-hooaska," Pookie interjected. "Celebrities like Sting and Tori Amos have taken guided trips on it in L.A. in the past year."
Jean couldn't help but look surprised, and Pook couldn't help but
notice.
"I live in the hood, but I'm not ignorant," he said. "I mean most of
these cats are, but I read." He pointed to an electronic bracelet on his ankle. "I have lots of spare time. I can get you some, actually. I never tried it. Tripping in the hood can be fatal. But give me two weeks. I'll have some people drop it off with you."
Jean reached to get his wallet back out, saying, "Wow! Thanks a
lot."
"Keep it," Pookie said. "This is on me. Call it an engagement
present."
Jean had no idea Maxine had told her family they planned to wed. "I'm really in love with Maxxy," Jean said, "I would never hurt

her."

fool."

"You best not," Pookie said, grinning, "That girl is a straight up "So how and when do I get the herb?" Jean asked.
"You already got it, my man. Drive safely."

"Thanks for everything, Pookie," Jean said, finally relaxing. "Anytime, cuz. But I do wish you didn't bring that red-ass car to my
house."
"Heh, I hear you." Jean said.
      There was an awkward exchange where Pookie extended his hand for a handshake, and Jean made a fist to give him a pound, and then each did the opposite.
      When they'd worked out these formalities, Jean was out the door, anxious to get home safely and smoke for the first time in nearly a month.
      Jean didn't speed, but neither did he dawdle. When he got home, the weed was the brightest green, stickiest herb he had ever encountered.
      A few weeks later, there was a break-through with the Douglas Project. Third layer encoding patterns were believed to have been detected. Jean went to his USC research lab to closely examine the latest second layer findings.
      When he was leaving campus that evening, he was approached by two well-dressed and immaculately groomed young black men, both as big as line backers.
      "Have you accepted Jesus Christ as you personal savior?" they asked in unison. Jean assumed they were Jehovah's witnesses.
"No, thanks," Jean answered.
One thrust a Bible into his hands, and they kept going past him. "Read it in the car, Pookie said," one of them called over his
shoulder.
      When Jean got in the Charger and sat down, he opened the leather bound Bible. The center pages had a square cut out of them, and inside was some dried roots in a Ziploc baggie, and two pages of instructions from Pookie on preparation and usage.

Chapter 17 - Rock for Light
      Paige napped, and when she awoke, showered and prepared for her final evening on the town in the Crescent City. She dressed conservatively, another shield to protect herself from the presumed wanton charms of the handsome stranger.
      She hadn't come to New Orleans to be swept off her feet, and so she simply resolved not to let it happen.
      By seven fifty-five, she was curbside, and soon heard the familiar clip-clopping	of	Rosie	O'Donnell,	Dennis's	workhorse.	She	hadn't anticipated him picking her up in his carriage, but neither was she surprised.
      Ever the gentlemen, he again got down and rendered her needless assistance with mounting the chassis of his conveyance.
      "So," she said as they turned off of the boulevard upon which her current abode resided, "Tipitina's, huh? I never heard of it."
      "You won't soon forget it," Dennis said. "You'll need this, I'd expect," and fired up a bomber joint of what Paige quickly determined to be Jamaican collie bud. He handed it to her without consulting her, or even looking at her, ever confident in his choices and actions.
Paige accepted it with the same surety of action.
      The twenty minute ride left Paige feeling quite nice, and they parked Rosie behind a two-storey nightclub near the levee, ringed with an eclectic looking crowd milling about outside. Paige saw dreads, mohawks, longhaired metal types, GQ college boys and even businessmen. Her interest was piqued.
"Aren't you afraid someone will bother or steal her?" Paige asked. "People know us, here," he said simply. "This is not Chicago. The
city is sort of an extended family." He looked at his watch as they walked in, nodding at the doorman. "Oh, good, the opening act is almost done."
Paige noted that Dennis didn't have to pay the cover charge or wait
in line.
The band on stage were white kids who played competently enough,
but were entirely derivative and unoriginal. She heard strains of Living Colour, the Pepper's, 24/7 Spyz and similar bands in their supposed original compositions.
      She understood why Dennis skipped their act. To be influenced was one thing, but these kids borrowed liberally, to put it kindly.

      "Come on," Dennis said, after buying Paige a bottle of Haze. "I want you to meet some people."
      He led her backstage to a dressing room that was a microcosm of the crowd outside, but with a heavier emphasis on the Rastas, about eleven people in all.
      "Darryl!" Dennis said, shaking the man's hand, and then offering a quick embrace. "How's the tour?"
      Paige expected a Jamaican patois to spill forth from the man's lips, and was surprised to hear the diction of a Yankee gentlemen.
      "It's good, man, you know? Except H.R he sort of flew off the handle and attacked the tour manager a few cities back." He nodded at a man with a slight afro sitting in the back on the room, wearing a bandage on the side of his face.
      "He sort of tried to bite his ear off," Darryl said. "No charges filed, Jah be praised."
      Dennis was nonplussed. "And where is Mr. Human Rights, at this critical juncture?" he asked.
      "He's is the can. Dr. Know is watching the show, and Earl's still at the hotel, or on his way. He's homesick. H.R. is on one of his 'no talking' kicks again."
      "That which goeth into a man defileth him not, but what comes out of a man's mouth," Dennis said.
"Exactly," Darryl said.
      At this moment, H.R. walked in, with a wide smile for Dennis. "Everyone out," the tour manager said, and the hangers-on back stage dutifully filed out.
      When they had gone, Dennis handed H.R. a brown paper-wrapped package that must have contained a kilo of weed. He threw it on a desk and cut it open, revealing the rich chocolate collie bud they had smoked earlier.
      H.R. produced a half sheet of newspaper and grabbed two fistfuls of weed. After a few practiced moments, he had the largest joint Paige had ever seen, but tapered into a cone-shape. It was a spliff of epic proportions.
      The tour manager wrapped up the remainder of the herb and left to secure it. Dennis helped H.R. to light it, and soon the air was thick with sweet, acrid smoke. H.R. appeared to swallow it as he drew on the cone, holding each hit in for what seemed like a minute or more.

Paige took several hits, but she couldn't keep up with Dennis and
H.R. She was thoroughly lit, and probably would have been so just from being in the room. She wondered if all reggae concerts were like this.
      Darryl wasn't smoking at all, and so he and Paige eventually excused themselves, leaving the two to complete their iron lung pre-show partaking.
      The opening act had finished, so people could finally hear each other speak again.
Darryl was feeling pensive and talkative. "I've got a bad feeling," he said.
"What is it?" Paige asked, concerned.
      "Not about tonight, New Orleans is always fine. It's about our new label, Maverick. As of last month, we now work for Madonna."
Paige was moderately surprised at how unlikely this seemed. "Rights has been saying she's the devil, and now maybe he's
correct. When she signed Bad Brains, we later found out she owns our names if we break our contract. That's how come my man got his ear nearly chewed off." He nodded toward their manager who was motioning for Darryl to get on stage.
      "Nice to meet you, enjoy the show, dere," he said, slipping in a tiny bit of the accent, as if to satisfy Paige's stereotype expectations. She later learned the group was from D.C and possibly Ethiopia, before that, but often visited Jamaica, where H.R. owned a home.
      Dennis emerged from backstage and found Paige. He brought her to the barricade, to the left of the elevated stage. "When the show starts, things might get a little crazy. I'll protect you, if so."
Paige had to wonder how crazy a mix of reggae fans could get.
      All at once, the band exploded, tearing into their first song with a fierce intensity. They didn't play reggae, but hardcore punk rock, with a metal edge and razor sharp precision. The crowd went wild.
      H.R. dominated the stage like a possessed lion, howling, snarling and sputtering, alternating with a most beautiful, melodic singing voice.
      Paige was treated to some of the best music she'd ever heard in her life. She thought that in a better world, the Bad Brains would be superstars, getting tons of radio play. Instead, they performed for five hundred people or so, each of which seemed to be die-hard fans.

      Devil or not, Paige thought, Madonna was extremely shrewd to sign them. She felt like she was watching history in the making.
      The crowd in front of the stage grew raucous, slamming and pogoing, but with none of the viciousness that often accompanied punk shows. When the sea of fans got too rough Dennis held the barricade with both hands on either side of Paige, keeping her from being crushed.
      Eventually, the band took it down several notches, and actually did perform a reggae song. When they did, Paige hoped Dennis would wrap his arms around her, but ever the gentleman, he did not, instead dropping his arms back to his side.
      They finished that sonic interlude and launched back into several short, intense punk songs. Paige was amused to hear H.R. say in one song, "Give me back my marijuana, give me back my marijuana!"
      All at once, the song ended, and the band had disappeared before anyone had noticed. The audience, thoroughly worked up, wasn't going to let it end just yet, and raised a chant "Bad BRAINS, Bad BRAINS!"
Finally, the band reappeared and a roar erupted from the crowd.
      "I gwan do one more song dere," H.R. said. "Dis for Dennis and de girl wit de red hair." He pointed to the couple at stage right and another cheer burst forth from the crowd.
      They rapidly counted to four and launched into the most intense song Paige had ever heard. She later learned it was called 'Pay to Cum' and was their signature track, dating back to the early eighties.
      The fans in front of the stage went nuts, in a literal frenzy of spiritual jumping and dancing. The energy was manic and electric.
      Paige happened to look to her right and saw a riotgrrl spin around and kick a metalhead to the ground with both feet, and wondered what that was about. She suspected someone had copped a feel, and the metalhead got the blame, right or wrong.
The song was over as quickly as it started, and with it, the show.
Paige wished they'd play another hour, but it wasn't to be.
      When their ears stopped ringing, Dennis asked her if she'd like to party. She was still very high, and said she'd have to pass. But it was a difficult decision. Dennis was a great guy, even though she could never seriously date a dealer.
      He seemed slightly disappointed, but not overly so, and offered to bring her home. Instead, she said she'd take a cab, and Dennis called one

from his cell phone. She thanked him profusely, and gave him a tight embrace.
      "You know where to find me," he said finally. "I'm always there." And with that, he was off to the night's party.
      Paige rode to the hotel in contemplative silence. She was leaving in the morning, and asked the driver to take her across the bridge and back before dropping her at the Radisson.
      The river at night was beautiful, of course, and the lack of moonlight made the lights reflecting off the water seem even brighter. When they turned back toward the city, viewed from up high, with the French quarter lit and the Superdome in the backdrop, Paige felt a twinge of regret at leaving.
      But she'd had a great time, and fresh adventures awaited. One thing she still did not miss was California and work. She'd hardly thought about it at all since she'd landed at the airport.
      She knew that would change, of course. But for now she had one foot in the Big Easy, and another out the door. She didn't have a firm itinerary, but she did have her next destination in mind.
      She crashed hard when she hit the bed, and huddled under the comforter for warmth and security, dreaming of settling down, some place, with someone. Her dreams were as vague as her future plans.
      In the morning, she got up, showered, and packed her bags, prepared for the next leg of her journey, which would be lengthy.
      She checked out, and drove her rental car to Caf� Du Monde, where she had two coffees, this time, and ate her fill of fresh beignets. Then she ordered a large cup to go, and another dozen beignets for the road.
      She knew she'd pay the price for drinking so much coffee when she was due to drive, but she couldn't help herself.
      She'd half hoped she would see Dennis and Rosie one last time before she left, but realized that it was far to early for that.
      Paige drove away slowly, turning over her newly acquired memories of New Orleans in her head. They were sweet, with only a faint twinge of undefined regret tainting them. All in all, a great vacation so far, she thought.
      She got on the elevated interstate heading east. At Waveland, Mississippi's outskirts, she passed a Charger that was the same color as

hers, moving in the opposite direction as she, and had the oddest sensation of both coming and going.
      Maybe it was from the Collie bud last night, and from finishing off the hash this morning, she thought. She made a mental note to start acquiring varieties of hash to add to her collection of smokables.
      When she's passed through Mississippi and was nearly through Alabama, she saw road signs announcing that Florida lay ahead. She checked her GPS and found an artery headed north to Auburn. Fuck Florida, she thought, listening to the copy of Bad Brains' 'I Against I' that she'd bought at Tower Records.

Chapter 18 - Revelation
      Jean's USC visits were primarily focused on obtaining specific DNA sequences from the archives of the biology and psychology departments. His research fellow status accorded him access that no grad student and few professors could match.
      From the biology department, he obtained a full set of samples of the major racial phenotypes: black, white, Asian, Indian, Indian, Sur- American, Pan-European, and Slavic. He didn't intend to discriminate, and instead sought a commonality that would insure inclusiveness.
      The psychology department contained a wealth of samples pertinent to his research. A few weekends of study resulted in obtaining samples from psychopaths, both overt and covert. The overt psychopaths, sociopaths, had publicly exhibited symptoms of psychopathy, generally violent criminal behavior.
