      The covert psychopaths were more difficult to discern, but Jean became something of an expert in what he considered investigative ponerology. To find examples, he used social demographics in conjunction with psychological profiles.
      He began correlating likely candidates from the psychology pool against social strata, status, and their past and present achievements. Most of the samples the school possessed were of former students.
      The rest were current students, and extreme cases of the criminal mind. A few were actually famous, including Kenneth Bianchi, one of the Hillside Strangler duo, and an anonymous sample long believed to be from San Francisco's Zodiac killer.
      Jean patiently recorded the DNA sequences of those he considered most likely to be psychopaths, and began a private database of sequences, which he intended to use in his own version of Project Douglas. He had obtained the full source code for the most current revision, with one eye on modifying it for his own purposes.
      The covert psychopaths were difficult to suss out, and often required judgment calls on his part. It involved selecting candidates who seemed to exhibit a lack of human emotion, according to their psychological case studies.
      Jean began to compare these candidates based on their real-world achievements and family backgrounds. It was in part also based on social strata.

      The operating premise was that psychopaths tended to be upwardly mobile and were descended from other upwardly mobile psychopaths, and it was certainly supported by the data. It was made slightly more difficult to pinpoint them because very few USC students came from poor families.
      There was a saying, "Behind every great fortune lies a great crime." Jean focused on those he determined to be the wealthiest, most driven of the families and offspring.
      From there, where possible, he followed up on this by checking the latest achievements of related graduates, and even a few current students attending USC.
      Eventually, a pattern began to form. He was accumulating a DNA database of those most likely to possess what he considered to be the fascist gene.
      The next Saturday after acquiring the ayahausca, an announcement went out on the Douglas listserv. Not only had the second layer of DNA sequencing produced quantifiable results, there was now solid evidence indicating third layer encoding.
      The news rocked the world of human genome research, and spread beyond the secretive world of genetics research and began creeping into popular culture. The members of Team Douglas were hailed, individually and collectively, as heroes. Jean's name appeared alongside others in Time magazine.
      Jean thought about his DMT experience, and what it showed him about the final, fourth layer of encoding.
      That particular revelation, which he had revealed to absolutely no one, led Jean's private research to some extremely interesting tangents which he intended to explore at a later date, once he had isolated the root cause of authoritarian impulses.
      There was a chance many of us were at least partial aliens. Many of these thoughts were particularly difficult for Jean to swallow, because they were in stark contrast to his own personal beliefs and values.
      First of all, he'd never put much stock in the idea that the Earth has been visited by extraterrestrials. Scientifically, he understood it was certainly possible, even one day inevitable.
      But space was so vast and empty, he considered interstellar flight to be a very unlikely prospect. That left the most likely mode of travel to be interdimensional, which he was even more incredulous of.

      He understood that string theory, which he regarded as the current best model of explaining the structure of our universe, only worked mathematically if one presumed the existence of either eleven or twenty- seven dimensions.
      He could intellectualize that notion, but found it very difficult to accept on a personal level. Quantum physics, of which he had only a passing familiarity, smacked of magic, or the divine, to Jean.
      Which led to his next mental stumbling block, religion. Jean had not only considered himself and objective realist, but an atheist. And some of the best evidence regarding our possible extraterrestrial origins came from the Bible.
Worse yet, there was a definite racial slant to the theory.
      The earliest writings to support this notion came from Ezekiel and Genesis. The book of Ezekiel detailed what seemed to be an early UFO sighting, during the pre-Christian era of the Old Testament. In it, Ezekiel describes a wheel within a wheel in the sky, made of colored lights.
      Better yet, Genesis made reference to the Nephilim, or Watchers. Considered to be angels in ancient times, the Nephilim were described as ten feet tall, and possessing awesome powers, far exceeding those of man.
      Aliens, in other words. And they lusted after and bred with human women.
      If one looks at the many references to angels in the Bible in this light, an entirely new perspective is gained. Perhaps the alleged healing powers of Christ himself indicated he was a descendant of the hybrid bloodline.
      It was very difficult for Jean to accept, but the data was beginning to mount. Only his DMT trip forced him to seriously consider it. What made it even harder for him to accept was his thorough understanding of mitochondrial DNA.
      Jean knew that homo sapiens originated in Africa, and then dispersed east through Yemen, finally heading in all directions around the globe, and acquiring the genetic differences we know today as race.
      The Nephilim theory, supported in his mind by his glimpse at the forth layer encoding, meant that Africans were the original habitants of the planet, and interbred with more or less whites from another planet.
      To Jean's anti-racist mind, the concept was quite unsettling. Then again, skin color wasn't what defined our differences, but rather the specific

DNA sequences we possessed.
      Skin color was quite misleading, as many people are superficially one race or another, but their DNA reveals them to be primarily composed of genes of a different race entirely.
      The extraterrestrial angle complicated everything entirely too much, in Jean's mind. Race and genetics, the product of evolution, made more sense without this additional complexity.
      Yet the evidence existed, despite Jean's personal beliefs. In science, you went where the data led you.
      Black Muslims in America, in part, cited legends of an evil black scientist, Yacub, who created whites in a lab as an experiment.
      Jean countered all of these thoughts with his knowledge of the historical religious texts of ancient India.
      The ancient vedic books spoke of great wars in Earth's ancient history, involving flying machines, energy weapons akin to lasers, and even thermonuclear weapons.
      There are even great fields of green glass sheets dotting the planet that could possibly be the result of nuclear explosions.
      And this was a primary argument against interstellar travel, light speed considerations aside. Civilizations are always more likely to destroy themselves via nuclear wars, viruses, or to die from a comet or meteor impact than to develop the ability to travel the stars, much less interdimensionally.
      In some strange way, this thought was more comforting to Jean. But it was a superficial thought. In more or less infinite space and time, everything that was possible would happen eventually. Or had already happened.
      There were other complications that seemed to support the notion that we had destroyed ourselves before. Fringe science cited evidence of modern man one hundred and even five hundred million years ago. Human footprints appeared in the same strata with dinosaur footprints, according to some.
      In other words, it was at least as likely that different races, once having a common origin, evolved into vastly different beings, and then again interbred.
      One example of this was the fact that people with red hair carried portions of Neanderthal DNA within them. They were essentially another

race unto themselves.
      We were all mutts, at this point, that it scarcely mattered. Even Murray and Herrnstein's The Bell Curve, a treatise on race and intelligence considered to be racist by some, was tempered by details such as the fact that twenty percent of blacks were smarter than eighty percent of whites in America.
      Race, and skin color in particular was not the best indicator of ability, in other words. If more people understood genetics, Jean felt, racism would cease to be a large problem, nor matter how biologically imperative it seemed to be, from a survival standpoint.
All of these complex thoughts and emotions made Jean's head hurt.
He wondered what Maxxy would think of all this.
      And then he decided to try the ayahuasca and see what secrets it held for him.
###
      Maxine arrived home after spending the night at the apartment of two roommates she shared classes with, studying, eating pizza, and smoking out of a gravity bong.
      A gravity bong, in reality a vacuum bong, Maxxy knew, involved a bowl of weed stuck in the cap of a two-liter bottle with the bottom cut off of it.
      You put the contraption in a bucket of water, and slowly pulled it up while lighting the bowl. The resultant vacuum filled the two-liter with thick smoke. You then took the top off, place your mouth on it, and pushed the bottle back down.
      The entire two liters of smoke, or as much as you could handle, was forced into your lungs. Much more smoke than one could ordinarily inhale.
      In truth, precious little in the way of studying was achieved, but new high scores and personal bests were recorded playing 'Guitar Hero' and 'Katamari Damacy'.
      Maxxy's heart leapt when she saw the motorcycle at first, until she remembered he had already left for California by now, and that he was nowhere in sight.
      Since there were no identifying marks, and she didn't know his license plate number, Maxine chalked it up to coincidence. But she was somewhat shaken by the initial shock.

      When she entered her apartment, she failed to notice the envelope, which had been pushed behind the door when she opened it.
      A few hours later, on her way out for some Pepto-Bismol, she picked it up. It was unmarked, and heavy.
      She opened it in the sunlight on the veranda. She saw a note, a pink slip, and two keys, and understood in an instant. She read the note, and swooned, holding on to the railing for balance.
      "Tom Sawyer, how could you be so noble?" she asked, echoing Twain's Becky Thatcher.
      When Maxine went downstairs and opened the storage compartment as instructed, she found Jean's symbol of his commitment to her. Her hands went to her stomach as she vomited, and Maxxy knew with the full force of feminine intuition that she was pregnant.

Chapter 19 - Rock City
      The panhandle of Florida, Paige knew, was a snake pit, a cesspool to be avoided at all costs. Lacking the attractions of southern Florida, and the accompanying income from tourism, the non-beach towns had but one thriving industry: the jails.
      Shithole towns such as Chipley preyed upon unsuspecting travelers who made the mistake of stopping for gas with so much as a broken taillight. Many a driver on route to Orlando found themselves residents of the local correctional center, and if they somehow were unable to make bail, interred for some thirty to ninety days, easily.
      These towns were laws unto themselves, and operated far outside the bounds of the Constitution. So, fuck Florida, basically. If Paige ever went there, she'd fly into Orlando International, and leave the same way.
      She drove north through Alabama, which was beautiful, if a bit too countrified for her tastes. Farming and livestock seemed to be the main industries she could see, aside from logging.
      When she made it to Auburn, where she stayed the night, Paige visited the university the next morning before she continued on her merry way. P.J. had attended a few classes here, he told her, before dropping out and entering the police academy.
      It was a stately, grand campus, with perfect weather and an industrious atmosphere. Paige wondered what had made Peter drop out.
      But Auburn was a mere pit stop on her way to her intended destination, Tennessee.
      Fall was approaching, and the cooler temperatures and changing leaves made for most pleasant driving conditions. She made great time, and reached the state line at nightfall.
      She checked her maps, and decided that any town inside the border was a good place to stop, and was surprised to find she had to drive another sixty miles before she found a town big enough to support multiple chain hotels.
      Money was no object, but Paige was definitely not spending the night in some seedy motel in an unnamed southern town.
      The next morning, coffee and doughnuts were a let down, after New Orleans. Still, she was excited by what lie ahead.
      When Paige was a child, she had taken an extended trip with her grandparents, from Ohio to southern Georgia. Now she was going to relive

that treasured summer memory by revisiting the places she could clearly recall.
      She drove on until she reached the town containing the first attraction she wished to visit. By the time she got there, she had nearly missed the last tour of the day at three p.m., but the ticket seller radioed ahead and asked the tour guide to delay the venture by five minutes, then brought her to the cave entrance in a golf cart, instead of making her walk.
      It was a small group of eleven, plus the guide, and no one seemed to be the least put out by the fact that Paige was the cause of their delay.
      After your basic amateur spelunking safety lecture, they descended a railed staircase into the past. Around a corner, mere yards away, lay a gigantic body of water in a chilly and unlit cavern.
      The guide's running monologue was interesting but essentially unchanged from Paige's childhood. The cavern, too, was unchanged. But instead of losing allure in proportion to her gains in height, age and experience, the lake was even more enchanting than she had remembered it. In fact, she felt she scarcely appreciated it the first time she had encountered it.
      It was truly a sight to behold. Not a pool, or a pond, but an enormous lake, stretching far beyond what the eye could see, despite the aid of torches and flashlights.
      They loaded onto a flat-bottom skiff, and using a mostly silent trolling motor, glided away from the dock. When they approached a narrow-mouthed passage in the forward cave wall after a minute's travel, the guide's lights shut off at once.
      "It's ok, folks, don't panic now. Give me a second." The boat continued forward as he spoke. Some of the tourists gasped or laughed nervously at the emergency, but none but Paige seemed to know the event was contrived. As a girl, she herself had panicked, ducking between her grandparents for protection from the dark unknown.
      When the tourmaster switched the boat lights on again, people involuntarily oohed and ahhed at the spectacle. The section of lake they were now upon dwarfed the outer chamber by an order of magnitude.
      He hooked the skiff to the right, and began the circuitous trek around the perimeter. Paige noted that after twenty minutes of travel, they had yet to reach the halfway point of their journey.

      The guide stopped for a moment, and used the reverse on the motor to counteract their forward movement. He then used his high-powered flashlight to direct their attention to a spot on the cave wall, slightly above their heads, where they were standing.
      Paige did detect one deviation from her youth. The flashlight was one that used white LEDs, a technology unknown at the time of her last visit.
      There was a single date on the cave wall, oversized and drawn as if with a shaky hand. The guide explained that this was rumored to have been done via torch by the Civil War soldier who had first discovered the cave in the late 1800s.
      Rather than continuing around the edge of the lake, the guide cut left toward the entrance, dimly visible across the dark, still waters.
      One quarter of the way across, he again stopped the motor, this time letting them drift toward the center of the cavern.
"Y'all want to see the fish?" he asked suddenly.
      There was a murmur of assent. Everyone but the guide spoke in hushed, reverential tones, as though they inhabited a gothic cathedral instead of a dank hole in the ground.
      He directed them to look beneath their seats, and there was a brief flurry of activity as each took out a small bag of popcorn.
One or two patrons, began to eat theirs, fully not understanding his
intent.
Before he could direct them, Paige tossed out a big handful, and
soon the mineral rich waters, clear to a depth of three feet or so, roiled with gigantic, apparently ravenous fish.
      "These," he explained, "are rainbow trout. Some good eatin', right there. And easy to catch, as you can imagine. But these particular trout," he went on as the tourists continued to cast bread upon the waters, so to speak, "are protected by Tennessee State Law."
      "You'll note their lack of pigment, or coloration, which is where rainbow get the name. These trout are a flat monochrome. We're not in Kansas anymore."
      Paige tittered at the joke, which had escaped the others in the boat, apparently.
      "Furthermore, each of these fish, to a one, are completely blind. Over time, the lack of sunlight eliminated their need for both markings and

eyesight."
      He let this sink in, and waited until everyone had exhausted their supply of popcorn.
      When they had, and the fish once again submerged, safely away from the prop of the trolling motor, he started slowly back across the lake to the narrow passage that had previously served as the ingress.
      They tied off at the dock, carefully stepped back out of the boat and walked up the stairs by ones and twos.
      They emerged, blinking, into the sunlight, and Paige got the sensation of evolution in miniature, creatures emerging from the dark sea onto dry land, or perhaps being born again.
      More unsettling, it was as if they had journeyed from the abode of the dead, the underworld, back to the land of the living. She shivered in the warmth. Such thoughts had not possessed her when first she had experienced this simulated rebirth.
      She thanked the guide for the experience, and went off to the tour office to acquire the requisite brochures and mandatory t-shirt. It occurred to Paige that she had vacated New Orleans with neither a souvenir nor the fresh fruit she had promised herself.
It was nearing five o'clock, and she was famished.
Paige had had enough of the high life of late, and felt like being bad.
She wanted road food.
      The park ranger who ran the tour office directed her to a greasy spoon, or family-run diner, as he called it, just a few miles past her hotel.
      Paige seated herself at the diner's counter, and a middle-aged woman in a pink waitress outfit which may as well have been a costume worn by Flo on 'Alice' asked, in all seriousness, "What can I do ya for?"
In for a penny, in for a pound, Paige thought.
      "I'd like a large coke, no ice, your largest hamburger with everything and a plate of fries."
      "No prob, hun," the waitress spoke in a shorthand that indicated a lifetime of short-order cookery.
"Do you have hot dogs?" Page asked. "Shore do, shug. Want one?"
"I'd like three, with everything, please."
      The waitress raised an eyebrow at this, but said nothing. She's probably going to binge here and purge in our bathroom, she thought to

herself.
When the food arrived, it was delicious as only foods that are
terribly bad for you can be. The waitress pretended to clean, and occasionally shot glances at the cook, as she watched Paige eat every last morsel of food, down to the piece of lettuce used as garnish.
      "Want a refill, boo?" she asked, automatically reaching for the empty plastic tumbler.
      "No,	thanks,"	Paige	replied,	"I	have	to	get	going.	Big	day tomorrow."
      Despite this invitation to conversation the waitress took her at her word and totaled up her bill by hand, double- checking it.
When she handed it to Paige, she asked, "Was everything alright?" "It was great!" Paige said honestly. "Too good. I almost hurt
myself."
      "I see that," the waitress said, still amazed at the sheer volume of food this petite woman had out away.
      Paige looked at the total and marveled at the difference in economies. Her meal was fully half of what it would have cost on the West Coast.
      She settled up, overtipping, as was her habit. She had to convince her server that she meant to give a tip that was essentially the cost of the meal.
      "Y'all come back now, ya hear?" the woman said, and Paige could only think of 'Hee-Haw' and 'The Beverly Hillbillies'.
      But she had to get some rest. Tomorrow she would walk off her meal, exploring Rock City.

Chapter 20 - Revelation, Part Two
The following morning, the phone rang early, waking Jean. "Hey, superstar," a voice said. Maxine.
"Hey, love," he said in return.
"For someone in Time Magazine, you sure don't sound too excited." "Sorry, I'm still asleep."
      "I woke you?" she checked her watch, "Damn time zones. I get excited and think everyone's on my time."
      Maxine	was	brimming	with	excitement.	"Damn,	wow!	Time Magazine. My fianc�," she said. "I'm so proud."
      "Well, it's not like I did it all myself, but yeah, it is pretty cool, I think. They gave me a raise at work."
      "I can't wait to come visit you," Maxine said. "I look a little different."
      She glanced down at her stomach, which was beginning to protrude, ever so slightly. "I hope you still want me."
      "Don't be silly, Maxxy. I love you. Did you cut your hair or something?"
"Somethin' like that," she said, laughingly.
      "Well, don't worry about a thing on my end. I only have eyes for you. Among other things."
      "Oh, stop, Smooth Talker, Texas Ranger. You're making me randy baby, yeah."
      "We can't both be Randy," he said, "I'll be Frank." They both laughed uproariously at this.
      They spoke at length, and finally Maxine released him. "Well, you go on and do your mad scientist thing," she said. "I need second breakfast." "I guess I can let you go, for now" Jean said. "I love you, Maxine
Jackson. Even though I don't know your middle name."
      "Action's my middle name, baby." They laughed together. "But you can use 'Elizabeth', if you prefer, Mr. Jean Paul Simons."
"Maxine Elizabeth Simons. I like it," Jean said. "You'd better," Maxine said with a smile in her voice. "Love it," he continued.
"Gotta go, I'm-," eating for two, she almost said. "Damn hungry." "Ok, Maxxy, go head on with your bad self. Love you."
"Love you too, love. I'm so proud. Byeee..."

      Jean hung up and showered. He'd had plenty of sleep, and decided he was taking the day off. He made a perfunctory call to human resources, and he was free at last.
      After a light meal of coffee, a banana and cottage cheese, he read and re-read Pookie's instructions.
      He Googled for them, and found out that this set didn't appear online. But the preparation instructions were essentially the same as on most sites pertaining to ayahuasca, so he followed them exactly.
      He'd also read a number of experiences with the drug, so he more or less knew what to expect, even though each person experienced differing reactions, in part.
      At any rate he felt he was ready. An unguided trip could be risky, he knew, but this was personal and private. Besides, he was a veteran psychonaut.
      He drove to a park outside of USC that he'd explored and staked out in advance. It wouldn't do to be disturbed, he knew.
      Jean walked several hundred feet into a copse of trees to a small clearing he'd decided upon for the start of his journey. In his hands was a small woven blanket that he'd gotten from s Navajo reservation, and a Mason jar containing a thin brown liquid.
      He spread the fabric out and faced the sun, sitting indian style, and took a few deep breaths. Then Jean decisively downed the foul-tasting mixture in a single gulp.
      For a long while he waited, fighting the impulse to expectorate and vomit, but eventually the feeling passed, and he retained what he had ingested.
      The sun swelled and pulsated slowly above him, mimicking the rhythm of his relaxing heart. The birdsong songs began to echo and distort. For a long while, he closed his eyes and enjoyed the calming sensations.
      When next Jean opened his eyes, he was in a different world. He occupied a void, but there was not a hint of shadow extant. He sat floating in a formless sea of white light.
      He felt no physical sensations, nor did hear any natural sounds, or discern an up or down. He simply bathed in a somewhat warm glow, as though he was in a space inverse of that of the firmaments.
      Eventually, new sounds reached his ears. Whisperings, slitherings like the sound of two pieces of paper rubbing together grew louder. He

began to see movement out of the corner of his eyes.
      The universe of his birth was gone. Jean had crossed over into the invisible world of the unknown. Lumbering shapes passed here and there in the distance, uninterested in his presence.
      Smaller, ribbon-shaped creatures began to circle him, weaving up and down, entertaining each other like braids as they went, in the manner of the physician's staff of Asclepius or the structure of DNA.
      Larger, similarly shaped creatures, which took the appearance of neon-patterned moray eels, chased the swarming serpentine entities this way and that, as if to dissuade them from drawing nearer to Jean's representation on this plain of existence.
      The smaller creatures darted exuberantly above and below the forms of the larger, describing loops and whirls, coming back to nip at their tails in a playful manner.
      During this time Jean felt nothing, but watched with detached interest. Whatever would happen here couldn't hurt him physically. His mental state, however, needed to be steeled against what might come to pass in this ether which surrounded us all, ordinarily undetectable.
      He felt distant rumblings, and the sounds grew louder, until the din began to crowd his thoughts, making further analysis unfeasible.
      All at once there was a thunderous crash, and the sea of light surrounding Jean flashed black in synchrony with the noise, as if negative lightning was arcing nearby.
      A rushing roar filled his entire being, and Jean braced for the impact of what felt and sounded like a hurtling freight train.
      Another, larger crack of this inverse electrical current occurred, and the largest of the creatures Jean had seen so far came rushing toward him like psychedelic bullet. He braced himself for whatever was to come as best he could, but the entire experience was so unreal, it was like attempting to resist a dream.
      Jean made no actions that could be construed as dodging, and faced whatever inevitable fate was in store for him with resolve, neither anticipating nor dreading the coming impact.
      The speeding N-dimensional sea snake bared its needle-like fangs and its eyes flashed red, inches from his person. Jean tried to close his already closed eyes, and of course could not.

      It aimed for his pineal gland, in the recesses of his brain.For a moment, the collision seemed anti-climatic. He felt the form of the creature pass through his cranium as a divine wind, and heard the cavernous roar of a millennia of hurricanes.
During this brief period, Jean was formless.
  When his avatar returned, it was to hear and feel an awful cracking as his skull and mind was cleaved in two.
      The larger snakes were gone, leaving only the vague, whale-sized figures in the indistinct distance, which never closed the gap between them.
      The smallest swirled around him, coalescing into a schooling mass that writhed individually while navigating in concert. At once, they darted in unison away from Jean, in front of him, until they occupied the blind spot that resulted from the left and right hemisphere of his skull separating.
      He considered this, and noticed that if he strained, he could see his own back and shoulders.
      What he couldn't see were the wrasse-like creatures reversing directions and rocketing back toward him until it was too late.
      They slammed into the gap betwixt his eyes with a pain existential in nature. Humanity and supernaturality collided in a fusion that changed his very being forever.
      His mind squirmed in a most unsettling manner, but he fervently fought the impulse to dislodge these otherworldly parasites with every ounce of his resolve, lest he despoil the gift of the god Ayahuasca.
      As Jean gained control of his natural revulsion at this occupation, the two halves of his mind slowly and deliberately began to move back toward each other. As they did, the discomfiture he experienced as host began to subside.
      When he was again of one mind, sight restored, the unnatural feelings were replaced with a deep inner calm.
      He found himself now entirely alone. The void around him began to slowly cycle through sixteen point seven million colors, moving through the color wheel from white to black.
      When he found himself in an inky sea, darker than the deepest black, Jean felt his travel was nearing its end.
      Ponderously, ever so slowly, the darkness began to recede. Jean began to discern trees, birds, and flitting insects, but bathed in ultraviolet and infrared.

      At long last, Jean was able to close his physical, as opposed to his mind's eyes, and attempt to regain his composure.
      Eyelids shut, he felt the all-seeing eye of the grand old architect of the universe upon him, and Jean knew his calling in life.
      Eventually, normalcy, insofar as it now existed for Jean, had returned, however infinitely improbable it was. He opened his eyes and blinked in the sunlight.
      He craned his neck upward to stare at the noonday sun for several moments. Yes, he thought, in response to the messages he was receiving, I understand. I'll do it. Soon.
      Second-layer encoding was for the most part was resolved in an instant. It would take Jean a month to detail what he had learned in the time it took for a photon of light to enter his retinas. And he would have no explanation as to how he came upon these complex explanations of life's deepest mysteries.
      The set of proteins that concerned Jean's purpose, buried deep within the third layer, would remain a mystery to all but himself for years to come. He would never detail them in writing.
      Jean glanced down at his lap to see a yellow and dark green-striped garter snake sleeping coiled in his lap. Feeling his gaze, the tiny serpent lifted his head, looked Jean in the eyes, flicked his tongue, and went upon its way.

Chapter 21 - Sleeping Village
      Road-weary, Paige slept in until after 10:00 a.m. She was considering the rut she had fallen into back at home, and didn't want her vacation days to fall into predictability, as well.
      Only two days after she'd quit smoking, for the time being, she reminded herself, her head was clearing up, and Paige began to realize that the weed was what had enabled her to take each day in stride, without looking at the gestalt.
      And she was determined to change her previously natural order of things. To that end, today she slept in, and instead of coffee, she went to McDonalds for two Big Macs, large fries, and a large Coke. She'd deal with the bigger issues later, she decided.
Perhaps she was rationalizing.
      As she inhaled her brunch, she considered what Terry had said before she'd gone, about her role in the department as a whole. Paige wanted a raise and a promotion.
      She could live without the raise, strictly speaking, although the price of fuel was finally beginning to impact the price of food, and Paige's taste for the gourmet and exorbitant when it came to gastronomy would be aided somewhat by a pay increase. She found she was saving less, lately.
      But more importantly, a raise came part and parcel with more responsibility and increased duties, which was her real goal. After seeing how often cases were being mishandled, she felt she could do some good in managing cases and shaping policy when she wasn't in the field. Her current role as mere detective didn't allow for such things.
      Of course, the chief seemed miffed about her bailing on him to take a vacation, but he'd be over that as soon as she returned, she believed. He huffed and puffed and blew a lot of smoke, but Terry knew that Paige was bluster without substance, where she was concerned. Her track record was impeccable.
      She got a refill and strode back to her rental. The weather was so nice and pleasant in Tennessee, she wished she had traded up for a convertible like her own.
      In accordance to her plan of avoiding routine, she hadn't checked out of her room that morning. She may stay the night there again, or she may not, but she would let the fates decide.

      Several miles outside of the town that gave home to the country's largest subterranean lake, she began to see billboards. "See Rock City," they exclaimed, without explanation. Were she not in Tennessee, but rather say, Seattle, she might have thought Rock City a museum dedicated to purveyors of bass, drums and guitar.
      Nashville proclaimed themselves the capital of country and bluegrass, but the reality was Nashville was much more in touch with the roots of rock-n-roll than most cities, and that definitely included over- privileged, bleeding-hearted Washington State.
      She dialed up 'Tennessee' by Arrested Development on her iPod, and then listened to the seminal Johnny Cash album produced by Rick Rubin. The juxtaposition of Cash's voice, and the weight behind it, the accumulated pain acquired by a lifetime of hard living, against the modernistic sentiments expressed by Trent Reznor's lyrics in 'Hurt' brought tears to her eyes as she drove.
      Perhaps she was crying for her lost grandparents. Her childhood. For Suzanne. For the life she hadn't lived, and the life she had yet to live. Perhaps she was getting her period.
      When the next sign proclaimed "Fabulous Rock City - 5 Miles," she decided she was all cried out. She checked her mascara. It was waterproof, but she had to make sure it hadn't run. She didn't want to look like a raccoon, or a porn star who'd gone three oral rounds with Peter North.
      Satisfied that she was at most red and puffy around the eyes, Paige relaxed and enjoyed the release of endorphins she gained from opening the emotional floodgates a tiny bit. Her profession demanded detachment and stoicism, so she grabbed her opportunities to feel emotion when and where she could, and relished them in private.
      Although it wasn't apparent just yet, Paige knew Tennessee was the most beautiful state in the south. It was a well-kept secret, and the residents of the state liked it that way.
      Newcomers and interlopers massed in Nashville and Memphis, leaving the rest of the state, the gorgeous, scenic, pristine portions, to the neo-aboriginals who had occupied it since the time when they themselves were the interlopers.
      When she reached Rock City, she was abuzz with memory, inspired by the promise of what lay ahead. She could see the vistas and valleys below, already, the rocky crags jutting upward from verdant blankets of

grass, dotted with clusters of trees. In her head, 'Rockytop' by Barbara Mandrell and the Mandrell Sisters was on a continuous loop.
      Unable to contain herself, she ran and skipped to the ticket office, much as she had as a child in the company of her grandparents, only without the laughing and admonitions for them to hurry up.
      Paige bought an adult pass, but caught her self-thinking "One Child" when asked by the man working the ticket counter. He put a wristband on her delicate, pale wrist and eyed her appreciatively, clearly grateful for the proximity to a living goddess.
      Despite her urge to rush out the door and begin the day's adventure, she exercised her waning discipline and rushed through the gift shop, so she could spend the rest of her day wandering and exploring.
      She quickly picked out the best available t-shirt and a bourbon tumbler. She'd smoke her hash from under it, she decided.
      Paige changed into the baby blue t-shirt in the ladies room, and secreted her souvenir glass under the seat of her car. Prepared for the day with a soft canteen of water and her Timberland hiking boots, Paige set off toward the sun, which was nearly overhead.
      She hoped the boots possessed function as well as form. Her steel- toed Doc Martin's were super-comfortable, but Paige celebrated the occasion by buying new footwear and was already realizing the folly of breaking in new, unproven boots on a hike of up to ten miles. Oh, well, live and learn, she thought, undaunted.
      It was a well-thought out, self-directed walking tour. The first leg of the day lulled you into complacency. Paige walked through a forest that seemed positively primeval, reading with interest the plaques detailing information on the local flora and fauna.
      Although it was high noon, and would continue to get hotter until two or three, the forest glens were cool and breezy, and the noonday heat prevented many tourists from setting out behind her, and so Paige mainly wandered in quiet solitude.
      The forest trail was idyllic enough, but what had enchanted Paige in her youth were the structures. They had lost little charm as she aged, but rather changed from magical to charming, quaint and endearing.
      The first building was a gigantic house made of candy and gingerbread. Frosted eaves overhung candy cane posts on a porch with a door studded with over-sized gumdrops. Ostensibly, this was the witch's

home from 'Hansel and Gretel', but the designers had wisely omitted anything overt that may instill fear in small children.
      As an adult, Paige made note of the cauldron in the front yard, already stocked with firewood beneath it, waiting to cook plump young children. Behind the house was a detail unnoticed by her when she was herself a plump little girl, an add-on to the house, roughly twice the size of an outhouse, with bars on the window, and an over-sized iron padlock.
      Paige caught a slight chill at these previously undetected sinister aspects of this particular display and moved on toward a patch of sunlight to warm herself and push the darker thoughts out of her mind.
      Before Paige left this enchanted glen completely, she passed through a mini-forest of giant mushrooms, populated by dwarven statuary. The 'shrooms had small doors, windows, and even window boxes where living flowers grew. She found herself wishing she could shrink down and enter these mycological houses, which were perfect to the last detail inside.
      In one of nature's ironies, there were actual mushrooms growing beneath the larger artificial ones. Sadly, Paige saw none of the amanita muscaria variety with their distinctive red caps dotted with white circles.
      The designers, apparently anticipating her thoughts had added a hookah-smoking caterpillar atop one of the last of the giant toadstools. A small wooden door was affixed to a nearby tree. Next to that was a simple table, and a bottle with a tag that read 'drink me' laminated against the elements.
      On a whim, she attempted to lift it, only to find that it was of course stuck fast to the tabletop.
      The forest trail ended soon after that, and she emerged at the edge of a cliff that seemed to drop down a mile or more, all at once.
      A simple guardrail and self-preservation were all that stood between visitors to Rock City and certain death.
      Signs directed her to the right, but Paige strongly considered going left and trying to find a safe path down to the valley below. What kept her from venturing out on her own was their policy of searching for visitors who hadn't turned in their wristbands by sunset, or 8 p.m, whichever was later.
      A few visitors had gotten lost, but Rock City had never had a single fatality, the literature proudly proclaimed.

      She walked along the cliff's edge, slowly following the guardrail and pathway, transfixed by both the view and the accompanying on rush of memories it brought about.
      She came upon a sight that now seemed more than a little incongruous in her adulthood, a balanced rock. It was really a stone pillar with a hugely over-sized top, but the effect was surreal, both because it reminded her of the Roadrunner and Coyote cartoons, and because the sight seemed reserved for western environments by association.
      At the next vista, proclaimed to be the literal high point of the tour, she spent her time watching the sun slowly set in somber reflection.
      The final landmark was a rope bridge, some hundred feet long, spanning the abyss below. Paige needed to hold the hand of both grandparents in order to steel herself to cross it before, and even then, she trembled.
      She considered it a metaphor for her gradual transformation into adulthood that she now decisively and sure-footedly strode across it, undeterred by the swaying motions, the wind, or the seemingly endless fall below her.
      Paige didn't spend a lot of time looking down, but at the midway point, she stopped and took a multitude of pictures. She held the camera at shoulder-level, and turned in thirty-degree increments in a full circle. Later she would experiment with stitching the pictures together in Photoshop to make a seamless panorama. If only she had someone to share it with, she thought.
      At journey's end, she realized her larger journey was also at an end. Paige had revisited the fondest memories of her youth, and found they were still intact, now enhanced with the passage of time even, rather than diminished. She said a silent prayer for her deceased grandparents and moved on with her life.

Chapter 22 - Messiah
      Jean collected his racing thoughts, slowly rose, and returned home. Upon arrival, he scrawled a single word in a fresh notebook, "Messiah". Then he slept for thirteen hours and eleven minutes.
      He dreamed he was the only man on Earth. But it wasn't Earth, as we knew it. It was Earth Mark Two. Paradise. There, he was without fear or need. He had no desire for food or water. It was not necessary to breathe. The untidy process of elimination was eliminated.
      He soared above the treetops and could see the landscape unfold ahead of him, in neat, tiled increments.
Simulation.
      Although he didn't see them, he felt the presence of angels. Each of them female, each a perfect, unique specimen. Jean knew they were his eternal reward, and this paradise would one day be his to share with them, to explore and enjoy, to repopulate.
      He touched down at the edge of a canopied forest. He found himself lecturing to an audience rapt with attention. The plants, the heavens, the insects and animals, each granted Jean their attention as he spoke to them at length.
      The fireflies blinked in agreement and anticipation of his next utterances. When he had finished his lecture, he was presented with a few options. The forest, a tunnel, a road.
      He could enter the forest, feral, to become the next incarnation of Pan. The trees parted ahead of him, to signal their desire that he live among them.
      The tunnel extended beneath the road, and indicated that, should he chooses that route, his future would be entirely unearthly. Alien. There was a note of unwholesomeness to this.
      Jean chose the road, the path back to civilization. To Maxxy, and his life's work. The architect of this simulated environment had encoded him with free will, and he exercised it. But it was important that he was given a choice.
      He walked along the road for millennia, it felt. When he could walk no more, Earth Mark One began to resolve around him. The landscape was unchanged, but cars and trucks, headlights blazing, began to once again occupy the roads. He turned back to look at a semi which blared its horn at him and was enveloped in the white glow of its headlights.

He awoke with his head on fire. Paradise. Angels. Goatu. Messiah. Simulation.
      Jean no longer had to think, or question. He was free to simply do. In this simulation, he was but one element in a nearly endless iteration of scenarios in pursuit of perfection. He was free from right or wrong. What he did was right, without question, because he followed his programming.
      Freewill was a particularly inventive illusion in this sim. Without this thin veil to shield us, our lives would be rote, empty, filled at best with quiet, impotent desperation. Hopeless.
      Instead, our every action was carefully calculated in advance to present us with the appearance of choice, satisfying an innate need for autonomy. This allowed the simulation to proceed without end, and our belief in our independence, and the resultant actions that proceeded from it, in exchange powered it.
      He considered entropy, and thermodynamics, and confirmed that energy was never lost or created, merely converted and exchanged.
      Powered by his own thoughts, Jean worked through the night. He mapped the relevant portions of the third layer encoding for later use, making countless millions of computing hours unnecessary.
      Whether extraterrestrial intelligence, or a glimpse into the deeper workings of the sim, life on Earth was to take a quantum leap forward.
      Jean suspected both. The holographical universe construct we occupied was but a process in a computer operating on the plane of existence above our own. No matter how you sliced it, it was alien to us, even if it were human.
      He strongly felt that the whole of time, past, present and future, all occupied the now. Which indicated he was already in some far-flung future, in a simulated present. And that future would end at the beginning, in the distant past. A perfect circle.
      With each infinitely long cycle the universe or metaverse, rather, the collection of multiverses, advanced toward perfection.
      At the beginning of this grand cycle, there existed only pure energy, and the absence of energy, or its inverse, negative energy. At the end lay perfection, where each subatomic particle had nestled into niches that complimented each other perfectly.

      In the penultimate act of perfection, it all had to be destroyed in order to begin anew. Creative destruction, Jean thought. Hail Eris! Hail Discordia!
      These rationalizations, implanted into his fragile mind as easily as a farmer pushed a seed into freshly-tilled fertile soil, enable Jean to toil toward his purpose unfettered by questions of mortals, mortality or morality. We occupied a transmutable perfection, and our every action was perfect, leading to a more finely tuned version of perfection.
      Heaven and Hell, he realized, were the two logical extremes of energy, token symbols used to make the equations understandable. One without the other would be static. Stasis. At which point, there was no difference between the two extremes. Where they mixed was life.
      He began writing his second layer discoveries, unedited, directly to the Douglas listserv. He presented them as theoretical ruminations, rather than as what he knew they were, unchallengeable statements of fact.
      Each of his lengthy screeds caused an uproar, which he ignored. Communication, for Jean, became a one-way street, and he, a conduit. Discussions would only distract him, and diminish the impact and implication of his hard-won discoveries.
      With each post, each assertion, Jean sent individual researchers into new tangents, armed with new pieces of the puzzle. The implications were profound.
      Divine though he may be, a random number generator in a sandbox we thought of as the entirety of existence, Jean still had to pay the rent.
      He prepared a double-strength pot of coffee, and went to work, which Jean now considered his cover story, a disguise he wore. Because for every Messiah, he knew, there existed an equal and opposite Centurion. A devil.
      His job became easier than ever. Solutions to problems entered his mind minutes before the problems themselves surfaced, this keeping his mind free to tackle larger issues, open to receive instruction.
      Call it Ra, God, VALIS, quantum communication, ESP, or what have you, Jean's thoughts were not his own. It is simply not possible, Jean thought, for a single mind to know so much on its own.
      The universe was the computer we each occupied, in a sense. Or, more to the point, each of us occupied a different universe, a different computer, all of which were quantumly networked.

      If he hadn't been given a task to complete, he would have refocused his considerable energies, knowledge and talents on cosmology and subatomic physics. He was on the verge of a unified theory, a single viewpoint that solved the quandary of microcosm versus macrocosm.
Instead, he listened to 'Meter Vs Yard', and checked out eBay.
      As things now always went for Jean, he immediately had what he needed at his fingertips. He couldn't always get what he wanted, as the song said, but the next piece required was available for the taking, without fail.
      It was both comforting and disturbing. He couldn't resolve the dichotomy, so he ignored it in zen-like fashion. He believed everything and nothing. In the words of Gibby Haynes, he was "Psychic...Powerless."
      This irrationalization, the tao method of doing by not doing, enabled him to sally forth undaunted, in the face of what most would consider crippling mental illness.
      Jean knew it as enlightenment, and was saddened by the knowledge that most would never attain such bliss. For whatever reason, their minds were blocked from admission into these colleges of cosmic higher learning.
      He began to consider them non-player characters in the role-playing game we know as life. Pawns in the game. Tools, at best. Hindrances and obstacles, at worst.
To continue the gaming simile, each had a class, and an alignment. Good, neutral and evil were involved in an existential ballet,
choreographed by an invisible hand. Neutrals were swayed one way or another, at times, and the balanced would shift, but each tip of the scales resulted in a new eddy, which moved toward balance.
      Despite what all of science believed, this was perpetual motion. It was so obvious and apparent, it could only be too simple to appeal to men of science, whose chief power over others lay in making their explanations overly complex.
      Jean began to consider class as archetypes. J.R.R. Tolkien was a prophet in the same manner as Heinlein, King, Clarke, Asimov, Philip K. Dick and others. In fact all writers, and artists in general, revealed parts of the undisputed truth.
      What Tolkien expressed in 'The Hobbit', 'The Lord of The Rings', and 'The Silmarillion' was a racial, or, more accurately, genetic memory.
      His writings, from which all RPGs flowed, from Dungeons and Dragons to Final Fantasy, to whatever the next big thing in gaming would

be, contained the secret history behind our present day genetic make-up.
      Each of us could be classified by his taxonomy of fantasy races and classes.
      We all knew ogres, orcs, elves, hobbits, wizards, paladins, clerics, princesses, princes, kings, queens. Golems. Chivalric knights, black knights. While each certainly had the capacity for good and evil, many occupied one end of the spectrum or another. For many, conversion was quite simply not a part of their character.
      Each side of the balanced but constantly in flux equation held a blend of the classes. While the physical characteristics were less apparent now, Jean knew that in the distant past, they were actual races, and were now simply muted and homogenized.
      Hybridized. Each of us halflings, to a degree. Some, however, retained a majority of one characteristic. If you looked closely, you could still see their origins, as plain as the looks on their faces.

Chapter 23 - Childhood's End
      Paige Frances Burner decided to grow up. She realized the niche she occupied was in fact a stagnating canal, lacking vitality. She nested in a tidal pool at the neglect of the ocean.
      It was no wonder, she thought, that she found herself without a mate. She was the biggest fish in a small pond of her own design, and had simply swallowed up any potential suitors, in an effort to eliminate competition.
      She needed to expand her horizons, and begin the second act in her life's tragic-comic drama. Paige decided to return to California, in the manner that a salmon returned to their origins in order to spawn.
      For all the temporary joy her vacation had brought her, she returned to her room numb, at best, and alternatingly depressed, at worst.
      Predictably, she began menstruating that evening, which spurred new thoughts, those of a mate, or mates. Of a homestead, an immovable keep. A castle of her own, with a view where she mastered all she surveyed.
Of children of her own.
      She ordered three pizzas with everything, and a gallon of Coke, comfort food, and burrowed under her blankets for protection against her own mutinous mind and body.
      Unable to stop herself, she switched on her Treo at long-last and dialed Terry.
      "Chief," she said, straining to keep the emotion from her voice, and failing, "I want to come home." She sobbed, silently, but he still heard her.
      "It's about good goddamned time, Burner," he said gently, "We missed you. Carry your ass."
      She cried herself to sleep, to be awakened nearly an hour later by a pizza delivery driver. She accepted the food without relish, and gave a perfunctory tip, for a change.
      Here's a tip, kid, she thought. Don't piss your youth away delivering pizza for a living.
      Her newfound morbidity felt liberating. She switched on the TV and became mired in schadenfreude, suddenly delighted that CNN could show her countless souls considerably less fortunate than she.
      She ate until she looked pregnant, and drank Coke until she found herself on the toilet taking in one liquid while discharging another, and decided to get a grip.

Tomorrow is another day, she hoped.
      She gripped Ms. Kitty, and eventually found the release she needed, both physical and mental, that enabled her to sleep.
      She awoke the next day, and slept with her eyes open, numb to life's endless possiblities.
      Idly, she flipped the channels and waited for the local pizza parlor to reopen, wishing all the while she had some dope. Being stoned all the time was infinitely preferable to the alternative, she decided.
      After she'd consumed the equivalent of both breakfast and lunch in a single afternoon sitting, she lucked upon Cary Grant in 'The Fountainhead'.
      While both he and his character were both dashing, heroic figures to Paige, she knew the real underlying hero was Ayn Rand, who went on to spend the next decade after writing the book on which her favorite film was based penning 'Atlas Shrugged'.
      This gargantuan work was both critical and unknown, outside of literary and philosophical circles. The average college graduate knew nothing about this tome, or its authoress, even though it had been parodied on The Simpsons, a cartoon which many could recite from, delivering endless lines from memory. Cult movies and cartoons were the modern equivalent of Beowulf, it seemed.
      Paige considered that perhaps she should start writing. She knew one thing, though. She'd never join the legion of hack authors who churned out rife and clich�d novels about detectives, lawyers, or cowboys. Cyberpunk was more to her liking, future fantasy worlds where technology and magic were merged and made indistinguishable. Perhaps with a healthy dose of lesbian sexual slavery and domination.
      The thought of radically shifting direction in life roused her from her waking slumber, slightly, and she booked a return flight for the next afternoon. Wherever her destiny lay, it was apparently not in Tennessee, or even New Orleans, as much as that city felt like home.
      She had the frightening feeling that perhaps she was going mad. The impulse came to her again and again to go outside without shoes, and tread upon the dewy grass, to get her feet dirty, to explore the nearby woods.
      Paige resisted, feeling that acquiescing to what she considered irrational thoughts would only further encourage this disturbing condition, and degrade her already tenuous and fragile mental state.

      Instead she slept in short bursts throughout the night and the next day, electing to spend the afternoon and early evening in her rented coop, rather than at an impersonal airport.
      When the time came to vacate, to visit the podunk puddle-hopper airport that would bring her to the next leg of her homecoming, she did so without a tinge of remorse or regret. She had depleted her allotment of maudlin emotions for the year, and she was glad to be released from the burden, which had felt for a time to be unbearable.
      Unencumbered by luggage or carry-on bags, Paige drank heavily all the way back to California. As heavily as one dared, and was allowed, in this brave new post 9-11, post-2001 world.
      A part of herself was disgusted to be even remotely associated with what she knew was the machinery of a police state. She knew she was one of the good guys, and that she played a vital role in averaging those unable to prevent their own demise, but Paige still felt dirty by association. Sick.
      At least that's what she blamed her bout of airliner bathroom puking on. There was a reason she smoked heavily, and only drank socially, though.
      Eventually, she dozed, and awoke in the land she had originated from, in the manner of Dorothy Gale. Tomorrow, she would gather her heart, brain and courage from home, and go off to see the wizard. Ms. Kitty would play the part of Toto.
      She'd taken a cab to LAX, since she despised the jock mentality that pervaded every facet of San Diego, in anticipation of not wanting to drive back home. It was money well spent, and she dozed again on the cab ride south to her apartment, paying for her dreams by the mile.
      The next action she was aware of was waking up the next afternoon, hungrier than hell. She consulted her sheet of take-out numbers and ordered sixty dollars worth of gyros, salads and baklava.
      Fuck work, she yawned after she'd eaten more than enough to feed two starving Greek lamb farmers. Maybe she'd go back to her job after the weekend. She was still on vacation time.
      She pulled out her Tupperware container and put it on the coffee table. Paige hesitated to start smoking again already. Instead, she found a myriad of excuses in things she needed to do in order to justify such a magnificent reward.

      She did laundry, washing her bedroom set, and her vacation clothes. She cleaned out her refrigerator, by either eating or throwing away anything close to old.
      Then she restocked her cupboards by going to Whole Paycheck and splurging the economy of a rural Mexican village on every decadent organic delicacy she laid eyes upon. Her shopping cart gave her the appearance of a welfare queen shopping at Wal-Mart on the first of the month.
      While she was lugging bag after weighty bag upstairs to her apartment, Paige realized a male companion had more benefits than she had previously considered. She simply had to stop looking at men as dildos possessing a modicum of artificial intelligence, at best.
      Conversely, she realized, she could expand her potential dating pool beyond cops, criminals and the deceased. Night classes seemed a safe bet, where people would be mostly her own age and social rank, and would also be generally intelligent.
      It was when it became time to make a batch of the smoothies Paige used to ingest her regimen of nutritional supplements that she realized she had yet another task ahead of her. It was time to go to the city of Pomona.
      There was a chemical supply house there that sold dietary-grade potassium by the kilogram. She found out about it via the loose circle of acquaintances she'd met in the course of spending tens of thousands of dollars at Whole Foods over the past few years.
      As unlikely as it would seem from her vacation escapades, Paige was keenly interested in her health, and usually ate accordingly. She was also acutely ashamed of her recent culinary travels and travails.
      The white breads and pastries she had ingested of late were made with bleached flour. White flour, and bleached wheat flour, gained their pristine, illusory ivory appearance via a process involving a chemical known as alloxan.
      Although it wasn't listed as an ingredient, it remained present throughout the baking process, and was so ingested at the end of the food chain of evidence. Alloxan was a chemical used to induce diabetes in lab rats.
      At the same time, Paige was consuming relatively large quantities of aluminum. Used as leavening and an anti-caking agent, aluminum (formerly aluminum, and currently thusly named in the United Kingdom) lodged in

the brain, eventually interfering with electromagnetic functions later in life, leading to Alzheimer's disease.
      The rat and roach poison sodium fluoride was actually a by-product of the aluminum making process. Until the forties and fifties, it was a serious toxic waste problem. Then the ADA helped convince the country that this substance that affected intelligence and higher brain functions while contributing to bone cancers was a good nutritional supplement, leading to healthier teeth.
      Forget that Kentuckians, more heavily fluoridated than even Floridians, had the most dental caries, and the fewest teeth, per capita, in the entire country.
      Due to their relationship, fluoride worked synergistically with aluminum, acting as a receptor key, expediting aluminum's passage through the blood-brain barrier.
      Monosodium glutamate, present in all fast food, chips, Chinese food, etcetera, was an excitoxin.
      Long ago, chefs in the Orient used a small amount of certain seaweed to enhance their recipes. In addition to sweet, salty, bitter and sour, our taste buds have receptors to detect glutamates.
      When MSG contacts the nerve ending of a taste bud, the nerve essentially goes a little crazy. The end results is that MSG-enhanced foods are perceived as tastier than other foods.
      It was also addictive, and was used to induce weight gain in order to study obesity in lab rats.
      At least, Paige thought, she had avoided the neurotoxin Aspartame, with its presumed brain tumors. She hoped she had, at least.
      Aluminum, MSG, Aspartame and toxic, brain-damaging and insanity-inducing mercury were present in vaccines. Not always together, but Paige certainly didn't queue up for flu shots and the like.
      While sodium, in trace amounts, was necessary for cell function, there was no earthly need to add it to our diet.
      The Yanomami indian tribe, land-locked, and essentially without sodium in their diets, received only trace elements via various means. They did, however, eat large amounts of bananas and potatoes, and cooked with ash, thereby receiving megadoses of potassium each day.
      The cellular function of potassium was to facilitate the transfer of sodium out of the body. As a result, the Yanomami had no angina, heart

attack or stroke epidemics, as we did, and did not gain weight as they aged.
      These were the concerns that led Paige to make her fateful sojourn to Pomona on this day.

Chapter 24 - Fate, Tempted
      Having placed several eBay orders to be overnighted, Jean began to shop for thermocyclers. This was sort of an electronic sifting machine for DNA. It allowed one to input a mixed bag of DNA samples and have them neatly sorted electronically.
      He'd always wanted one, anyway. Now he had an excuse. The boom and bust lifestyle of California life guaranteed good deals to be had by all with capital or disposable income.
      He picked up the phone and dialed a number from an online ad and struck gold on the first call. A plasmapheresis lab had just folded. Jean could pick up a slightly used one for eighty-five hundred, or a brand new, packaged unit for ten thousand. They had four in all.
      It was no wonder they went bankrupt, Jean thought. He opted to get a new unit, which seemed best, considering his mission, and arranged to pick it up immediately after work. At 5:30 p.m., he headed down the five freeway.
      Traffic was bad, of course, but Jean had the patience of Job. He busted with a preroll and enjoyed the silence. Occasionally, he cracked his window and listened to the wind speak, or tuned his radio between two stations in order to learn new secrets.
He was ablaze with purpose.
      When he arrived, the parking lot was nearly empty. He backed into a space in front of the door. The owner had promised to wait for Jean, and for ten thousand dollars, he'd damn well better.
      As he was opening the door, a deep rose-colored convertible raced in to park beside him, tires screeching.
      Out jumped one of the most alluring women Jean had ever seen. Although she was clad in sweat pants and a t-shirt with a washing machine on it that said 'Sonic Youth', she out-classed the average Hollywood starlet, hands down.
      She had fiery red hair. Jean strongly supposed she was one of his angels from the future, although she was rather petite.
      Promised as he was to Maxine, he couldn't very well hit on her, but neither could he studiously avoid her. The circumstances dictated his actions.
"Where's the fire?" he asked.

      Paige noticed him for the first time, so intent was she on her current task. She really wanted to get home and burn one.
      "What? Oh, sorry. They're supposed to be closed by now." She gestured at the edifice.
      Jean held the door open for this hurried seraphim, and they entered together.
      The store was a chemist's dream. When Jean made the call, he'd only gotten an address, and had no idea where he was going. It was a two- storey warehouse packed to the gills with every element of the periodic table, packaged for bulk sale. Everywhere Jean looked, he saw chemical symbols, biohazard stickers, and marks indicating radioactivity.
      Nearer the counter, like some sci-fi 7-11, there were rows and rows of beakers, graduated cylinders, glass tubing, and rubber stoppers. It looked like the back lot of some b-movie studio, Troma Film, maybe. Jean looked around for the Toxic Avenger.
      Chivalry wasn't dead in Southern California; it had never arrived there in the first place. Weighing his options, the storeowner bypassed the beauty and her routine minor purchases to cater to his new and wealthy client.
"Jean!" he said loudly, as if to an old friend.
      Paige, undeterred, got a shopping cart and went to the section that housed consumables.
      Jean shook the man's hand, and the two walked into a back room to complete their exchange. When they had emerged, the owner was pushing a dolly with a device slightly smaller than a mini fridge, but clearly much heavier.
"Will this be it?" he asked Jean.
      "Actually..."Jean handed him a short list of chemicals, which the man scanned briefly, them sped off to retrieve. These were items Jean needed to use in conjunction with the thermocycler.
      To pass the time, he watched Paige, who had her potassium and calcium, and was carefully selecting glass globes, rubber gaskets, sections of clear tubing, and a hydrostatic pump the size of a cigarette pack.
      She arrived at the counter just ahead of the proprietor, who bagged the items and slid them toward Jean.
"What's the damage?" Jean asked.

      "Oh! Well, there's no charge for these. Just keep me in mind the next time you need something, Mr..."
"Splicer."
      "Of course. Mr. Splicer. It was a pleasure doing business with you." He again shook Jean's hand, clearly very happy to have unloaded a somewhat obscure piece of lab equipment to the benefit of both.
      Paige wondered what the device was, and if the guy was a good- looking meth cook. LSD chemist, maybe.
      Only when Jean was headed toward the door was the owner's attention refocused on Paige's paltry, sub-two hundred dollar purchase.
      Such was Southern Californian life, Paige thought. They were either gay, in love with themselves, or in love with money. None of them would even procreate, she thought, were it not for appearances and an ego-driven need for heirs. Children were props, here, like dogs that matched your outfit.
      Paige caught up to Jean as he was closing his trunk. He was obviously successful, single, and intelligent. Too bad he almost certainly was a criminal, she thought.
      She spied the Auburn University parking pass on his back window, and felt an odd chill of coincidence pass through her.
      "Jean Splicer, eh? Does Warner Brothers know you're abusing their copyright?" she asked him in mock seriousness.
"Heh," he said. "The guy didn't bat an eye at that one, did he?"
      "Oh, I'm sure he's used to assumed names, in his line of work. Are you off to cop some Sudafed or something, next?"
Jean was rightly insulted. "What are you, a cop?" he asked. "Maybe," Paige replied.
      "Then, yes," he said, "I'm going to make some bath tub crank using discarded Duracells and the corrosion off of my car's battery posts, thus putting at risk my six figure salary so I can make a quick two hundred dollars."
      He pointed at his bumper sticker, which read, "My kid cloned your honor student".
      "Maybe I should stop advertising my credentials before I get busted, eh?" He grinned widely at her.
      "At least your criminal lifestyle affords you a nice car," she said, continuing the charade.

      "What profits a man if he should gain a fly ride and lose his only freedom?" Jean said, with humility. "Quite alright, madam. At times, I wonder if the difference is academic. Ostensibly legitimate chemists ruin far more lives than the illegal variety. I cite Monsanto and Dow as prima facie evidence."
"So, you're a jail house lawyer, too?" Paige asked, smiling.
      "I'm all things to all people," he said. "But my name really is Jean." He extended his hand, which she accepted.
      "Well, Dr. Splicer, are you going to stand around parking lot pimpin' all night or are you going to help an old lady with her groceries?"
      "Cry pardon, m'lady!" he exclaimed. "I'm used to the liberated sort who snarl and sneer when one attempts to render neighborly assistance."
      "Well, I've a lot of cats to feed obviously, pensioner that I am," Paige said.
      "How do I know you aren't brewing absinthe with your Acme Junior Scientist Kit, yourself?" Jean asked, as they both arranged her purchases so the glass items wouldn't break on the ride home. "Or," he said, "Maybe you're a student of Mary Shelly."
      "You smell good," Paige said, getting a whiff of Jean's hydroponic cologne.
      "Why, thank you, Dr. Franhkensteen. I should point out that you, yourself, are more melodiously odiferous than the most highly-prized bouquets in existence."
"Are you going to ask me out, or what?" Paige asked bluntly. "Under any other circumstances, I most certainly would have. Sadly,
regretfully, I find myself both married to my work and promised to another, besides."
Jean offered a sad smile in consolation.

      They silently connected, and then parted ways, positively and negatively charged ions, strange attractors, both alike and exactly opposite

of each other. Nature was cruel indeed. Fate, doubly so.
      Were it not for his deep and abiding love for Maxine, Jean certainly would have attempted to court the redheaded stranger. Even now, he felt regret, despite his meaningful affection for his future bride.
      He blamed the predicament on a silly American adherence to a monogamous convention that ran entirely contrary to evolutionary biology. In a more advanced society, like-minded individuals would develop families beyond the nuclear standard of the 1950s.
      In fact, the single man, single woman standard, or even the homosexual equivalent, was crippling, economically and stifling, fundamentally. A trio offered more to each other, to any offspring, and to the world at large.
      Many people dreamed of such things, but few had the determination to make them a reality, in the face of centuries of western peer pressure, he thought. Even himself. It was entirely selfish to want to acquire a second partner while still in negotiations with the first. An unreasonable expectation.
      Besides, he had work to do. In the words of the The Blues Brothers, he was on a mission from God. And he couldn't wait to get home and play with his very expensive tinker toys.
      It never occurred to Jean that he himself was playing God, meddling with biology and human nature, potentially about to upset the natural order of things.
      Even if he had, it would have made no difference at all. His revolutionary zeal was derived from a higher power, and it couldn't be resisted.
He was, after all, the Messiah.
      Or the latest in a long line of Messiahs, at least. It was still pretty cool. Cooler than being in Time Magazine, in fact.
