Chapter 26 - Ouroboros Rising
      The next day Jean worked at a furious pace. Not doing the things he was paid huge sums of money to do, but on his own projects. His work for D-Gen was mostly delegated now.
      He was getting things in order for his first attempt at creating a custom virus. Moving beyond the theoretical, Jean was now stockpiling supplies in order to create a vehicle for delivering what he considered the world's salvation: a disease that would kill the elite psychopaths who polluted Mother Earth, plaguing the rest of mankind like so many devils, flesh and bone.
      Once or twice he found himself thinking about the redhead from Pomona, and trying to dismiss the feeling that it was fate. He'd always felt that way when a particularly delicious piece of ass came his way. God was a pimp of sorts, he felt, and sent you hot sex as a reward for something or another.
      But he had no desire to find her again, and had the unsettling notion that the chance encounter wasn't about sex, but was still some twist of fate. Jean felt considerably more...psychic, lately.
      He wasn't going to cheat on Maxine, anyway. She would be the mother of his children one day, he felt, and infidelity was sure way to ruin a relationship.
      He knew this mainly because he'd used it to intentionally ruin many relationships in the past. Even when he'd been caught accidentally, and patched things up by talking his way out of it, the affair inevitably soured whatever closeness had been reached with the other party.
      Nothing would keep him from a life with Maxxy, just as nothing would thwart his plans to implement paradise on earth. After all, he couldn't very well bring children into a psychopathic society such as the one he currently labored under.
      Jean stole a few germ samples from D-Gen. Stole wasn't really accurate. He simply grew some cultures and divided them in half. If anything, he grew more than he took, leaving the corporation with more samples than they had before.
      He knew the divine wouldn't begrudge him breaking a few commandments in pursuit of higher goals, but lately the very idea of stealing, lying, or cheating disturbed him to his core.

      Never mind that he would soon be killing. Then again, he thought, the virus would be doing the killing, not him.
      Jean's thoughts always came full circle now. They always resolved into perfection, each new thought connecting perfectly to the last one in a neverending sequence, always in synchronous harmony with his calling in life.
      Messiah. It would be frightening if it wasn't all so perfectly engineered by some higher ordered mind. Call it God, or collective unconsciousness, it could not be resisted. The universal will couldn't be ignored once contact was made.
      Jean worked as though he had a holy gun pointed at the back of his head at all times. But instead of being nerve-wrenching, it brought a deep feeling of contentment, of reassurance. He could do no wrong, or he would die.
      Even, dying in service to this great unknown, this faceless divine architect, or group of architects, would be good and right. This, he decided was Nirvana, all day, every day.
      Even his dreams were magical. He'd recently been shown the shape of the universe.
      It was, as some suspected, a hypertoroid. In an existence Homer Simpson could be really appreciative of, we all basically were born, lived and died inside of a giant magical doughnut.
      At the same time, it wasn't a doughnut at all. It was a snake swallowing it own tail. Ouroboros. A beginning without ending, and an existence without any beginning.
      His recent experience with ayahuasca had changed Jean in fundamental ways. The small snakes remained in his brain after he had come down, of course. But he knew that in advance. For the rest of his life, he would always know who his enemies were. It was a gift.
      Which is what puzzled him about the girl he'd met at the chemical supply house. He didn't get the sense she was an enemy, as such, but there was something there, and it wasn't simple chemistry, or animal magnetism.
      It couldn't matter, anyway. He'd probably never see her again. He still talked to Maxxy regularly, and she kept him grounded.
      But he came away from his recent solo flight with something else, besides. He now understood that western culture conspired against the snake, and that there were dark reasons behind this.

      Snakes were possessors of a deep and primal magic. They were the link to our past. No other reptile had such a close relationship with man, not lizards, alligators. Only snakes lived among us, for us, even.
      Without snakes to keep down the vermin, humans would probably never have made it this far. Even, with them, the black plague had decimated the population of old world Europe.
      The connection between reptiles and humans was an obvious one: the human brain. Our pre-frontal cortex, and cerebellum were relatively new additions, evolutionarily speaking. They were wrappers, which encased our medulla oblongata.
      Our reptilian brain, in other words. It controlled anger and fear, our fight or flight responses. These characteristics were part of our psychological make-up, but originated in our reptile predecessors.
      Emotions were high-level responses unique to mammals. So, in a very real sense, psychopaths were reptiles in mammalian clothing. Jean suspected this would prove to be the key in developing his holy hand grenade, which is how he had started thinking of his virus. A devastating weapon that would turn the tide in an otherwise eternal holy war.
      Humans needed balance, of course. Left and right brain. Work and play. Wakefulness and sleep. Reptilian and mammalian responses.
      Only Western culture, it seemed to Jean, feared and reviled the snake, and there had to be reasoning behind it. India, Africa, South America, even the Native American worshipped, or at the very least, respected serpents.
      Western culture, instead, feared and loathed the snake to the degree that the primary tenet of our majority religion since time immemorial seemed to be that snakes represented evil. Ironically enough, humans exhibited a reptilian response toward reptiles.
      And perhaps, Jean thought, reptiles, snakes primarily, did represent evil, in humans. Cold, calculating, without concern, care or compassion for others. But it was a representation, a symbol. Snakes weren't evil, some people were evil.
      As usual, people confused the symbol with the underlying meaning, resulting in all kinds of confusion and cognitive dissonance.
      In a logical world, puppies and kitties would dominate popular culture. Even a casual perusal of the issue reveals that in fact reptiles are far more popular, or occupy more of our consciousness, at the very least.

      Dinosaurs, Sleestaks from "Land of the Lost", movies like Anaconda, "snake eyes", "ride the snake", Jim Morrison as The Lizard King. Lift any rock in popular culture, and you'll find a snake, or at least a reptile lurking there.
A clean-cut case of collective cognitive dissonance.
      Of course, Jean suspected the whole snake fear and attraction could just be shrouded penis envy and worship.
      At any rate, Jean had snakes in his brain now, and he liked it. He understood that they were misunderstood and no longer feared them. Of course, he didn't seem to fear anything at that point.
      He was so in touch with the universe, he sometimes had trouble walking on grass, so aware was he of the implications. Each of us was a destroyer of worlds.
      But you couldn't get anywhere in life by being a devout Buddhist, too respectful of life to kill a fly. No where Jean wanted to go, at least. What's next, germs and viruses with a fundamental right to life?
      Jean couldn't wait to get home and play with his thermocycler. With any luck, he'd have something useful in one of the packages he'd ordered the day before.
      He really had mixed feelings about his first test subjects, but he needed dramatic and surefire results, and a death row inmate was the least morally offensive person he could come up with.
      It was also a test of himself, to see if he could stomach murder. Again, someone who technically probably should already be dead seemed to be the best route to take initially. He could expand outward in concentric circles from there, with each test increasing by an order of magnitude.
Within three runs, Jean would be ready to cleanse the world.
      He had a vague feeling he should visit Pookie again. God helped those that helped themselves, of course. Speak softly and carry high- powered weaponry.
      Just in case. Be prepared, like some thugged-out boy scout. Or a knight templar.
      He spent the rest of the day immersed in the thermocycler's immense set of instruction manuals. He'd used one before, of course, but this one was his. You might know how to drive a car, but when you bought your first new one, you read the owner's manual.

      Besides, he might discover some features he didn't know about in the process. It was a bit too obscure to have a discussion group devoted specifically to it, he'd found.
      And he really didn't feel like wading through the tripe on Google, or the muck of Usenet, only to extract some entirely useless but interesting factoid, after hours dedicated to searching and reading.
      Google would leave him side-tracked, and he had been engrossed in too many technical discussions that devolved into 'meow' or 'snuh' cascades to bother with Usenet unless he had a spare weekend and was feeling sort of wacky. Writers and scientists sure had odd senses of humor.
      When Jean finally got home, there were three packages leaning against his door. He snatched them up and opened them on the kitchen table, under the brightest lights he had.
      The packages Jean had been so excited to receive contained some fingernails, some hair, and a chewed pencil.
All three were purported to be from Charles Manson.
      This, Jean decided, would be a good test of both his new toy, and of the authenticity of these newly acquired collector's items.
      First, he used his microscope and a tiny blade to extract a tooth imprint from the pencil. Then he prepared tinctures from that, a small section of hair, and a portion of a yellowed fingernail.
      Finally, Jean drew some of his own blood. He felt the whole process was taking on a ritualistic air, appropriately enough.
      He put each of the mixtures into separate chambers of the machine, and set it to work. Having done the hard part, he smoked and waited on the results.
      In the meanwhile, he tinkered with AutoCAD. By the time the thermocycler began printing out the results of the analysis, Jean had designed a new pipe.
      He liked smoking in public, at the movies, in the park. Even in California, it wasn't always appropriate behavior. His new design solved that problem.
      It was the size of a Walkman, basically, and could even house an mp3 player or radio tuner. A sliding compartment revealed a ceramic bowl with a spiral heating element in it. There was also a reversible fan, which ultimately fed a small tube that ran alongside some headphone cabling.

      You packed the bowl with weed, and switched it on. The element either burned or vaporized the pot, and the fan helped push it up the tube. Take a hit, switch it off.
      When you were done, and waiting to exhale, you reversed the fan and blew back into the tube.
      The smoke was then diverted through a scented filter, and came out smelling like citrus, or new car, or nothing at all.
      Jean was rather proud of it. Perhaps it would keep a few people out of prison, rather than just enable his smoking habit.
      Assuming it was ever manufactured. Electronic and mechanics were not his forte, unfortunately. If he could design and build, rather than just conceptualize, he'd probably already be a multi-millionaire, instead of a serial killer in training.

"Yeah, boss?"
"Don't call me. Whatever it is, handle it. I'm going to Jamaica." She tried hard to conceal the surprise on her face, and failed.
      "Oh, I used to be like you, Burner. Don't look so shocked. I just grew up when I made detective."
"Have fun, Chiefy," she said.

      "I intend to. Me and the wife will play Bob and Rita in the sun for two weeks. Fun is all we plan to have."
      Terry had apparently planned for today, because he left the breakroom and exited the building.
      Paige hustled back to her office in an adrenaline-fueled panic. Suddenly, she not only had to learn her new job, but she was taking over for the chief, if only temporarily, and he'd been running things for twenty-two years.
      She couldn't decide if he was wise or foolish, but Paige knew it was sink or swim.
      The first thing she did was send for reinforcements. She dialed Human Resources and had P.J. promoted to detective, got a list of the available labor pool emailed to her, and the number of an approved temp agency they used.
      It took a number of "goddamnits" in order to get the point across, that yes, goddamnit, she did have the authority to promote, hire, and fire. It helped that she put that particular emphasis on the word 'fire' and sounded like the chief when she got angry.
      Then Paige emailed all of her detectives and told them to be at the office for a meeting at six a.m. Anyone who wasn't there should find a new job, and not to expect a recommendation from the department.
      She figured any detectives that didn't use their email accounts regularly probably weren't worth a damn. And if they weren't team players, the others wouldn't bother telling them about the meeting.
      I might as well cut out the deadwood first thing, she thought, before things get busy around here again.
      She called P.J. at the end of his shift and asked him to come by the office on his way home, but she didn't say why.
      Paige didn't see any superstars on the team, but neither did the names of any obvious dead weight jump out at her. She supposed she should start fresh, anyway. It's not like the department was in great shape in the first place, or she wouldn't have a new job.
      It was too late to catch any one at the temp agency, but she left a detailed message explaining what she needed. Basically, she wanted two highly computer literate personal assistants with no criminal records of significance, and she needed them tomorrow.

      Paige grabbed all of Terry's paperwork from his desk and studied it, trying to get a feel for what she'd have to be doing, the decisions she's have to make.
      She was relieved to see it was mostly standard stuff, shift change request, situation reports, departmental updates and the like. Otherwise, she'd be really overwhelmed right now.
      On the other hand, it gave her an overview of the entire process, a bird's eye view of how things worked. The latent software engineer in her twitched at the room for improvement apparent in the untenable process of paper shuffling.
      Paige was about to start outlining major areas of rapid application development when P.J. knocked on the office door.
"Chief?"
      "Come in, Pete," Paige said. She wondered why he didn't knock on her office door first, and understood he had probably walked right past it. Not very observant, but under the circumstances, she was willing to let it go. Maybe it was habit, or the fact that the chief's office light was on and hers was not.
"Hey, Paige! Is the chief alright?" Peter asked, concerned.
      "He's fine, Peej. He's on vacation." P.J.'s shoulders slumped slightly at this news, relieved.
"That's good. He's getting old, and I thought..." "Nope. He's a-ok. Want a job, Peter?"
"Here?" he asked, incredulous.
      "Well, you'll have a desk in the building somewhere, but you'll be in the field, mostly."
"Detective work, Paige?"
"You catch on straight away, P.J." Paige said, grinning. "Gosh. Of course. I don't feel ready."
      "I'll put you with someone else to train. But I need someone with half a brain that I can trust."
      "I'm your guy, boss." P.J. had already shifted Paige from peer and friend to overseer. A good sign. "My half a brain, such as it is, is a loyal one."
      "Heh," Paige said. "You know what I mean. You're smart, you follow procedure, and you don't have an agenda. You'll be my inside man. Keep me informed."

"I can do that."
      "Great. There's a pay increase, a promotion in rank, and an unmarked car waiting for you in the morning. Stop by HR tomorrow at five
a.m. and then come see me. There's a detective's meeting at six."
      "Wow. I'm stunned. I can't wait to tell my wife! She hates me working the streets. She worries too much."
      "Your life's changing for the better, Pete. Detectives rarely get killed in the line of duty. Still, you'll be doing investigative work, and the people you encounter have a lot to lose, at times. Don't get complacent."
"I won't, believe me. How can I ever repay you?" P.J. asked.
      "Just do your best. You can hang out with me tomorrow and we'll learn the ropes together. When a juicy new assignment comes along, you can tag along and see what you can come up with."
      "Will do, boss." Peter extended his hand across Terry's desk, and they sealed the deal with a handshake.
Paige knew she'd made a good decision.

Chapter 28 - So It Begins
      Jean stayed up way too late that night, but he'd learned a number of very useful things. Things that moved his plans further along. It was lucky for him that he never needed more that four hours of sleep anymore, and could often get by on two.
      For one thing, one of the three purported tissue samples from Charles Manson was a fake. The triple-blind test he'd run had established that.
      The thermocycler had returned three unique signatures from four samples. Since the three samples had come from independent sources, and Jean was reasonably sure he wasn't Charles Manson, Charles Manson's father, or Valentine Michael Manson aka Pooh-Bear, Manson's son, it was safe to conclude that one tissue sample was fraudulent.
      Like any good geek, Jean was on eBay publically voicing his displeasure, and lowering the offender's rating with a lengthy diatribe backed by voluminous incontrovertible facts.
      Feeling only a tad better about being ripped off, he did some rudimentary mapping, and realized it was a blessing in disguise, of sorts. The negative result reinforced the other two, making Jean confident in the authenticity of the other two samples.
      There was a brisk trade in serial killer mementos and keepsakes, and Jean thought that if he had endless time, he could eliminate that strain of human deitrus. No matter what Howard Bloom and others postulated, Jean felt any evolutionary benefit derived from the presence of such monsters was out weighed by their negative impact on society.
      The irony was not lost on Jean. The difference was, he was going to be beneficial to society. Or, such was his belief. Rationalization or not, without being convinced of this, he could not go forward.
      Unless he was impelled by some higher power, of course. And it was really difficult to tell, after a time, where he ended and the overmind began. So he tended to dismiss questions of morality, at that point, and simply do.
      The other interesting thing Jean learned in the lab at work the next day was that Charles Manson was not a psychopath. Or, rather, not a genetic psychopath.
      Psychopaths, like homosexuals, were both born and made. One could inherit a dominant or recessive gene that would be the primary

influence on your psycho-physio make-up, which was the most common route, or either behavior could be induced via external means.
      What many hadn't realized, Jean included, was that both conditions had been imposed upon Manson at an early age.
      Actually, Manson may have been born gay or bisexual, but it was an area of study Jean had avoided. He knew others were working to isolate the genes that determined homosexuality, and that such knowledge would inevitably be abused. He saw no reason to advance research in that direction.
      Regardless, Charles Manson had been violently raped as early as age nine, and possible before that. Probably, in fact. But Manson himself retained knowledge of this event, as detailed in Ed Sanders' book 'The Family'.
      So, in all likelihood, Manson's psychopathy, also touched upon in Sanders' pivotal study, stemmed from the same events. Being raped, being raised by a whore, pimps and johns, being institionalized and dehumanized, all at early, formative stages of his life greatly contributed to his psychological make-up.
      While there were undoubtedly genetic co-factors, detailed analysis of Manson's DNA in comparison to Jean's larger collection of believed psychopathic genes led him to conclude that Manson's mindset was imposed from without, and wasn't genetic in origin.
      Strangely enough, instead of dissuading Jean from using Manson as a test subject, it strengthened his resolve. If it worked, he would be doing the man a favor. In fact, society as whole might suffer from the loss, but Jean's sympathy for his plight made him determined to end his life.
      The specifics of the virus Jean developed were brilliant, and would have made him an invaluable asset to any superpower or group of elite interested in genocide.
      It also would have gotten him killed once he had out-lived his usefulness to them, he knew. There were a huge number of biologists, virologist and genetic researchers who had died in a cluster in the past few years. A war was brewing and Jean knew he was on the side of good.
      Specifically, Jean had combined elements of Ebola, the influenza virus, and Manson's unique genetic footprint into a specialized killing machine.

      Ironically, he owed most of the credit to various government agencies and groups. They had provided Jean with the tools and research he needed to destroy them.
      From Ebola, he had isolated the organ liquefaction process, now specifically tuned to attack only the liver. Ebola had long since been believed to be lab created in the first place, much as AIDS had proven to be. According to the Congressional Record, the Department of Defense,
by way of the Army, requested ten million dollars in 1969 in order to develop a bioweapon that would attack the immune system. Naturally, the request was granted.
      Within a few years, the World Health Organization published articles stating a need for the same, on the grounds that the U.S. needed to develop a defense against such a weapon.
      According to their own literature, the WHO inoculated ten thousand children in what is now known as the AIDS belt of Africa for small pox. It is there that AIDS made its debut on the world stage.
      The WHO acknowledges that this occurred, but maintains it was a mysterious oversight, for which they cannot be held accountable.
      In the late seventies and early eighties, a program to inoculate young, promiscuous gay men with an experimental hepatitis vaccine was rolled out in major cities across America.
      These incidents, along with some work in Haiti, led to the current epidemic. It was also the reason AIDS was primarily a hetero problem in Africa, but became considered a gay disease in the U.S.
      In reality, it was an equal-opportunity bioweapon. Jean suspected Ebola was similarly developed because AIDS, a combination of a cancer- causing cattle disease spliced with the sheep-affecting visna virus, operated far too slowly.
      Which is why Jean himself selected Ebola as a precursor. It worked in days, not months or years.
      He didn't have access to the entire Ebola chain, of course, but he did have enough of it for his purposes, as a result of his unique position at work, and as a USC research fellow.
      Better yet, thousands of researchers had access to the same components, making detection unlikely.
      The influenza family was selected for its recombinant abilities, which were critical to insuring the virus would spread to its intended target,

rather than being killed shortly after it was launched.
      On the other hand, it was somewhat controllable, unlike the common cold. His biggest fear was a mutation that would get out of control and kill millions.
      Which is why the early experiment would target a specific individual.
      It was a technique he had picked up from the Israelis, who had purportedly targeted Arabs, solely. This was a significant leap forward in biowarfare, as Arabs and Ashkenazi Jews were genetically almost indistinguishable.
      Jean simply culled enough of Manson's DNA sequence until he was reasonably sure it would only be fatal to him.
Reasonably sure.
      It was a risk he had to take. Jean would be the first to carry the test case. Everything came together when he found out Manson would be making another appeal.
      Ordinarily, he only made appearances before the parole board, where he was summarily denied without consideration.
      In an unprecedented twist of fate, an upstart defense lawyer hoped to follow Melvin Belli and Johnny Cochran's shortcut route to fame by defending a celebrity against impossible odds.
      Naturally, this was all over the press. But thanks to Google News alerts, Jean had a headstart. In reality, it just moved his plans forward, and saved him a drive up north to visit the prison in which Manson resided.
      In a bigger sense, this non-coincidence only confirmed to Jean that he was carrying our God's will. He was unstoppable, a demigod wielding a fiery sword he had himself forged according to heavenly instruction.
      A prophet with attitude. Had Moses had such knowledge and resources at his disposal, the entire Bible would have been written differently.
      At the end of a long Sunday at USC, at the end of a long week, in a series of long months, Jean was ready to make the leap from theory to practice.
      He injected himself with his creation and went home to wait for symptoms.
      Two days later, he was a puking, sneezing, shitting mess, right on schedule. Jean only hoped he wasn't dying himself. He certainly felt like he

was. week.

It was definitely justification for missing work for the rest of the The next day, the day of the hearing, Jean was as sick as ever. In

fact, he felt even worse than the day before. The things we do for love, he thought. It was a strain just to get out of bed, much less get down to the
L.A. county courthouse. He managed with the help of pot, caffeine and Dayquil.
      He arrived early in order to assess the situation. Jean made inquiries while trying not to draw attention to himself. He was innocuously dressed, polite but not memorably so, and gave absolutely no indication that was sick. That was the difficult part.
      He found out that the hearing was to be held on the third floor at ten-thirty, room 346. Private hearing, of course, police, press and court employees only. Press credentials would have been ideal.
      His opportunity arose in the form of a bored young hipster guard who'd been assigned to work the door of the courtroom. Although burdened with a cop's gear, handcuffs, gun, taser, he really didn't put our cop attitude, which made him a good choice to approach. The fact that his hands touched both doorknobs constantly sealed his fate.
      When Jean got closer, he saw that this was not an ordinary cop, even by L.A's lax standards. Each forearm bore detailed shamrock tattoos, inexplicably linked to monkeys, whose tails curved around the underside of his arms, connecting the two images.
      He gave the impression of someone who was working his way through school as a security guard, and his nametag read, "I. Hayes."
      Jean went to the bathroom and sneezed heartily on both of his hands, fanning them dry, but resisting the urge to wipe them on his pants or anything else.
      He approached with what he hoped was the proper amount of deference. "Excuse me, officer...Hayes, is it?"
"Ira."
      "Ira, hi." Jean said. "Can you tell me where the civil courts are located?"
"Erm, there's no civil section, just a bunch of random courtrooms.
Let me guess, divorce?"

      Jean brightened at this new misdirection. "Right, divorce. That obvious, eh?"
      "Either that or some lawsuit, and you lack the air of a man litigious. Go down to reception, the round desk in the lobby. There's a chick with big tits, she looks a little like Monica Lewinsky, but hotter. She'll find you on the docket."
      Jean resisted the urge to ask about the Manson case, and it was difficult. He extended his hand. "Thanks a lot, Ira, I really appreciate it."
      Officer Ira Hayes extended his hand in return, and three days later, Charles Manson died in his sleep, after a bout with the flu.
Jean felt he had put the man out of his misery.

Chapter 29 - Unintended Consequences
      For the next few days, Paige's life was quiet, hectic, and fulfilling. Her first detective's meeting had gone well. One of the remaining nine dicks hadn't shown, and Paige, intimately familiar with the track records of each, summarily fired him on the spot, in absentia.
      This set the tone, and grumbling and grousing was kept to a minimum. Paige had to deal with a few catcalls and wolf whistles, at worst. She was generally well-liked in Investigative Services, and no matter what any of them thought about her, she got results.
      When HR failed to yielding any suitable replacements, Paige ran a few ads on her own, hoping to recruit from outside the force's incestuous little gene pool. Ultimately, she hired two new recruits, one from Ventura County, and another from San Diego. She didn't hold his city of origin against him, in the end.
      She really lucked out with the temp agency. They recruiter sent her an eye-popping pair of resumes. John and Lisa Chang were Chinese- American twins.
      Both had administrative assistants experience, both had strong IT backgrounds. John was a database analyst, and Lisa, a criminal justice major. They were working their way through school, and each had a spotless criminal record.
      Better yet, they performed as a single unit. Only politeness kept them from finishing each other's sentences.
      Lisa immediately began fielding phone calls, handling all of the routine paperwork, and generally took over the chief's office in his absence. Her only drawback was that she was so attractive she impeded productivity, as suddenly every officer in the force needed something signed, stamped, duplicated or mailed.
      Paige was relieved to have her, if only a tiny bit jealous. The truth of the matter was Paige was their boss around the station, and was considered one of the boys anyway. She mostly knew that, and would have spurned the attention anyway, but a deep feminine instinct tried to make her dislike Lisa, regardless.
      Paige showed John Chang what she was trying to achieve with her database, and the troubles she was having. She showed him her notes and outline, and what she'd designed in Access, so far.

      It really wasn't working for her. She'd never written anything this large and comprehensive. It encompassed timesheets, budgeting, caseloads, statistical analysis, promotions, and a lot more. Or at least Paige wanted it to. It was an ambitious project.
"Well, there are a few problems with this model, Mrs. Burner." "Please, call me Chief," Paige said, suppressing a grin.
      "Well, Chief," John said, emphasizing the word as to indicate he caught her joke despite her best effort to make it a private one. "You first of all have to normalize the database."
"Is it abnormal, now?" Paige asked, kidding.
      "Actually, yes. The first normal form dictates that each table have a key field, and no table should contain any duplicates from any other table. It's redundant, and hinders development. Normalization is the first step in writing a DB. Without it, we can't proceed."
"So, what should we do?"
      John's hand flew over the keyboard, and all of Paige's existing tables were dumped to the HP. Then he saved her work, closed it, and started a new project.
"I think we need to start over."
      Paige groaned. All of her hard work, wasted. On the plus side, she could tell John really knew what he was doing, and the department was getting critical custom software written for less than what a Burger King manager made per week.
      John's intellect reminded her of Bryce, and it was lucky for her that she wasn't particularly attracted to Asian men. Otherwise she'd have real trouble keeping her mind on her work. Throw the sister in on the deal, however... No, Paige had to stay focused.
      To her slight dismay, John wasn't the least bit distracted by her.He showed Paige how to map her ideas on the whiteboard, first.
      "A hierarchical list, or outline, while suitable for organizing your thoughts, doesn't lend itself to the non-linear nature of database design," John intoned with the patience of a seasoned lecturer. "The relationships between tables are complex, and not always readily apparent when laid out sequentially."
      Soon the whiteboard was a mass of circles and squares, each labeled with fields written below them, and a wild tornado of arrows connecting

them. It looked like ten year's worth of hurricane tracking charts, but it also made sense.
      By the end of the day, John had a functional prototype up and running, and Paige was wondering how she could convince the pair to join the force.
      P.J. was mostly in the way, and after Paige had taught him what she could about procedure she assigned him to assist a senior detective, and told him to go buy some cheap suits to be ready for his first day in the field.
He was elated.
      At the end of her third day as surrogate chief, Paige's customized new Glock arrived. From a geek standpoint, John was intrigued by the mechanism that prevented anyone else from using it, and was duly impressed.
      Lisa said she didn't know guns could look both powerful and feminine, but it clearly made her a little nervous. Maybe it was the way Paige looked at her while holding it.
      Paige couldn't wait to get out to a shooting range and break it in. She dissembled it, cleaned it, and generally fondled it at home that night. It was all she could do not to put a condom on it.
      She was getting high when the ten o'clock news came on. Charles Manson had apparently died of old age in his sleep. Paige didn't shed any tears for him, but did think he had gotten a raw deal in life, early on. Still, that was no excuse for murder, especially a pregnant woman.
      She wondered how he had survived in prison for so long, anyway. It must have been his celebrity status, she thought, which was pretty sick.
      Things were going so smoothly, Paige began to feel superfluous. The twins had readily adapted to the department, and the department had adopted them. They stayed busy and were saving for school, so they were happy to help out.
      Industrious as they were, each recognized the opportunity to enhance their resumes, and seized every chance to also expand their repertoires.
      Paige was actually relieved when two days later, there was an apparent murder-robbery in the foothills near Riverside, and no one was available to handle the crimescene.
She called P.J. and gave him the location so he could meet her there.

      The estate was massive and gated, with a thoroughly modern security system in place. There were cameras visible in outdoor Pelco housings, motion detectors, even a pair of Doberman Pinschers.
      Paige considered waiting for P.J, but she didn't want to waste a minute's time. The longer a crime scene was left unattended, the greater the chance evidence would be lost or disturbed.
      She had no confidence in the ability of a pair of uniformed officers to secure a crime scene, especially one as big as this one.
      The dead man was named Carl Cameron, and he was some sort of financial Mogul. Apparently he was out of Paige's league, which was strictly Motley Fool, MSNBC middle-tier material, augmented by her own considerable knowledge of social-historical trends and technology.
      This guy was big league, blue chips, and pricey, high performance mutual funds. A Carlysle Group investor, war profiteers whose minimum investment was fifty thousand dollars. War was always profitable for some, and full of tragic losses for the rest of us. Paige would never invest her money that way, even is she could afford to.
      But, in this guy's case, it had definitely paid off. What it hadn't done, however, was save his life. Like, lobster lady, there was no visible sign of trauma. Less, even, as this guy hadn't even hit his head.
      Paige didn't really like what she considered coroner cases, where the cause of death wouldn't be known for days. She decided to dig deeper.
      The servants all told the same story, the old man had been sick with the flu for two days, and then seemed to have gotten better. They all appeared sincere.
      The gardener was the least helpful, but Paige figured the guy had a record. The stories corroborated, but not so uniformly as the raise suspicion.
      When P.J. arrived, Paige left him to take witness statements while she explored the house and grounds.
      She wandered more or less without purpose. The house was historic, nearly one hundred years old, and had a vaguely claustrophobic feel, and was decorated as if by a weird old aunt.
      She made her way down to the basement level, where there were more copper pipes than she imagined were in a lot of buildings. Paige counted six four-inch lines. Someone had definitely oversold this guy on the plumbing and HVAC.

      She knew a little bit about the cost of copper and the associated installation from her family, and oddly enough, that gave her a more real sense of the wealth she was dealing with than the d�cor, which she found to be somewhat tacky.
      She elected to go upstairs, and then work her way back down, looking for anything amiss.
      The body was still warm, and Paige didn't see anything that looked out of place. No jewelry boxes, safes or collectibles seem to have been disturbed. The servants would probably have prevented each other from committing crimes of opportunity.
      She briefly examined what seemed like a hundred rooms on her way back down, each as cramped and over-decorated as the last. It was a veritable museum of bad taste and cramped construction. Paige would have gutted the interior, torn out half of the walls and gone ultra-modern, just to exorcise the lace doily demons that obviously dwelled there.
      She eventually made her way back to the ground floor, and spent time in the property manager's office, kitchen and garage before walking the front entrance hallway back to the study where the body lay.
By then, Paige had cause to arrest the three servants for grand theft.
      Fearing the gardener would run or commit violence in an attempt to escape, she drew her new handgun with the mouthless cartoon cat engraved on the pink palm rest.
      "Cuff 'em Peter," Paige said when she entered the room. Panic spread over the faces of the females, the maid and property manager, but the gardener remained impassive.
With Paige's .45 fixed on him, he made no attempt to flee.
"Where are the paintings?" Paige asked, and the maid began crying.
      They had made some attempt to disguise it, but Paige noticed the outlines in the main hall where two large picture frames, and a cluster of smaller ones had hung for years.
      The screws had been removed from the walls, and the resultant holes filled in, but the wood behind where the paintings sat told the tale, being a shade or two darker than the bare walls.
The maid continued her emotional outburst.
"I dinna wanna steal them. They say for me to be quiet." "Again, where are they?"
"In the study, una the sofa."

      In the study, Paige knelt down two feet from the corpse and looked under the couch. She pulled out two large picture frames, and a series of smaller ones.
      The smaller set contained photos and letters of someone Paige recognized as Che Guevara, the Cuban revolutionary leader.
      The first large frame was a photo of a stern old man Paige didn't recognize. She later found out it was one of only three photographs of industrialist J.P. Morgan in the entire world.
The last was both stunning and heart breaking.
      It was a photo of Marilyn Monroe that had never been seen by the public. She was dressed as glamorously as always but she was clearly drunk and high on pills. She was practically drooling. The pain of a little girl lost was all over her face. A tragic picture worth far more than a thousand words.
It was also worth a small fortune to private collectors, no doubt.
      Paige returned to where P.J. and the uniformed officers had the three suspects handcuffed and corralled.
"So where's the statuette?" Paige asked no one in particular.
      "You took the Rodin, you asshole?" the property manager hissed at the gardener, who said nothing.
      Paige had heard enough. She unlocked the maid's cuffs, and explained that although she would be brought in for questioning, she probably wouldn't be charged with the other two. As far as she was concerned, the maid had been threatened and coerced into cooperating after the fact.
The uniformed officers led the three away to waiting squad cars. Paige called for the coroner and an investigative back up team.
      Two hours later, they retrieved an original piece by the sculptor Rodin form the bottom of a shallow fishpond. It was estimated to be worth
$350,000, and probably would have fetched half of that on the black market.
      Paige showed Peter the dust-free square on the coffee table where the pedestal of the statuette had previously sat. They took photographs of this and the spots where the photos and picture frames had hung.
      For good measure, they also got samples of the plaster, still wet, that were to cover-up the theft, and eventually found what they believed to be the actual screws that were removed from the wall.

      Forensics would match the wood from the wall to the screws, Paige knew. Although she felt good about the case, she didn't have the chief to call for praise, so it was a little anti-climatic, for her.
P.J. was duly impressed with her prowess, at least.
      The duo were charged with criminal conspiracy and grand theft, but Paige didn't get the impression that murder was a part of the crime, so the charge was withheld, pending a report from the coroner's office.
      Peter and Paige returned to the office to find the Chang twins running the show in a model of efficiency.

Chapter 30 - Onward and Upward
      Jean was still recovering from his custom flu virus when news of Manson's death was splattered across the major news channels. The media loved it.
      The media loved anything that distracted people from the war and the economy, and how the two problems were intrinsically linked. With this, they had a field day.
      The story had had it all; sex, murder, drugs, nostalgia, revenge. Vincent Bugliosi, all pomp and smugness, gloated over the death of his imagined adversary, oblivious to the fact of how shamelessly he himself had exploited the tragedy for his own personal benefit.
      Sharon Tate's aging mother said Manson's death was delivered by God himself, revenge for the brutal death of her daughter and others. Jean thought she was half right, but a little misguided all the same, because of the thirty-nine year delay between the murders and Manson's death.
      Somewhere in Washington State, a rechristened Valentine Michael Manson was recovering from the flu, and didn't shed a tear for the man who had irresponsibly sired and then abandoned him. Pooh Bear, now Christopher Waldo Emerson, would be visited upon by the sins of his father for the rest of his life. Roman Polanski elected to make no statement of any kind.
      The test had been a smashing success all around, in Jean's mind. Society was uplifted by the news that justice had been belatedly delivered, however misguided the notion. A kindred spirit had been releases from this mortal coil, no longer imprisoned by the society and institutions that had created and betrayed him.
      And, best of all, the viral formula had worked. It had worked a little too well, actually. The CDC was issuing reports of a new flu strain that was sweeping the country.
      As far as Jean could tell, only Manson had died from liver failure, but some seventeen elderly or otherwise infirmed people died in the U.S. before the test case burned itself out in the Midwest.
      Survival of the fittest, Jean thought. Besides, it gave him the opportunity to study transmission rates and infection patterns. It was all additional research material he would incorporate into the next test case, as well as his planned third and final iteration.

      Jean named the virus Captain Trips, in homage to the engineered virus in Stephen King's 'The Stand'.
      The next version was to be the most critical. Rather than targeting a specific individual, this batch would attack those bearing the so-called fascist gene.
      The problem, at this point, was one of communicability. It was critical, at that point, that the virus not spread beyond Jean's intended victims.
      For one thing, Jean had no desire to kill more innocent people, no matter if they were near death on their own or not.
      More to the point, he didn't want to unleash this unprecedented plague without letting the world know exactly what was happening and why.
      He developed the next batches in parallel, sharply limiting the communicability of one, while enhancing the other. The first was to be a surgical strike, an opening salvo, intended to send a message.
      The second, and last, was to be a pandemic, a global holocaust of biblical proportions.
      Jean had no plans to tamper with Mother Nature beyond that. If he got away with it, the entire planet would be changed forever. In his mind, it would be a great leap toward paradise on Earth.
      When Jean felt he had his new formulas perfected, he hand-drafted a message to the world: "End all tyranny, over the minds of men. Restore the Republic, and human freedom. Keep your laws off of our bodies. Man- made laws made man lost. Renounce oppression immediately, or face the wrath of Captain Trips. - Messiah."
      He knew this was wasted effort, merely a symbolic gesture toward freedom. Those who would limit human rights would never voluntarily relinquish their control.
      It would, however, strike fear in their hearts, and implant hope in those with the moral and intellectual capacity to desire the most freedom for the greatest possible number of people.
      Jean knew many stood at the ready waiting for such a situation to arise. They would seize the reins of power from the hands of the fallen, and steer the world back to a far less totalitarian future.
      He wondered if men at large deserved freedom, seeing as how the majority had rejected it, and instead seemed to have embraced oppression

of their spirit, flourished under it, even.
      It was the eternal struggle, Jean knew. At least humans might gain a few centuries of freedom, and a headstart against future repressions.
      Ponerology and psychopathy would certainly get a lot more attention after this. As it currently stood, books on ponerology were routinely stolen from university library shelves, and probably destroyed.
      Apparently, some of these monsters were fully aware of what they were, and feared detection. As messiah, Jean would shift the balance away from this secret brotherhood.
He took pains to cover his tracks with his message to the media.
      Jean knew modern printers had embedded signatures to help track counterfeiters, kidnappers and the like. In Jean's case, the solution was simple.
      He purchased an ancient dot matrix printer from a Commodore 64 user group swap meet. Jean was just another anonymous geek in a crowd of some thirty eight-bit enthusiasts.
      Better, yet, it came with a half of a box of yellowing tractor paper. He had to use DOSbox, an MS-DOS emulator, to actually get the printer to work, but Jean printed three copies, with only slightly different wordings.
      The idea was to insure it was printed by at least one major newspaper by distributing it to three of them. It would be too easy for an editor, or perhaps the FBI, to squash one copy, in an effort to avoid a panic.
      When he was satisfied with the printouts, Jean burned the remaining paper and the box in his BBQ grill on his back patio, and then scattered the ashes.
      The printer was painstakingly dissembled by hand, each screw removed one by one. Every component ended up in dumpsters in various small towns in the area. If Jean got caught, it wouldn't be due to carelessness. He used the facilities at work to sterilize the printouts, which had only been handled with gloves, anyway.
At last, Jean felt he was ready for phase two of the operation.
      He had an inkling that his next move would bring the heavies to town. Obscurity was his primary advantage, currently. The gene sequence he was targeting was as of yet undiscovered. It would only be a matter of time, though, before the big boys took issue with him infringing on their territory.

      So it was going to be a race, from this point on. They couldn't very well inoculate all of the world's leaders, captains of industry, majors, generals, and heads of state. All they could do is try and stop him before the third vial was broken, to borrow some imagery.
      That weekend Jean booked a hotel suite in San Diego, checked in, then headed for San Francisco.
      On the drive up, he swung by Pookie's in a deep blue rented Cadillac.
"What's up, cousin?" Pook asked.
"Not much, my man. On my way up north, and I wanted to talk

metal."


"Like, Slayer and shit?"
"Peace through superior firepower."
"Word? Maxxy said you're kind of a nerd. That's pretty hip. A

weed-smokin, gun-slingin, white boy who's not a wigger, with advanced degrees. That's a lethal combo."
"I just want a little added insurance in my life. I have no plans to use
it."
"Would you like automatic insurance, semi-automatic or manual?"
Pookie asked.
      "I was thinking .380, double-action, perhaps one that wouldn't involve some tedious registration process and waiting period."
"Damn. You need me to forge a concealed carry permit, too?"
      Jean laughed. "I hope to never get popped with it," he said. "It's just for special occasions."
      "Hey man, that yo' business. I'm just trying to cover all the bases. Explore the options. Provide an enjoyable one-stop shopping experience. By the way, how was your freak out?"
      "Oh, that, it was...real crazy. Hard to describe. Thanks for coming through. Wild stuff."
"I know. I did some two weekends ago..." "Were there..." Jean started to ask.
      "Snake? Yeah. Big motherfuckers, little ones, some whales or dinosaurs or something, too."
"Did your head split open?"
      "You know this," Pookie replied. "I got snakes in my head like a bad Samuel Jackson movie. That's why I know I can trust you, fully. It's really

helped me in my current line of work...a few bad apples have been plucked."
      Pookie dialed his cell phone, which Jean noted was an entirely different make and model from the last time he was here. "Percy," Pook said, "Want to play checkers?"
      Jean thought Percy was a funny name for a thug, but he kept this to himself. Which was just as well. Percy turned out to be nearly seven feet tall, and of a build that looked like he could rip Mike Tyson's head clean off.
      He pointedly ignored Jean completely as he and Pookie talked business in low whispers.
Percy turned to Jean. "Gimme three fiddy."
      Jean could only think of Chef from 'South Park,' and very nearly laughed out loud. Instead, he maintained his game face, and dished four bills into Percy's gigantic hand.
      Percy then left without another word to either of them. Jean knew by now to leave his car unlocked. Any car parked in front of Pookie's was protected, anyway. It was safer here than in a police impound lot.
After ten minutes of chit-chat, Pook said, "Ok. You're straight." "Thanks, as always, Uncle Pook," Jean said.
"Just be careful with that pea-shooter."
      "I will, man. Maxxine's coming in less than two months to visit. No way I'd miss that."
      When Jean was near the Ventura county line he stopped for coffee and checked out the alligator skin briefcase on his seat.
      Three hundred fifty dollars bought enormous value in the underground economy. Inside was an ivory-handled pistol that was more than likely never fired before, nested in a red velvet lining. It was loaded, with two more clips and a full box of ammo besides.
      Jean was loaded for bear. He turned the gun over in the light and saw that the serial number was scratched off.
Very nice, indeed.

Chapter 31 - No Such Thing as Coincidence
      The next day Paige kept P.J. at the office to take him the rest of the way through a murder investigation. She started by making him do all of the paperwork.
      John Chang was converting all of the forms to editable PDF format, but for now his sister had grouped the necessary forms into individual packets. That alone saved time and eliminated errors. And in this line of work, a simple procedural mistake could mean a killer went free, in a worst- case scenario.
      She let P.J. work opposite her on her desk while John and Lisa deconstructed police procedure in the chief's office. Occasionally, he had a question, but for the most part he was able to do it on his own, which was a good sign.
      Currently, John was creating a subset of the database that was of his own devising. He said it was patterned after FogBugs, a bug-tracking database used by some programmers.
      Essentially it had a public and private face. Detectives could access a portion of it via the web, at home or in the office, and contribute to the knowledge base of a particular case. This part was password protected and searchable, so cases, victims and witnesses could be cross-referenced.
      Paige could already see how useful this would soon become, and could imagine it one day helping to solve a case.
      The other half of this modified bug-tracking concept was a public face. John got with IT and was given server access so that he could write a web front end for it. Paige thought this meant HTML, but no, John said it was actually PHP-Nuke, and she was in no position to learn the nuances right now.
      John assured her it was kid's stuff, and she'd be able to modify it in the future. She glanced at the source over his shoulder once, and was skeptical.
      The public end of it was different, again. Regular people couldn't be allowed access to the details of murder investigations, for obvious reasons.
      The public information, such as was available from the newspaper, provided the framework. Citizens could flip through pending investigations, and provide information either by name, or anonymously. Either way, tipsters were anonymized by giving them unique PIN numbers.

      John and Paige agreed that anonymity could encourage some individuals to contribute more candidly. Once their personal information was entered, the users only used a PIN system, so even they maintained the appearance of anonymity, a neat psychological trick.
      Of course, on the backend, these details were weighted according to whether the person who contributed gave contact information, etcetera. Paige knew that as often as not, entirely anonymous tips were the ones that could make or break a case.
      It was open to human error and fraud, but so were non-computerized tips. John planned to implement a statistical analysis program on the back end to rate the effectiveness of each piece of information, and maintained that the gathered stats could eventually predict potential false tips.
      Paige wasn't clear on how that would work, but at this point John was a saint, and his words were gospel. She only hoped the chief would grasp the intrinsic value of the work they were doing.
He was due back Friday, Monday at the latest.
      She felt pretty good about things overall. She obtained the budget information for the year, and even with P.J's promotion and associated expenses from the start, and the Chang kids, Paige was well under budget for the year.
      At the same time, the ratio of solved to unsolved murders was creeping up ever so slowly, and she hoped it wasn't a fluke, but rather the beginning of an ongoing trend.
      What she and John were currently collaborating on was the piece de resistance: a dashboard display frontend for her and the chief. Actually, two of them. The IS portion, which she thought of as hers, got the most attention.
      At a glance, a single screen displayed all of the relevant details of her department: budget vs. projections, win-loss ratio, caseload, personnel. It allowed you to drill down indefinitely, or just get from it a general sense of how the department was doing. Brilliant, if she did say so herself.
      Paige was juggling numbers in an effort to see what sort of offer she could make in order to induce the twins to stay on the payroll when Lisa buzzed her from the next room.
"Paige, Mr. Rogers at the coroner's office would like a word with
you."

      God, she's hot, Paige thought. I wonder if she likes Maggie Gyllenhaal and James Spader? She dialed the extension of the coroner, the inaptly named Mr. Rogers.
"Dave, how's it going?"
      "Good, Paige. Well, not good, but weird. Which is good, to me. I think you might want to come down here to my office instead of reading the report."
"Gotcha," Paige said, and hung up.
      David Rogers was an anomaly, a coroner who wasn't creepy. He was somewhat effeminate and had bad breath, but more importantly, wasn't creepy.
      Most coroners, Paige had found, were definitely creepy. It came with the territory. You had to be a little off to even want to work with dead bodies, Paige reflected. That applied even to herself.
      But Dave didn't give off the air of a man who molested dead children or something, as most of them did. He was more like a doctor with a lousy track record, trying to figure out where he went wrong.
      If you had to have some guy cutting you naked carcass apart, you could do worse than to have Mr. Rogers at the helm. Even his name was reassuring.
She found him in his kitchenette, waiting for the microwave. "Want some soup?" he asked.
      Paige shook her head no, politely. He ate the most wretchedly unhealthy meals. She wondered where he got his recipes.
      Dave ignored her as he prepared what seemed to be a delicious gourmet meal, in his mind. "Don't mind me," he said "I'll just me a moment."
      He had an array of ingredients set before him on the counter, in what seemed to be a deliberate parody of a classy TV cooking show. But Dave took it all too seriously Paige could tell.
      From the microwave, he removed his bowl containing two packs of ramen noodles. To this, he added a handful of BBQ Fritos, two flavor packets (beef and shrimp, she noted - gross), a pile of crushed-up Cheetos, several spoons of heavily processed chili that actually came out of a bag, and an entire cup of nacho cheese.
      He stirred these together quickly and began to consume it with obvious delight.

      Paige couldn't imagine eating anything that was so salty and bad for you, much less wolfing it down like a starving person. She counted no less than five sources of MSG and sodium and shuttered.
"Dave," she had to ask, "Where did you learn to cook?" "What? Oh, this?" he indicated with his spoon. "In prison."
      Paige let the entire subject rest. There was a lot she didn't know about Dave, clearly.
      David ate with a dignified air, he just did it rapidly. Knowing a little about his background, now, that made a bit more sense.
      When he had finished, he washed his plastic bowl and disposable spoon, dried them and put them both away in a cabinet well-stocked with ramen and chips.
      "Never again," he muttered to himself, and Paige knew he was talking about jail. She deduced that his diet was a way of reminding himself daily of the horrors his time in the pen must have held for one so obviously intelligent and sensitive as he.
      "Detective Burner, I warn you in advance, things are about to get weird around here," Dave began. "It may be said that this wizard of Wall Street you dragged in here died of natural causes. It may also be argued that he was killed. The problem is, skilled though I am, I cannot be considered knowledgeable enough to make such a determination."
"Really?" Paige asked rhetorically.
      "There was no trauma, no trace of poisons, nothing to indicate foul play, in the traditional sense. However, the cause of death is one I've never encountered before. His liver seems to have been liquefied. Often," he said wryly, "that can lead to death."
"Liquefied?" Paige asked sharply.
      "Liquefied," Dave said, belaboring the point for Paige's benefit. "Turned to liquid. Made into mush. Reduced to its component elements. This liver has ceased to be."
      Paige realized Dave was performing Monty Python's dead parrot sketch, but she wasn't amused.
"And there's no medical precedent for this that you know of?" she
asked.
"Oh, sure there is. It's called Ebola. Only it's highly contagious, and
attacks multiple organs. Within a few days all of your insides melt. Nasty stuff."

"But this is Ebola, right?"
      "My dear, if it were, twenty-five percent of L.A. would be dead, myself included."
"So, what could it be?"
"That, Detective Burner, is the question that resides at the top of the
$25,000 pyramid. But wait, there's more." "More?"
      "Much more. I did some checking. We California coroners tend to gossip. Talk shop, call it what you will. We lead lonely, solitary lives. Only other coroners understand us. Consequently, we have a little network online where we have private group discussions. Guess, Paige, who just so happened to have died from the exact same malady?"
"I have no idea, Dave." Paige answered. "Guess!" Dave urged her.
"Um, Uma Thurman?" she said, not really trying. "Not even close, Paige. Charles Manson."
      Paige was more than a little startled by this news. Had he said almost anyone else, she would have been less surprised.
"Holy shit," Paige exclaimed, "What do you make of that?"
      "Well, me and my friend have been talking. I realized this is a bit esoteric. But the general consensus around here is that we're looking at biowarfare."
"What?"
      "A custom-tailored disease, Paige. Such things exist, of course. Or, can exist, rather. If they do, they're in secret government labs, or deep with the stores of private corporations, waiting to be unleashed upon us. So, accept my premise, if you will, and bear with me."
"Sure," Paige said.
      "So, we have two men dead from an undiscovered disease, which I believe was manmade. They are the only two men in history, to my knowledge, who have died in such a manner.
      So, given that, why would these two men be targeted for termination, given their disparate positions at the ends of the social spectrum?"
      "Um," Paige began, "I know some people want Manson dead, understandably, but I don't think they'd have the ability to engineer a custom virus. As far as the rich guy, I guess I can see that happening, at a

stretch. Still seems unlikely, though. But those super-rich get caught up in some wild stuff, at times, I heard."
"Indeed," Rogers said. "But you're missing the point, Paige." "How's that?"
"What would be the connection between the two?" "Yeah, that's a good point. I'm not sure, honestly."
      "Genetics, Paige. If we're looking at something engineered, and only two people died from it, they must share a common bond. In other words-"
      "In other words, Carl Cameron might be Charles Manson's father," Paige concluded.
"Exactly."

Chapter 32 - Soup's On
      Jean was cooking up a very special batch of virus. This was to be his final test case before unleashing the completed disease on the world.
Except it wasn't a disease. It was the cure.
      This iteration of Jean's righteous plague was going to cause quite a stir. It was short-lived, but highly communicable. And it targeted the fascist gene.
      He was so confident in his work that he produced two batches. The second was identical, except it had no pre-determined lifespan. This was the true Captain Trips, the world's savior.
      Having accomplished the heavy lifting, Jean set about planning the release of version two. He needed a site with sparse population, a probability of numerous psychopaths, and one that was relatively isolated, so it didn't spread out of control.
      While he pondered this, running various searches to find the perfect spot, he called Maxine.
"Hey!" she said.
"Hi, love. How's school?"
      "Good, good," she said, hand resting on her protruding stomach. "But I'm thinking of sitting out next semester."
      "Oh no!" he said. "That'll delay your graduation by four months, counting graduation ceremony. How come?"
"Oh, I dunno. Ennui, I guess. Don't worry, nothing I can't handle." "Are you sure? I could come visit you."
      "I'm fine, Jeaner. Really. But, I'd love to see you. It's just...I don't know. A bad time."
"I love you Maxine. You know that, right?" "I know, Jean. I love you, too. So much."
"If you change your mind, let me know, ok? I'll come running." "I will, boo."
      "Say, Maxine. Lots of people like different places. Is there any place you don't like?"
Maxine thought about this for a while.
      "Colorado ant never done shit for me," she said. "Buncha white folks, no offense, too damn cold and rich. Why?"
      "Ok, no reason. Just curious. I wouldn't want to take you on a honeymoon you'd hate."

"Oh, Jean. You're so damn sweet."
"I know. So are you. That's why we're perfect for each other." "We really are." She brightened.
"You know I think about you all the time, right?" "Do you? I love you. I do too."
"You think of yourself all the time?" she teased.
      "Heh. You, Maxine Jackson, occupy a disproportionate chunk of my frontal lobe. How's the coding?"
"Oh, good. I'm an iPhone dev, now. It looks lucrative." "Wow, that's a big step forward."
"Yeah! I couldn't have done it without you."
      "Stuff and nonsense. You had the framework. You just have to cut through the professor's convoluted explanations. Truth be told, most of them can't code their way out of a paper bag."
      "I'm starting to pick up on that. Those who can, code. Those who can't, teach others to code?"
Jean laughed.
      "Yeah, it's a shitty system. I've found most learning that occurs at college is self-directed. There are a lot of four point ohs out there that don't know anything."
"And a bunch of dropouts who are brilliant," Maxine added.
      "Look, I'm serious about seeing you, Maxxy. I can't believe we've waited this long."
"I know. But the next time we're together I want it to be forever." "It will be, angel. I love you so much."
"I love you, too. Gotta go, honey." "Ok, I guess, if you have to."
"I really do."
"Alright. I love you, Maxine." "I know, Jean, I love you, too." "Bye," Jean said.
"Bye," she replied. "I love you."
"Ok, talk to you later." "Ok. You hang up first." "No, you hang up first." "We'll hang up together."

"Ok, One..." "Two..." "Three."
"I love you," Maxine said.
"Aargh!" Jean said. "I love you, Maxine. Talk to you later." "Ok, bye."
Click.
They hung up together.
      Jean felt concern over Maxxy, and her reluctance to discuss her problem with him, but there was no point in pushing her for answers. She was nothing if not independent. What other woman would wait an entire year for him, if necessary?
      Instead of dwelling on something he couldn't change, he selected a small ski town, Deer Valley, Colorado, and made plans to visit there soon.
      It made sense in a lot of ways. The high-income bracket of the vacationers would guarantee a decent number of psychopaths, and the isolation of the area helped to insure a limited geographical impact.
      The fact that Maxine somehow didn't care for Colorado helped seal the deal. Because, come what may, Jean would never forget his adoration for her above anything else in life.
      He was packing a travel bag and feeling clever when his house phone rang.
"Hello?"
      "Hi. Dr. Jean Splicer? Detective Paige Burner. Do you have a few minutes?"
"Detective?" he asked.
      "Oh, right. Sorry, I neglected to mention I'm a cop. It kinda scares guys off."
"Do tell," he said.
"Anyway, I have a case I'd like to discuss with you, if you have the

time."


"Should I bring a lawyer?" Jean asked, in all seriousness.
"What? No, silly. I need your expertise. I have a hot case, and it's

outside of my field of discipline."
      "Well, I was about to drive down to USC for the week, but I can put that off for a day. But only because I find you intriguing."

      "That I am. But there may be an expert witness position in it for you if you play your cards right."
"I'd rather not, if it can be avoided. I sort of have my hands full." Jean looked down at the third vial of Captain Trips he was holding. "What's this about, exactly?" he asked.
      "Well, this is sort of off the books, which is why I'm contacting you privately. Biowarfare."
"What?" Jean yelped. He tried to keep the rising panic out of his
voice.
"Yeah, I know. It's crazy. By the way, your supply house friend gave
up your number, once I assured him he wasn't in any legal jeopardy and neither were you."
      "Hmmm. On one hand, I have nothing to hide. On the other hand, I should find another supplier on general principle."
      "Awwww. Then I'd never get to see you. It's not his fault, I leaned on him pretty heavily." Paige said.
"I'm sure you could subpoena me," Jean quipped. "Or handcuff you..."
"Yow. So where do you want to meet?"
      "There's a little restaurant in Pasadena, Pepper's. Is seven o'clock good for you?"
"Pepper's. Pasadena. Seven. Sure, I'll see you there."
      Jean hung up and realized he was sweating. He wasn't a suspect, or she wouldn't have handled it like that, he knew.
Still, it was far too fateful to be ignored. He decided to play it cool.
Perhaps he could extract useful information from her, instead.
      His hands were shaking. He had to remember that according to Ayahuasca, she wasn't the enemy. But it was endgame, and things were moving forward so quickly now, he'd have to be extra careful.
      Jean finished packing his bags, then made arrangements to take a week off of work to conduct research at USC on his own time. He'd found an undergrad willing to sign him in and out of the labs a few times during the week to provide himself a cover story.
      He also decided to leave for Colorado right after dinner with the petite, strawberry detective.

Chapter 33 - Dinner at Pepper's
      For the life of her, Paige couldn't decide what to wear. She wasn't sure whether to classify Jean as a potential suitor or an expert witness. Or a suspect.
It was a crazy notion, one that refused to leave her mind completely.
Currently, the entire field of gene manipulation was suspect.
      But the fact was she had no suspects. She reminded herself not to twist the facts to fit the available people involved in the case.
      Ultimately, she tried to select an outfit that worked under all possible circumstances. She chose a charcoal-gray skirted business ensemble with black trim. The skirt nearly reached her knees, which was actually a bit longer than she wanted to wear.
      Paige found herself spending more time on her make-up than she ordinarily would have. Still, she was ahead of schedule so she smoked a bowl of blendo, and then immediately regretted it. The food at Pepper's was excellent, and the portions generous. To go to dinner there with the munchies was a surefire plan for gluttony.
      She went over her notes for the case. Paige had a lot of questions, but she wanted to keep dinner casual. Pulling out notepads and index cards tended to make people more guarded, even when they had nothing to hide.
      Confident that she had a handle on things, she phoned ahead to the restaurant.
"Pepper's" a voice said. "Hi, Toni, it's Paige."
"Paige Burner! I haven't seen you in months. Are you coming in?" "I am, actually. I wanted to see if you could hold a booth for me in
the back."
"Oooh. Hot date?" Toni asked.
"A hot case. I mean, he's cute, but this is a working dinner." "I see. Well, maybe I'll take a crack at him," Toni said.
      Twenty-six year-old Toni Pepper was a notorious tease. She was the hostess at Pepper's, and had been since she was ten years old. The restaurant was owned by her father, Antoine Pepper, who was also a doctor.
      Paige suspected that Toni, who was just on the other side of voluptuous, was still a virgin. Or at least a technical virgin.
"How's your dad?" Paige asked.

      "Good." Toni said brightly. "He's been falling down a lot, but no major problems."
"Falling down? Why?"
"No idea. Doctors," Toni said. "Doctors," Paige said in agreement. "So, when are you coming?"
"I'm supposed to be there in...crap! I'm on my way." "Byee."
      She grabbed her clutch and started out the door. She caught herself running and stopped. She wasn't late, she was anxious to see Jean again.
      She was trying to be professional about this, and failing. There was just something about him that she really liked, however indefinable it may be.
      But her feelings seemed to be unrequited. It was the story of her life. Why did one person always have to care more than the other? And why was she always the one who cared more?
      There was never any way to avoid it. But love sometimes felt like a pitcher plant or a venus fly trap. You were drawn in, feeling safe and secure, and then through some reversal you were suddenly crushed and in pain.
      Love was the bait, and the outcome was always the same. At least in Paige's life so far, that was the case.
      Human nature being what it was, she remained hopeful. But each time, she became more wary and less trusting. She feared that she would one day be unable to love anymore. And what then? A life without love surely wasn't worth living.
      She pushed these thoughts away as she drove. She loved the wind in her hair, and the feeling of power and freedom driving gave her.
      Even then, she wondered what it would be like to be a passenger, driven by another. She wanted to be safe and secure. Loved forever. Was such a thing possible in this day and age?
It seemed the stuff of fairy tales.
      Paige felt her throat constricting and her eyes welling. Could love take away the pain in her heart, or was it the cause? She found herself pulling into the parking lot at Pepper's, with no real memory of driving there. I need a drink, she thought.
      There were no other Chargers present and she felt another pang of disappointment, Irrational, but she had hoped to see him in the parking lot

again.
There were a few motorcycles, and for a moment she wondered if he
possibly drove one. She had always felt any man she settled down with would ride, with her on the seat behind him.
      But they wouldn't settle down. She wanted her life to be a grand adventure, full of surprises and excitement. Paige Burner was dying to live.
      She chirped her alarm and turned to go inside, when head lights illuminated her silhouette in the darkening parking lot.
      Her heart skipped a beat, and she chided herself for being silly. But it was Jean, and he pulled into the spot next to hers, recreating their first meeting rather perfectly.
Great minds really do think alike.
      She stood silently and watched him emerge from his vehicle. Their eyes met and she watched his expression brighten at the sight of her, and she was sure she was doing the same.
      When he approached her, Paige fought an overwhelming urge to run into his arms.
      "I always thought the dashing knight would arrive on a white charger," she said, smiling.
      "Alas, m-lady, yon steed was all ye olde dealer had in stock on that fateful day."
Paige giggled at this, her worries forgotten.
      Jean extended his elbow to her as a matter of course, and they walked arm-in-arm to the door, which he held open for her.
      A black knight is better than none at all, she thought. Or gray, at the very least.
"Paige!" Toni greeted them.
"Hey, Toni!" Paige replied. "Good to see you."
"I know," Toni said. "It's been forever. Who's your friend?" Jean turned to look at Paige.
"Toni Pepper, meet Dr. Jean Splicer." "Oooh, a doctor," Toni said.
      "Mad doctor," Jean corrected. "Enchanted," he said as he kissed her hand. "Any friend of Paige..."
"Down boy," Paige said, adding to Toni's delight. Without another word, she led them to their booth. "Have a great time," she told them.

When she was far out of earshot Paige said, "Well, that was mean." "What?" Jean said. "Mean? How?"
      "You leading her on. Getting her hopes up. You have no intention of doing her. You're a tease."
      "I was being a gentleman. And it's not that I wouldn't, even though I prefer a size -" he conspicuously eyed Paige up and down, "-six. But since I've met Maxine, I've become a one-woman man for the first time ever."
"Is it hard?" Paige asked.
