Chapter 34 - Cold Virus
      Jean's hands were shaking as he pulled onto Interstate 15. He never needed to get high so bad in his entire life. He reached under his seat and pulled out his stash box.
      He selected a bomber joint of Northern Lights, and dumped two Valium tens into his palm, swallowing them without water.
      As soon as he did it he considered what he was doing. He was never going to make it to Colorado on Valium. So, on top of those he ate an Adipex, the most heavily-regulated pharmaceutical speed there was, short of medical grade methamphetamine.
      Finally, he lit the joint and let the wind speak to him. He could see himself driving, somehow. Disassociation was a phenomenon he'd read about, but had never experienced. It didn't seem possible, but there it was.
      He watched himself smoke and drive, captivated by every nuance of his actions. He flicked ashes out the window. He scratched his chin. Each mundane move he made was fascinating to watch.He only hoped his tattered psyche would hold together long enough to allow him to complete his life's work.
      Yet, he knew it would. It was all pre-destined, written in black and white since the dawn of existence. He gave no thought beyond the culmination of his mission. He had no firm belief that he would survive. Such was martyrdom. To cling to his own petty mortality would be a rejection of the divine.
      Jean was the Japanese kamikaze pilot. He was the plane itself, and the ocean. He felt the serenity of the pilot dying in service to the creator. He felt the consuming flames engulf him. He looked down, this time with his own eyes, and realized he was in the burning tip of the roach that hung from his lips.
"Woah," he said
      He remained in his own skull for the rest of the trip, driving straight through to Deer Valley, Colorado. The first time the Charger's gas gauge showed a quarter tank, Jean got the strangest sensation. He got the strong impression that he no longer needed fuel. God, he felt, would get him where he needed to go.
      But as he passed an isolated gas station, "The last gas for 100 miles," the sign declared, he felt foolish and turned around.

      God helps those who help themselves, he reminded himself. Providence and science were not mutually exclusive, but two halves of the same whole. There's your unified field theory, he mused. Good luck getting it published.
      Several hundred miles after he began to veer north, the snows came. Jean kicked himself for lack of foresight on his part. There was little he could do but reduce his speed and plunge on ahead.
      Eventually, the roads were too steep and treacherous for even Jean's unshakable faith. He found a tire dealer, who sold and installed a set of snow chains.
      While he waited, he went to a sporting goods store and bought an anorak, thermal coveralls, battery powered socks, and snow boots. His hands hesitated over a set of snow shoes that looked suspiciously like badminton rackets, and then he grabbed them as well.
Nothing was going to stop him from completing his mission.
      For all of these items he paid cash, likewise for his gas. He did worry a bit when the salesperson at he tire store asked for his name, make and license plate number.
      Then he realized the smug clerk, so warm and dry, would never check, and so Jean gave him entirely false information on all counts.
      Around one a.m. he couldn't drive any further. The drifts were piling up, occasionally as high as the hood. The best laid plans of mice and men, he thought. At the same time realizing it hadn't been his best laid plan to begin with.
      He parked on the shoulder, or what he hoped was the shoulder. It was more or less impossible to tell, at that point.
      He remained hopeful that the road would be cleared in the morning, and so he plugged the nine-volt batteries into his socks and downed his cold water.
      Another factor, aside from the fact that the Adipex was wearing off, was that he didn't want to be remembered as the guy who walked in during a blizzard in the middle of the night.
Astute lodge clerks tend to notice things like that.
      He slept off and on. And, oh did the great winds howl. And talk. But he set his alarm for six hours of sleep, no more. He would walk toward the rising sun at golden dawn.

      His dreams that night were extremely vivid, and he got the impression that sleep, not orgasm, was the little death. Where you cannot account for your time spent.
      He had visions of another reality, a hellish one that was like the worst of Las Vegas all the time. Jean came to see that although theologically, he could dismiss the concept of hell and devil, the reality was, there was a hell.
      Multiverse theory tells us that each decision we make creates another reality, the option not taken. Hell was the one reality in which everyone was at their worst.
      Hell was not for the dead. Unless the dead were sent to a place called Earth. Hell was a spiritual condition.
      Thus heaven and hell coexisted around the globe, around the clock. People in hell rubbed off a little bit on the people who weren't, and, not in hell, they shone a little light into the lives of those who were.
      Or such were Jean's thoughts as he mixed a cold cup of extra strong coffee on the morning of his fateful second test.
      He carried with him a bottle of water, beef jerky, and a vial of Captain Trips, version two point oh six.
      The oh six came from a few modifications he made to his design before he did the splicing. He was still toying with the lifespan of the virus, and equally ran the risk of it being too short-lived.
      So the snowstorm was definitely a godsend. With a large group of them essentially trapped by the weather, he could study the effects from long distance without fear of releasing his plague too soon to the world at large.
      On the other hand, nature proved daily that if something survives, it deserves to survive.
      Either way, Jean thought he had hoped to give the world another chance to listen, but he knew it would be a fruitless effort. People didn't want to be saved.
      He set out on foot, snowshoes and all, shortly after the morning light began to peek out from behind the trees. Jean trudged six miles on a winding road that seemed to only go uphill.
      He drank all of his water. And then ate the beef jerky, leaving him parched.

      By the time he made it to the front door at the lodge, he was fairly well exhausted, and so he had a seat in the lobby.
The next thing he knew, someone was shaking his shoulder, saying,

"Sir?"


He snapped awake in an instant.
"Sorry, my car got stuck way down the road. Are there any

vacancies?"
      "No, and there isn't any vagrancy either. You can stay in the lobby for a while longer, but I'm afraid the facilities are for guests only."
      "Sure. Of course. Thanks, I appreciate it." Jean replied. When the clerk was safely out of sight, Jean made his way to the men's room, where he cracked the seal of the second vial.
      Sometimes he wondered why the book of Revelation spoke of seven seals, or vials. He was no longer sure which. It didn't matter. He was pure action. His thoughts, he felt, were perfectly programmed to provide a predetermined outcome. Yet, he couldn't see what the outcome would be.
      All he could do was do. He spattered the solution containing the virus around in a few places, careful not to leave anything glaringly obvious. Satisfied, he resealed the vial, stuck it in his pocket, and walked out of the bathroom, a modern day Typhoid Mary.
Reflexively, Jean washed his hands before he left the men's room.
      The virus spread through the lobby along with Jean. By the time he reached the door the damage was done. Effectively, the population of a small town had been exposed.
      He didn't encounter the desk clerk again, thankfully. Jean was on such a manic high following this second release, he trudged his way back to his car in half the time it took him to reach the lodge.
      He felt for the first time that he might be going insane. Not because of his course of actions, but because all the walking through the snow called to mind Jack Nicholson in 'The Shining'.
      He slept again. This time, there were no dreams that he could remember. A snowplow woke him. Like clockwork, he was back on the road.
      Jean pulled over at a rest stop and walked about a quarter mile into the woods. He uncapped the vial and flung it into the undergrowth. The cold may preserve the virus until the spring thaw, but that was the chance he had to take.

      It was designed to survive a scant twelve hours once unleashed, but he had no real assurance that would be the case. So he employed every caution he could.
      Jean had brought latex gloves, and used them at deserted gas pumps, never risking contact with another person. He ate from vending machines at empty rest areas.
      Not long after he entered Nevada, Jean sneezed. He immediately began to panic. The virus was programmed to survive twelve hours on its own. Its life expectancy once it infected someone was twenty-four hours, the same as the host.
      He felt flush and his head was swimmy. He was infected, he was sure of it. And now he was contagious. For all he knew, he was dying as well.
      He stuck it out and made it home, collapsing on the couch in a cold sweat. After nearly ten ours of sleep, and one brief bathroom visit, Jean woke up feeling okay. He wasn't sure if he was contagious or not, so he resolved to stay in his apartment for another twenty-four hours.
      He turned on CNN. Nothing. A scan of the online news sites revealed nothing of interest, either. It was a decidedly odd turn of events. People should definitely be dead by now, he thought.
      He rechecked all of his field notes. Could he have a made a mistake? The fact that he got sick seemed to indicate the serum wasn't inert.
Several hours later, he found the story, a local one. "Five dead in suspected food poisoning case."
      According to the article, guests and staff alike became ill yesterday morning from unknown causes. Most recovered in a day, but five people died.
They suspected bad eggs.
      So it was either a cover-up, or the medical examiner was clueless. He expected that a follow-up article would be run later, if any more was learned about the cause of death.
Jean was just glad the article wasn't syndicated.
      As tests go, it was a rousing success. He could find nothing to indicate the virus had infected anyone outside of the lodge. Most interesting to Jean was the fact that there were two sets of family members that died together.


cases.

Confirmed, he thought. Psychopathy is inherited, at least in some

The last vial of Captain Trips had no shelf life. Instead, it would

keep on killing until the fascist gene was effectively eradicated. Or so he hoped.

The doctor's consulted their superiors and the decision was clear. "Open him up."
      For two more hours they infused Jean with fresh blood as they did exploratory surgery in an attempt to stop his blood loss.
      They opened the wound to three times its initial size. Finally, they found the problem. A bullet had chipped his clavicle and resultant shrapnel clipped an artery.
      All told, Jean was in surgery for five hours. When they had finished with him, he was alive, barely. He was wheeled to the critical care wing and the next day he was still unconscious.
###
      With Jean in surgery, Paige had little to do but wait around and be depressed. She decided to go home and get some clothes.
      She would smoke, too, of course, but even that held little appeal for her. Already, smoking was now something that made her think of Maxxy.
      On the freeway, Paige's mood was most black indeed. With nothing to lose, she opened the vial of Captain Trips and slowly poured it out of the window as she drove.
      The solution atomized and misted in the breeze. It coated cars, and was drawn into the air intakes of air conditioners. It got on birds and insects, and at least one dog, she suspected.
      Thy will be done, she thought. She turned on the radio, and the classic rock station seemed to vindicated her actions.
      "Well, I ain't no, new messiah, but I'm good enough for rock and roll." .38 Special? Kansas? She was no longer sure, but it was comforting when the universe let you know you were on the right track.
      At home, she threw the empty vial into a dumpster, but she didn't bother to shower. Thus Paige was still infectious when she went back to the hospital later that night.
      Having been rejected by Maxine, Paige slept in Jean's room. The next morning she drank coffee and turned on the TV, as was her morning habit.

      "Reports are coming in from California about a new virus raging up and down the coast, from San Diego to Humboldt County, and as far inland as East L.A. Three deaths are confirmed, and as many as one hundred thousand are ill with this new contagion."
Paige turned off the TV, numb with shock. Had she really done that?
She knew whatever was coming, it was only the beginning.
She moved over to the chair she had set up next to Jean and held his
hand.
"Guess what, Jean?" she said. "We did it." She kissed him on the
cheek, but he didn't stir.
      The nurses encouraged her to touch him and talk to him on the basis that it was likely to bring him out of his coma-like state sooner.
      It was a day she spent in glorious solitude, wallowing in melancholy sentimentality. She curled up with a blanket and sometimes flipped channels, but mostly she stared out of the window, or watched Jean sleep.
      The virus ravaged the west coast, and took off for all points beyond. Paige watched with interest as it began to travel the globe. And the death toll was mounting. More than three hundred dead in California alone, and that was in less than twenty-four hours.
      By ten p.m. that night, Californians recognized it for what it was: Captain Trips. The name began to appear on the local broadcasts, and first made the national news on the morning of Saturday the fourteenth.
      And still Jean slept. Dreaming, Paige hoped, in his comatose state. The nurses and occasional doctor all agreed that there was nothing they could do. It was just a matter of time, but he would wake up again, they assured her.
      By Sunday it was apparent that there would be no great body count. Still, fourteen thousand dead internationally was nothing to sneeze at. And Captain Trips raged on.
      But there was something more going on beneath the surface. Some people who became ill from the virus were transformed by it, in a manner similar to religious conversion.
      Some attributed it to God, but others correctly claimed that the virus itself had made them better people. CEOs, statesman and others began to testify on television that they could no longer steal, or kill, or whatever their previous negative behaviors had been.

      It was as if a large portion of the world was born again, or had converted to Buddhism. Paige knew what had happened. The virus had cured them of their psychopathy.
###
      Maxine was up and around, so she made her way to see Jean. She walked in with her son, and upon seeing Paige, emitted a soft "Oh" in surprise.
      Her eyes were already red and wet. When Paige turned from the window to meet her gaze, tears once more began to fall. Quickly, Paige got up to hold the baby, whom Maxine handed over without question so she could embrace Jean, who remained in shock.
Maxxy lay across Jean's chest for more than ten minutes before she

spoke.


"I talked to Pookie," she said. "I'm sorry."
"He's beautiful," Paige said. "He looks like his daddy. What's his

name?"
      Maxine got up and walked to the foot of the bed to address Paige face to face.
"Whitfield Drama Simons," she said. "Is Whitfield a family name?"
      "No, Whitfield Diffie was a famous hacker Jean and I both admire. And when you consider how he was born, Drama is definitely his middle name."
Both of them smiled at this. "What are you going to do?" "Wait for Jean to wake up." "No, after that."
"Well, it's funny. I've been thinking about a ranch, of all things." "Where?"
"Colorado," Maxine said, and they both giggled.
###
      By the time Captain Trips had completed its circumnavigation, nearly twenty thousand were dead, with Russia and Europe being hit particularly hard. There was no way to gauge how many people's lives were altered by being infected, but it was as though a worldwide revolution was in progress.
###

"I love you, Maxine Jackson," Paige said. "I love you, Paige Burner," Maxine replied.
Paige leaned over the baby and kissed Maxxy's soft, full lips. Just then, Jean's eyes opened and he awakened with a start.
      The smiling faces of Maxine and Paige both turned to him, looking not unlike angels. Without missing a beat, Paige said to Jean, "Hey, you. Welcome to paradise..."
      
The End
